Fiction
The thousand guineas offered by Hodder
and Stoughton in their recent novel com-
petition could not have been awarded to
anyone else than Jessie Douglas Kerruigh:
her Miss Haroun Al-Raschid (per Dym-
ock’s Library) is aptly named, for these
amazing adventurers in Kurdistan and
Mesopotamia have some of the quality
of The Arabian Nights. The heroine, the
daughter of an English Assyriologist and of
an hereditary pasha in Kurdistan, in other
words a Eurasian, had, it is hinted, that
celebrated Sultan of Bagdad for an ancestor.
Her mother was an Abbasside. On the
distaff side she was of sacrosanct lineage;
and Haroun Al-Ilaschid or Haroun ’r
Bashed, as she tells it should be, was also
an Abbasside,
Kurdistan, a-seethe with mixed religions
and assorted races, is the place where any-
thing may happen; and in this novel it
happens in most of the chapters. “The East
is not wholly populated by obsequious ser-
vants, obliging sultans, well-bred harem
ladies and agreeable aides.” The author
knows her. terrain so thoroughly that at
times she is almost unintelligible; she for-
the average reader is not familiar
with the ceremonial of Eastern intercourse,
and would not be able to tell a Chaldcean
from a Kurd. Thus when we learn that the
heroine was in Kurdistan during the Autumn
fusing, when “the sedentary tribes indulge
in little outbursts of animal spirits: tribal
hghts and some slaying of tax-gatherers and
native Christians” and that “half the Arton-
sha were up, the unspeakable Herki were
worse than usual, the Dikrans were restive
and the Gadarwand showed signs of breaking
out, ' we are duly impressed, but are we
enlightened ?
The heroine’s adventures include desperate
enterprises,' disguises and a charming adven-
ture in a Christian church, where she had to
take the place of a corpse in a coffin in order
to avert suspicion from the village. The
kindly Turk had removed the corpse and
filled that coffin full of rifles; but the
heroine foiled his plot of “discovering” those
rifles in the village and thus giving the Bey
an excuse to exterminate the Christians and
secure his preferment as a vigilant official.
Even excavating the ruins of buried cities,
under the heroine’s directions, proves wildly
exciting; and the author knows as much
about Assyriology as she does about the
tribal habits of the Georgians and the psy-
chology of the Turk. There are tricks even
m. the trade of Assyriology. “A Museum
iriece is a trade term for arms, statues
votive images, etc., as distinguished from in-
scriptions. Inscribed tablets are what the
Assyriologist is seriously concerned in find-
from them scholars are now rebuilding
the history, life, literature and magic of the
f.f*? 1 .worhl; It is advisable to keep an eye
lilting for Museum Pieces, however; unless
you can read them, inscribed tablets are not
inordinately exciting—to the exoteric eye
they suggest badly-shaped buns and short-
bread , but anyone can feel the romance of
arms and sculptures three, four or eight
thousand years old. If you get together a
ueat lilt!*- Collection lor exhibition in
London, the British public comes to gape,
the newspapers print, nice articles about, you,
people realise you are doing something tangi-
ble, and—subscribe ip your fund. This is
the sordid side ot Asisyriology, divine science
of grave-ransacking imperially.'’ And the
excitement of it! The race between two rival
parties to be the first to stake out a claim
on a virgin Tel —an Unexplored mound in
the desert I
The author cleverly provides an unex-
pected hero; and tin* plot, dovetails neatly
into an unexpected climax. The vivacity
and assurance of the style, the character
drawing of a host of diilercnt racial types,
and the exotic charm of Asiatic Turkey com-
bine to give this novel a un’uue distinction.
It is the .l,oU2nd .Irit hi nit Nights' Enter-
tainment. ' *
d*
There is the same intimate knowledge of
the Chinese in Putnam Weale’s The Altar
Fire (Methuen, per Dyinock's Library) as
is shown by Jessie KerrCtish of the inhabi-
tants of Asiatic Turkey; and the picture of
the Chinese Revolution in 1911, by which
the Republic was established, suffers from
its excessive detail. There is left in the
mind of the reader a confused impression of
vague motives, inexplicable actious, inco-
herent utterances and mysterious reactions,
out of which hell’s broth the Republic was
brewed. His characters include Chinese
revolutionary leaders, Manchu princes, cos-
mopolitan financiers, diplomats, assassins,
society wpmen, an American girl married to
a Chinese, and a Chinese girl married to a
British Indian officer.
We see Peking in a thousand aspects; and
we see the revolution revolving not about
any high principles of Republicanism but
about money. Apparently every political
movement in modern China depends on
bribery, and the problem that confronts the
rival leaders is chiefly whether each can bribe
the .army of the other. Thus when Nanking
was unexpectedly without any obvious reason
evacuated by the Imperialist forces, the
revolutionary leader was naturally worried.
He explains to his follower: “Why, do you
know we bought all the gunners in the forts
defending the city a month ago. That’s why
our camps were never bombarded, that’s why
we were able to invest the city completely.
Now I will, tell you why I suspect that some-
thing more dangerous to our cause than de-
feat in the field is beginning to be used
against us. We never paid the city to sur-
render ; we hadn’t money enough for that!
We never paid the Imperialists to evacuate
a single position; the agreement was only
about the artillery fire, which we knew our
new recruits couldn’t stand. Now, if our
side didn’t pay the Imperialists to evacuate,
somebody else must have.” Obviously the
Chinese Field-Marshal has problems solve
that never troubled Hindenburg or Jo fire,
lake the recent Imperialist coup d’etat, of
the motives and meaning of which this his-
torical novel aptly affords a brilliant illumi-
nation, the struggle of 1911 was conducted by
both sides on the Chinese assumption that
the conflict was j lrely political and financial,
and could not he in any way influenced by
military considerations. Apparently the
Chinese win or 1 se their revolutions accord-
ing to the amoufij of readv cash that each
side can borrow, exact or be bribed with.
Ihe intrigues within intrigues that Weale
describes are extraordinary and almost in-
explicable.
The foreign community in Peking plays this
financial game witii the same feverish zest
as the cunning Chinese leaders, though the
author is careful throughout to represent the
foreign official diplomats as standing aloof
from this mad orgy of loans and bribes. The
frivolous European residents are swept
to and fro by their emotions, love affairs and
society enmities; and the author leaves us
with a lasting picture of these helpless
Europeans adrift on a sea of unplumbed
depths of Chinese rascality, cunning, ambi-
*lofi an A contempt for the foreigner.
Tiie story has a dozen different plots, which
the author rightly does not allow to be anv-
thing but incidents in his main plot. That
is China in revolution.

According to Butters, Australia Officially
Presents the In Memoriam Wreath of Pari
Kitchener (also, according to him, published
by authority). Space precludes our publish-
ing the whole of this characteristic tribute
from one great man to another: the world
must content itself with Kitchener’s Monu-
ment : *
It stands for Everlast: eternal stands ;
That army raised by him, aye, him alone;
On base basaltic: firm defying- time;
Great Monument, beheld: replying well
To worlds unborn, who grateful shall
Upwrite with pride, We of His Nation, Are.

In Will Wynning's opinion “Dunedin’s
girls are great.’’ Also
Dunedin’s girls are pretty girls—
No powder theirs, nor dye;
And khaki boys give each and all
A clinging kiss —“Good-bye” !
Princes Street and Other Otago Rimes
(Stark, Dunedin, M.L., 6d.) is full of girls.
Even the sunlit flappers pacing by (the
blossoms of an ancient stem) cause tottering
men to wonder vvhy time passed so speedily
for them ; and when the snow is on the town-
ship ,and the bush is all a-frown, he sits
alone and thinks alone of the girls who
turned him down. Winning Willie writes
simply but sincerely, and one effort, “The
Answer,” is a fine bit of stately writing, as
will be seen by the first two stanzas: —
Pot for the glamor and the trumpet call,
Nor all the magic of a splendid pride,
Do tee, the sons of Britain, one and all
Go forth to battle in the field and tide!’
Nor yet for dreams of all that might have
been,
Nor yet to mock a hundred trite decrees,
Nor yet to widen still a wide demesne —
No, not for things ephemeral like these!
But there is something that we still can
claim,
A stirring of the heart, a nameless heat
That finds ns fearless of all ends, save shame,
And heedless of all ills save one — defeat!
The call is not to war for war’s vile gain,
And yet the clarion makes weird miseries;
And though our toll be shipwrecks, wounded,
slain.
We fight for peace and honor, yea, for
these!

The Story of the Ansacs (J. Ingram,
Melb.), by some writer who is ashamed to
sign his name—the theory that he is too
modest is untenable since presumably he is
an Australian writer—deals without distinc-
tion with the part taken by the Anzacs on
the outbreak of war till the evacuation of
Gallipoli. There is apparently little that is
n’ew in this history; but the ground is well
covered. Yet the Anzac story that Australia
is waiting for, and probably will have to
wait long for, must be written in more living
language, and by the bloke who has beer
there There is no evidence of this first-hand
knowledge in this conventional publication.
Lhe photographic illustrations are poorly
selected, though luckily there is includes
that historic picture, “The First Roll Call
of the 3rd Brigade after the Great Landing ”
which should ultimately form the basis of ’a
great historical painting by a great Austra-
lian artist. The selection of maps is equallv
insufficient; there should have been a large-
scale folding map of the Peninsula—an ad-
mil able one has been on sale for manv
months, published by 11. E. C. Robinson,
Sydney; and this page has seen some re-
markable photographs, taken from aero-
planes, that give a much clearer impression
of Anzac than the small scale renroduction
from a relief model in this volume. The
general map of the Anzac Area (p. 68)
should have been put into the hands of a
cartographer.

The Cinquain
Here is a dainty little verse form. built
by an American poet on a Japanese iaad,.|
First for an example ,V -
XOVKMBEK XIGIIT.
listen,
Uit It joint. dri) sou ml, " ..I*-
Like steps of /msslag ghosts.
The leaves, frost-crisped, break from the trim*
And fall. O
The Japanese parentage is obvious, yet the
cinqoain is not so difficult, since in the
Japanese forms there is still further
compression. Examples of the cinquain are
difficult to find, and the few this page has
seen are so poor in quality that the one
quoted is the best. It is with -the confident
hope that Australian verse-writers will he
able to transmute this trivial form into some-
thing infinitely liner that this page com-
mends the cinquain to its contributors. *
The structure of the cinquain consists of
live lines of iambic feet, though inversion
may take place occasionally, as in the first
line of the quoted example, where its value
will be felt. The first line contains one foot
the second line has two feet, the third line
three, the fourth four, and the fifth line one
Each foot, of course, contains two syllables-
and there is no need of rime. It has been
said of the cinquain that it has the effect
of a ballet-dancer poised on the single toe
pleasing in a way but too exotic to be of
real value. The cinquainist will doubtless
discover that the thought must be led grace-
fully and gradually up to the end of the
fourth line, where fancifully it may be said
the sentiment remains an instant poised on
a dainty toe, thehce sharply and neatly it
regains solid ground. The final figure of the
pirouette may be either .comedy, pathos or
any variety of the grotesque or unexpected.
To give the ballet-dancer a rest it may he
said that the sting of the cinquain is in its
tail. It should be O. Henry in five little
lines.
Half a guinea will be paid for the best,
cinquain received by this page by August &1.
The Patriotic Publishing Company, Cham-
ber of Commerce Buildings, George-street,
Sydney, is a bright young firm. Its glitter-
ing idea is to publish The Commonwealth
Gallery and Register of Australian Herees
of.course, by subscription. In this volume
will be printed the names, biographies, war
records, photos and particulars of every
soldier who has enlisted. In the form sent
out to sign the editor furnishes a poor recom-
mendation for himself by the bad grammar
he uses. Early in the prospectus it is inti-
mated fbat there will probably be half a
million names available for inclusion, for
provision is also made for “responsible ad-
ministrators, ’ etc. And no charge whatever
wul he made for publication of photographs
and synoptical biographies. However, if you
want the book you must immediately sub-
scr.J be an d sign &n order form ; for no copies
will be printed in excess of the number actu-
ally ordered in each such order form, AND
a preliminary payment of £2 11s. 6d.~
whteh is half the price of the publication—
must be made with the order. Thus this bright
firm will be able to pay its way from the
start; and it points out that its labors will
be expensive and arduous, BUT as the
matter vvill have to be continually amplified
and revised, “it obviously follows that the
publishers can only bind themselves to com-
mence delivery of the completed work within
a reasonable time after the date upon which
peace shall have been declared.” The enthu-
siastic post-dated publishers commit them-
selves to the assertion thaf “innumerable
copies have already been ordered.” Such a
record would have an indubitable historic
value; but it is a task that lies within the
province of the Commonwealth Statistical
Department. No private publisher, however
bright, would have the confidence of Aus-
tralians. It is to be hoped that full records
ot every unit in our army are being filed by
the Government for future use. This can be
done without the previous payment of £2

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Vigil.
Late fires of dusk are swiftly dying down,
The stars are coming soon ;
There rides above the housetops and the
town
A sickle-shred of moon.
Our sister Moon, a sly seductive witch,
Gloats on the world agape—
Morals and moods of mortals, poor and rich,
Changeable as her shape.
Few pungent stories that she doesn’t know,
This cynic dame so slim:
The world she watches is a raree-show
To please her sidling whim.
Yon widow, garbed regardless of expense,
Stalks her economies ;
While down that lane, against a backyard
fence,
Two lovers stand and kiss.
One Raliab (trade and training plainly seen)
Bewails a time of dearth.
Soon will she be (for competition’s keen)
Competed off the earth.
A pallid curate sniffs and slips away.
How sad that such as she
Should smirch a world all swept and gar-
nished—nay,
All sweet with cakes and tea!
The moon, our peering sister, sees it all,
And snickers as she sees.
She sees us sin. She sees us duck and crawl
To dodge the penalties.
She knows what is, and what is yet to be,
And what of old has been;
She cares no whit for midget you and me,
For all our toil and teen.
Hanging above old Babylon she saw
The lemans and the guests
Of that Great King who held the world in
awe,
Who slept between the breasts
Of ladies lovelier than those that gleam
A-down the woodland ways
Of Dream, bedecked (as fitting ’tis in dream)
With pearls and chrysoprase.
The soldier-boy who yearned against the
fence
And kissed the girl has now
Been, by some other quest or vow, called
hence.
What matter, anyhow?
The widow, halted by the gutter’s edge,
Discloses some meek plan
To one to whom, maybe, her heart’s in
pledge—
I think an alderman.
Two souls well-soaked are fain to cross the
street,
But wave and vacillate
In the coy breeze of evening cool and sweet.
One croons of Bess; one, Kate.
Your souls in wine are ever souls in love,
A-hungered for a boon.
No toper he who snared that dame above,
The simpering, wanton Moon.
She found him in a glade. He was asleep.
She waked and kissed him too ;
But still some reputation seems to keep,
As other humbugs do.
Tbe fires of dusk have died completely down.
The virtues are a-5w00n....
See! winking o’er the scandals of the town,
That naughty jade, the Moon!
N.S.W.
FRANK MORTON.
Adventure.
The world is charted out from Pole to Pole,
Measured and docketed and filed away;
And old Adventure, portly grown and grey,
Sits in his office. But his fiery soul
Yearns for the magic seas that used to roll,
The dragon-terrors that were once to slay,
The perilous journeys past the rim of day
In joyous quest of some forbidden goal.
Yet there remains to him one land untrod,
One venture beckoning still, one keen sur-
mise
To fan the wanderlust and fire his eyes,
To spur his pulses and to rouse his breath,
One vision still to stir his rover-blood :
The panorama from the peaks of Death.
MAORILAND.
BARTLETT ADAMSON.
Helen.
Upon a bank of violets and thyme
Stirred by the Summer moon’s slim finger-
tips,
Helen and Paris couched, lips close on lips,
In ecstasy that pealed a silver chime
Throughout the night, while young buds burst
to flow’r,
And water-lilies shimmered golden flame
Upon a lake where scarce a ripple came,
And blue moths fluttered through the love-lit
hour....
What time the dawning flashed with rosy joy
His first shaft through the east, and rent
the dreams
That nightly drew her heart with lilac cord
Back to the sunshine and low hills of Troy,
Helen shook loose her hair and through
its gleams
Smiled over Menelaus, her stre-- i™*
N.S.W.
NORA McAULIFFE.

TERMS TO SUBSCRIBERS.
(In Advance, Only.)
For £1 6s. 6d. per annum you can have The
Bulletin sent regularly to you every week for a
year, including Special Christmas Number, to any
ADDRESS IN AUSTRALASIA, post paid.
Post free to any address abroad, £1 10s. per
i mu urn.
Cheques, drafts, etc., which should be crossed, and
all Business Communications to be addressed to the
Manager.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be
refused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby expressly
reserved by the publishers.
The Editor u ill carefully read, and, where practic-
able. acknowledge in the “Correspondence” column all
contributions submitted, whether in the form of Politi-
cal. Social or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales or
Sketches (those dealing with Australian subjects, and
not exceeding two columns in length, or, say, 3000
words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs, Letters
or Newspaper Clippings. All communications will be
regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. The Bulletin desires, further, those
sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of MS.
To Amateurs in Black and AA'hite.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash,
of sufficient merit and interest and adapted for
direct reproduction, or any interesting photograph
illustrative of a current event, will be paid for same
on acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea
but unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any picture
which has to be re-drawn —will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

after the arrival of each mail, from Messrs. Gordon
and Gotch, Ludgate Circus.

THURSDAY, JULY 26, 1917.
“SINN FEIN.”
The Sinn Fein movement in Ireland, which
last year made martyrs and now makes mem-
bers of Parliament, has a force and signifi-
cance which, far from being confined to one
northern island, is world-wide in its sweep.
Though the words are Irish their meaning
has gained a firm grip on all the peoples.
“Ourselves alone” is practically the watch-
word of every nation of men worthy of the
name; and the “rights of small nations” for
which the tremendous Allied forces bleed and
battle is the practical acknowledgment of
the principle behind that watchword on the
part of the peoples that have been touched
by natural democratic evolution. Sinn Fein
is the hope and desire of Belgium, of Servia,
of Montenegro, of Bohemia, of Poland, and
probably of the renovated and reawakened
Greece that now shouts gladly for \rENiZE-
eos where it lately yelled under compulsion
for Constantine. The expected breaking
of China will be the response of Orientalism
to the Sinn Fein feeling moving through the
vast mass of smiling, long-suffering Asiatic
humanity. In fact, every big movement of
the time that seems to possess anything in
the way of decent purpose is at heart a Sinn
Fein movement. For Sinn Fein the war is
being fought; for Sinn Fein peace will be
made; and if it is a good Sinn Fein that will
stick and remember its own purpose, the
world is going to get a great lift forward
because of it. The evolution of such an idea
was inevitable. The first associations of
men were for mutual protection. Any sort
of union or amalgamation of tribes and races
with diverse feelings and interests was to be
tolerated as long as it meant safety. The
dominant interest of keeping alive out-
weighed everything else; and the worst
ordinary despotism was a mere circumstance
compared with a split throat and a burnt
homestead. But as promiscuous throat-cut-
ting decayed, the artificial amalgamations of
men were no longer necessary, though, as
they kept kings and statesmen in jobs, new
excuses had to be found for them on com-
mercial and economic grounds. Empires of
trade were advocated instead of empires of
force; but no heed was given to the different
make and shape of the racial soul. The idea
that all men were vats escaping from a trap
of some sort lay behind the notions govern-
ing international politics, varied occasionally
by the idea of all men as rats trying to get
through the largest hole into the world’s
wheat store. Never did it seem to occur to
anybody in power that a German could not
think as a Slav thinks or an Englishman get
the viewpoint of a Celt; and that, conse-
quently, a German could not govern a Slav
to the real advantage of both, or an English-
man guide the destinies of a Celtic people by
the rules that were good in Sussex or East
Anglia. Now the universal uproar of a
great war has blown away a thousand
obscuring non-essentials of human govern-
ment, and as the dust begins to settle it
becomes clearer that nationality is about the
one thing that really does count. The unity
of mankind which religions have taught and
poets dreamed of is to be reached by no
artificial methods or shortcuts of statecraft,
but by the recognition of the claims of the
racial units in the human family, and by
giving them free play to develop their in-
herent decency. The State can only advance
by the development of its individual citi-
zens; the world can only advance by the
development of its individual race families.
All the artificial things that hinder this are
inimical to progress. A traction-engine, a
bullock, an Arab horse and an Eskimo dog
would make a poor team to draw a coach,
however well constructed the traces and
harness might be. But each can do some
strenuous pulling of its own ; and the road is
broad enough for them all to run abreast,
each with a separate vehicle in tow.
On all evidences, the next stage in world-
development will involve a splitting up and
dividing in order that the larger unity of
mankind may rest on a sure foundation. The
rights of small peoples necessarily make this
imperative. Belgium, Servia and Monte-
negro have already tasted the freedom which
is to be restored to them by force of arms ;
but the rights of Servia are no more im-
portant than the rights of Ireland, nor can
those of Belgium take precedence of Bohe-
mian claims. Hitherto the small Xtate has
been regarded as an international danger
because its existence was a perpetual temp-
tation to the big milita y power next door :
but with the destruction of militarism this
danger ceases, and small Xtate and big come
together under one universal internnti mil
police control. The small man was probably
once a menace to the tribe owing to the
temptation to the big man to kill him and
collar his possessions, and thus disturb the
balance of power: but mankind advanced
beyond the idea of stealing things by force
merely because they were easy to steal. Trade
rivalries there must still be, and the greater
productivity of one tract of country may
cause it to flourish while another tract re-
mains comparatively poor; but it has yet to
be shown that any inhabited portion of the
globe under the settled and responsible gov-
ernment of its own people, who best under-
stand it, is too poor to create wealth beyond
anything that has yet been produced in the
earth’s favored regions. This is the real
economic aspect of world settlement; and
wdiile it remains and intensive culture and
close-linked industry are possible, the idea of
huge artificial combines for economic reasons
may well be put out of court. The machine
must always be a dead thing until it is ani-
mated by a real spirit of popular enthusi-
asm, and the cry for Xinn Fein is really a
cry for dynamic force to get the beM
national results out of the great busings- • f
living. It is a cry for decentralisation in it<
best form —a pleading of neglected and over-
governed peoples to be allowed to get to
work.
Australia has not yet felt the impulse of
the Xinn Fein movement, and in a sense
cannot feel it racially except in as far as her
people are intolerant of interference fro n
overseas; but a force sufficiently like it to
easily wear the same name is bound to
gather head if we are ever to get out of our
present confusion and step into a larger
place. Our Xtates—mere artificial divisions
of the continent —are coming to be regarded
as the absurdities they are; but our central
Federal Government is almost equally
scorned. The fact is that in emphasising
the solidarity of the whole Commonwealth
we have strangely forgotten the district
units that make up that Commonwealth.
Those units are not the States, but natural
divisions with natural community of in-
terests, some of immense area, some very
small, but all in some sense self-contained.
As a people in the course of years develop
with their environment, there will in the
ages to come be racial distinctions also
between the dwellers in one part of the con-
tinent and those in another —distinctions
that are both natural and on the whole
advantageous to Australia. Just as Ireland
turned the Norman Raymonds and De
Burghs into thoroughly Irish Redmonds
and Burkes, so Northern Queensland will
take any kind of white man’s family and
make it clearly and unmistakably her own.
Tasmania will do the same; so will the
broad lands west of the Darling and the
Northern Rivers of N. S. Wales. There will
be many varieties of the Australian race,
though all may continue to preserve some-
thing in common. Not anticipating this,
however, but merely with a view to the con-
tinent’s best development, a large measure
of Sinn Fein is needed. Let us have the
central Federal Government by all means—a
broadly representative Parliament and Execu-
tive with wide functions and very little power
of local interference; but let the division of
the country be on the sane plan of com-
munity of interests without respect to terri-
torial area. In a word, let us have com-
plete decentralisation—the only decentralisa-
tion that can mean any thing*—by establish-
ing many self-governing provinces, each with
a sufficient measure of Sinn Fein to enable
it to get at the job that needs doing without
waste of time, or energy. The idea that
the centralisation of the continent might be
a good thing has been wholly exploded by
the conduct of Federal affairs since the Com-
monwealth came into being; while the notion
that the artificial State boundaries make for
uiy sort of efficiency of government has
been thoroughly dead for some time. The
talk of elaborating the machinery of
an ! d more while that machine
centralisation loses most of its motive
power, is equally futile. Enthusiasts point
to Switzerland as an example of the triumph
of the machine of popular government; but
in doing so they leave out the chief factor
in the Swiss success. The democratic co -
trivances in that country are good enoug ,
but the national success is rooted an
grounded on the cantonal system, by whicn
each natural division of the .country enjoys the
largest possible measure of local legislative
freedom. Thus, in Uri—the father State of
free Switzerland, where William Tell fired
off the arrow- —the people coihe out and make
their own laws exactly as they did hundreds
of years ago. And Uri w r ould be lost sight
of territorially in an Australian sheep-run.
It is Swiss Sinn Fein rather than the
shape of the central ballot-box that accounts
for the triumph of democracy there; or. to
put it another way, it is the local freedom
of legislation that makes the fine general
democratic spirit possible. Uri does as Uri
wants to do; and. therefore,. Uri gives the
staunchest and most intelligent vote to help
along the" United Switzerland That permits
it to do so.-. But centralise Switzerland as
Australia is centralised in a big town or two.
and all the referenda and preferential voting
that could be devised would not save
the democracy there from a mess like our
own. In the w 7 orld-movement towards Sinn
Fein Australia cannot stand out. By "our-
selves alone” can we be saved : and. getting
down to bedrock fact, “ourselves alone” can
only operate effectively in the comparatively
small sphere where there is complete com-
munity of interest. The ideal of an undi-
vided continent will only be realised w 7 hen
each part of the continent is working its own
share of the thing to the full advantage
which only a fair measure of Sinn Fein can
give.
THE WAY OF THE FISHERMEN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The grey seas were ruffled into rippling
columns, which eddied and broke, swelled
and broke again. Under the sombre mono-
chrome of sky—rr"y like the sea—a million
gleaming forms threshed through the troubled
waters, a million fins ploughed a passage
across the shallows of the Barrier. The deep,
sonorous blast as the fish, coming to the sur-
face, gulped in the necessary air, proclaimed
the sea-tailer. Groping through the torrid
seas they were seeking an outlet of the
Barrier to harry lesser fins. The sea-tailer
is a scavenger and fighter combined. Hang-
ing on to the flanks of a bluenose shark he
awaits the kill and the subsequent banquet.
But when the Arctic flow entices the killer
further north, the gaunt tarpon collect in
schools, and, raiding the smaller fry, eke out
a scanty subsistence until the return of their
benefactors.
The trail of foam eddying in the wake of
the school startled the wheeling gulls. It
also attracted the attention of the look-out
on the fishing smack Rosetta.
In the crow’s-nest, Scotty, glass focussed
on a patch of w 7 ater glinting a league away,
roused himself and called to the deck-hands
below. 1 hey tumbled up, growLng and curs-
ing at being disturbed from their midday
sleep, but eager withal. For days they hail
been waiting, close-hauled in the shoals of
sandspit and islet, for a tin to show; and
now, when the look-out reported that a shoal
of fish were approaching, the bunks were
precipitately abandoned and all hands
burned aft —a motley ship’s complement
gathered from the seven seas. A swarthy
Greek from lonian ports rubbed shoulders
with a fair-inured Saxon- —he was out of the
Bristol fisheries. Deep-sea toilers these, for
a fisherman who flouts storm and tempest to
gain a catch may well be associated with the
bummer-jacks.
A squat form reeled from the shelter of the
cuddy, crossed tween decks and brought up
panting at the railings. There was a note
of authority in the newcomer's voice as he
hailed the look-out.
‘What do y’ make of them, you aloft?
Ferguson, you’ve a good pair of eyes—-
mount the ratlines and have a peep.”
But Ferguson’s assistance was not
needed. Scotty, after a long scrutiny
through the glass, told his own tale.
school of sea-tailer, Cap’n,” he called.
“They’re coming this way, and should pass
light across our bows. It’s a big mob
collecting for a raid, I think.”
A cheer was raised, for sea-tailer is a
marketable fish and easily captured. Assum-
ing command the skipper took the wheel,
whistled for a wind that failed to come, and
concentrated his attention on the impending
struggle. &
The approaching horde were visible from
bulwarks now, a seething wall of water
lifting up in their wake. Under the propul-
sion of the heavy oars the fishing-smack
steered out to intercept the prey. Usually
m deep-sea fishing the nets are lowered in the
form of a circle to enclose the spoil. The
skipper, judging the distance between him-
self and the oncoming fish, waited calmlv for
the moment to strike.
No one spoke; tense silence had succeeded
the busy hum of speculation. Only Fer-
guson the mate, at a sign from the skipper,
issued a brief command.
“ Cl . ear tbe stern-falls!” he ordered.
Stroke oars stand by.”
Two of the rowers shipped blades, and,
raising the buoy attached to the nets, pre-
pared to cast it at a further command.
«mnHifa^ ong . Pr ° CeSsion of gaming silver,
scintillating to green and gold beneath the
sun ‘ a th^/ Cho °} thre shed in. Eddies suc-
ceeded eddies; leaping, playful forms leaped
anew; the whole expanse of ocean was
broken into ripples.
Signalling to the crew\ the skipper threw
up the wheel and as the smack fell awav the
nets were lowered in the heavy rollers to
tail out a bobbing line astern. The arduous
struggle had begun. With muscles outstand-
ing and a fierce, exulting heart in each
breast, the spoilers bent to the oar, for now 7
the fish were dividing, and it would be a
question of speed if they were to be all
en f lathTI ath T after fathom of line ran
s ? ft ’ . su rgmg of greased rollers
to link up the barrier astern
The wooden trestles rattled and hissed
hung for a moment, and a tangle of rope
drifted out. The hitch, though slight, neces-
sitated a stoppage for the runner-out to clear
the falls. Ferguson fumed at the delay.
Already the netted fish were endeavoring to
escape; long, gleaming forms disappeared in
a smother of spray ahead and around the
craft. Mis language as he bullied and threat-
ened was interlarded with nautical oaths
and curses. But the falls had been cleared,
and the delinquent runner-out, standing by.
paid out with an assiduity which betokened
his respect for Ferguson. Only a cable’s
length to run and the shoot so zealously
planned vcould be ended.
On the half-deck the skipper, eyes intent
on the nets droning musically over the stern-
sheets, watched and waited while the voice
of the runner-out checking the length
of the diminishing nets came to his ears.
This is a necessary precaution, for a
steersman may “overshoot,” i.e., his length
of net may run out before the circle is
established. And that would mean the loss
of the haul.
“Rink one —link two.” ~ ~
There were 10 lengths or links in the net,
and the skipper knew that two lengths had
gone through the falls. As yet there was no
imminent danger of an overshoot.
“Two —three —four.”
The heavy oars, deftly feathered, were
dragging a little now. In the maelstrom of
waters created by the fleeing school, the fish-
ing smack pitched heavily, tossed, rolled. A
cascade of waters, lifted up by the dipping
forefoot, washed along the decks, threaten-
ing to extinguish the galley fires.
Excitement was hot during the final move.
As the Rosetta swung round in answer to
the helm the last links of the net crossed the
riding-buoy, effectually enclosing the haul.
The shoot was ended.
Only in time. too. Ferguson, going aft
on the completion of his task, saw the line
of mesh lift with the impact of the impris-
oned fish vainly endeavoring to escape.
Panic-stricken they rushed hither and
thither to discover an opening into the sea.
But the net was always there. One or two
leaped high, like shooting pencils of silver,
and. evading the snare, floated out into the
freedom of charging seas and breakers. One
or two —that was all. With the bobbing line
of net slowly hemming them in. the confu-
sion of the victims increased ; the water was
flecked with flying sheets of spume, and the
constant splash and flapping as the mesh
enclosed another victim mingled with the
growl of the shoreward rollers.
Evening found the crew of the Rosetta
busy on the lines. Across the black water
torches flickered and flared, and the eerie
shadows of night crept up. Hands groped
in the feeble torchlight, groped for the nets
and the spoil therein. Fathom after
fathom of mesh trailed in over the rollers,
spilling its splashing freight into the holds.
Arms blistered with the rough friction of
the ropes, but. seemingly unconscious of the
pain, the fishers toiled.
Skipper and mate, both clad in oilies and
heavily booted, worked with the crew 7 , for on
a fishing-smack rank is disregarded when a
shoot is in progress. To the mournful
accompaniment of the creaking rollers and
the swish-swish of the rising tides the net
and its heavy burden w T as dravm in.
It was the runner-out who first observed
the riding-buoy at the end of the surging
lines. His joyful shout drew sighs of relief
from all hands. The buoy was passed in.
the end lines—glad spectacle to a fisherman
—following. The most interested in the
catch crowded round the hold to inspect the
gasping silver horde within.
An experienced man can tell at a glance
the extent of a catch. “Five ton if there’s a
pound,” was a bearded toiler’s comment, and
a subsequent tally showed that he was
right.
Skipper and mate, exuberant, retired to
the cuddy to discuss a tot of rum and the
market quotations for sea-tailer. As for the
crew, the nets housed and mended, they clus-
tered in the shelter of the poop to sleep till
dawn.
* * * *
Out of the yellow loom of fog the sun
crept, up and found them wearily patrolling
the deep-sea grounds in quest of further
spoil.

PLAIN ENGLISH.
An Invitation to Damn the Cost, Anyway.
Fusion, talking through the mouth of Mil-
len, has produced its scheme of Repatria-
tion ; and as The Bulletin expected, the
thing commences long after the start and
closes up suddenly before it reaches the end.
a. he scheme contemplates the creation of
comprehensive. _ administrative machinery
(with the politician well in evidence), the
appointment of consultative central Boards
and local committees, the establishment of
workshops, sanatoria and hostels, co-oper-
ation with . manufacturers and others to
secure the industrial training of men and
the completion of interrupted apprentice-
ship. It is further in contemplation to settle
soldiers on land, provide them with ad-
vances, guarantee them minimum prices for
their produce and market it, arrange estab-
lishments for slaughtering hogs and inaugu-
rate systems of reserve employment. All
this, of course, looks well: but there is a
practical side to it, and Tiie Bulletin is
compelled to say that until this is produced
fi scl ’£ me can be regarded as nothing better
than the cumbersome invention of carrot-
wa.ying politicians who are afraid to face
their difficulties. The crux of the business is
the rash. How is the scheme going to be
financed . Millen, when questioned on the
subject, cheerfully announced: ‘‘l am not
concerned with finance at this juncture.” As
his estnnate of the sum required for land
settlement is a mere £60.000.000, and as
there are. clearly huge additional amounts
lnvohod in other directions, this is cer-
tainly a novel proclamation to make. If
also.seems fairly cheeky; and it makes dis-
cussion of Millen’s scheme futile. This.
owever, can be said with confidence —
u the Commonwealth and States embarlf


upon an undertaking involving tens of mil-
lions of pounds without knowing where the
money is, or, at best, trusting to luck to
stagger along from month to month, the sub-
sequent proceedings will be very interesting,

While Some Men Die.
E\ery nation in the war now raging is being
economically bled. In every one of them
there are men to whom the war has brought
riches. W ith these they lay the foundation
of more wealth by lending to their country
in its agony,. so that the common herd may
have the privilege of paying them tribute and
unborn generations live to curse the servility
and cupidity of their fathers. What is the
place of these spiders in the social
scheme? They are a curse, a menace. The
•people carry them on their backs and fight
the enemy at the same moment, and continue
the dreary job until death relieves them.
M herefore men raise a wild, unpractical
howl for the “conscription of wealth,” only
understanding, in a dull sort of way, what
they want and being hopelessly bewildered
about how to get it. They are really in
rebellion against their exploitation, not
against “wealth.” It is because they want
wealth and cannot get it—because the spiders
and parasites take possession of the national
earnings—that they are in revolt. And they
are right to be so. Parliaments fail abjectly
in their duty where they allow this horrible
evasion of citizenship to continue. A tit
man who will not tight for his country is
dodging a sacred duty. But, by comparison,
one who has not only kept his body in a safe
place, but has also stuffed his pockets, is
contemptible—almost as contemptible as the
public authority which allows him to go on
doing it. War is bringing a heavy burden
of personal woe and national embarrassment.
The mass of the people are already greatly
poorer; for although for the moment a per-
centage of the population may be earning
more than before, the majority find the pur-
chasing power of their incomes rapidly
shrinking. They are paying for the war—
making profits for the**: who. by getting
richer, are making the poor poorer and the
nation weaker. No gabble about supply and
demand can alter either the fact or the
abomination of it. Not all the blither on
earth about “equality of sacrifice” can make
equality where none exists, or make the sur-
render of affluence acquired while other men
are dying a sacrificial act. A man who is
no poorer because of the war is a chartered
shirker, and it matters nothing whether he is
hanging on to “war profits” or any other
profits.
The National Pantry and Prices.
Once more there is a yell for cheap wheat
and cheap meat, with an incidental demand
upon the Government to “step in and fix
prices.” The answer to it is that the
Government has stepped in and fixed
prices, and that the people of Aus-
tralia are getting both wheat and meat
cheaper than the people in any other part of
the world, and the producers of that wheat
and meat are getting substantially less also.
Under the Wheat Pool, prices of wheat are
fixed. Under the schemes by which various
Governments have made huge purchases of
meat, prices are nominally fixed for the
locally-consumed chop also. What seems to
be demanded by the people who are asking
for cheaper bread and steak and food for
the suburban rooster is that the prices shall
be pared down —that the fixing shall be done
with charitable feelings towards the purse
and appetite of the local citizen. The speci-
ous argument, after a prelude about “famine
prices,” is put this way :
Here in Australia are large quantities of wheat
and plentiful supplies of meat. The mice are
devouring the wheat, yet if a citizen wants any
he has to pay a high price for it. Why should not
the citizen be able to buy cheaply the wheat that
is rotting and the mouse is multiplying on ? There
are no ships to take the meat away. Why cannot
the Government order that the meat which is not
being exported shall be sold at cheap rates? The
country is bursting with food, yet the public finds
difficulty in getting enough.
As criticism of a mad social system this is
all very well. As a statement of the case for
cheapness it is hopelessly infertile. The
consequence of disposing of wheat in Aus-
tralia at, say, 2s. 6d. a bushel would be a
reduction in the earnings of the man who
grew it; and Heaven knows these are small
enough already. The mice would not eat an
ounce less. The meat problem is on much
the same plane. If bullocks and sheep are
taken from a grower and distributed at bar-
gain prices to the local population, that indi-
vidual is going to suffer in the pocket. There
are some cockies and some pastoralists
whom this would not injure very much, but
there are others who would merely be
assisted into bankruptcy. It is quite con-
ceivable that the country might organise a
system under which every individual growing
wheat or meat would pass over a percentage
of his produce every year to a National Grub
Department charged with the job of dis-
tributing it to the public at rates which
would stabilise the calculations made by
Iynibbs. This would, of course, demand
an organisation and readjustment of
taxation and other things which can-
not be arranged in five minutes. What
is not conceivable is that any authority
which does not control production can
fix prices without making a ipess some-
where, or that the prices of wheat and meat
can be “cheapened” in Australia to-day by
the order of politicians without inflicting a
gross injustice upon the grower. The yell
for cheapness is one for low wages for the
people who provide the public with refresh-
ments ; and for yells of that sort, or for
cheapness in any of its forms, this paper has
no time. Anyway, it knows of no reason why
a farmer should have his wages reduced in
order that a carpenter’s wages may be worth
more. It is well within the region of possi-
bility that the German submarine may create
conditions making wheat and meat in Aus-
tralia worth 2d. a barrowload. That would
be a tragedy, however, not only for the pro-
ducer but for the nation, and tuppences
aiight then be as hard for the consumer to
bud as shillings are now. Whereupon there
would probably be a cry for free wheat and
free beef, and great amazement to find that
after the first meal the pantry was empty.
This paper has a profound mistrust of the
price-fixer and all his works. If Govern-
ments are going to provide foodstuffs the
material should either be produced at
national farms or directly acquired on a
plain, business-like system. To start howl-
ing that there are heaps of sustenance about
and rushing it with open hands is piracy and
lunacy. The indisputable facts of the
moment are that Australians have a cheaper
meal than most other people ; that less than
any other people on earth should they yearn
for a fall in the price of their produce; and
that if the torpedo intervenes decisively
they will have food as cheap as dirt and a
disaster of incalculable magnitude to flavor
it with.
Should the Attorney-General be Chained
Up?
N.S.W. Attorney-General D. It. Hall
in his defence :-
The Bulletin rather savagely suggests that I
should be chained up because I have dared to doubt
the propriety of the Prime Minister re-opening the
Conscription contest. May I re-state the position?
On March 27, 1917, Mr. Hughes said, in his policy
speech at Bendigo, that he would not attempt to in-
troduce Conscription by either regulation or statute
during the life of the forthcoming Parliament. But,
if the national safety demanded it, he would again
submit the matter to the people.
In his speech at the Sydney Town Hall on April
4, 1917, he said:—•
We pledge ourselves in this forthcoming Parlia-
ment not to attempt to introduce Conscription either
by regulation or statute. We shall not attempt to
sneak in Conscription behind the backs of the people.
Only if the circumstances of the nation—of the
Empire—demand it shall we again put the question
to the people. If a great reverse should send the
Empire reeling are we to do nothing? If the very
foundations of our national being are endangered are
we to sit supine? Mr. Tudor says he will not accept
our pledge. Well, let me now ask him what he pro-
poses to do in the case of such a contingency as that
to which I have referred. If the great fleet which
now protects these favored shores be defeated : if a
great land victory gained by the Germans shall send
the allied forces reeling; if the dagger of the Hun
shall pierce the armor of the Empire, will Mr.
Tudor do nothing?
On these unequivocal statements he and his party
went to the election and were returned to power.
Does The Bulletin suggest that it is open to the
Prime Minister, who has definitely stated that only
on certain conditions would he re-open the con-
troversy, to take that course when not one of those
conditions exists? If so, I —and I think the majority
of the people^—beg to differ. A public man is as
much bound by his solemn promise as any other man
of honor.
Apart from the Prime Minister’s promise, surely
it is clearly unwise to take another referendum. On
the last occasion Australia turned down the Con-
scription proposal by 72,478 votes. Nothing has
happened to make the great bulk of the decent and
loyal citizens, who comprise portion of the
majority, change their minds, and certainly if this
loyal section would vote “No,” those whose "No”
was inspired by disloyalty or by personal fear have
meantime become possessed neither of loyal senti-
ments nor of courageous hearts.
It is, of course, impossible to foretell accurately
the future: one can only hazard an opinion. If the
question of Conscription should be really submitted
again I should, of course, vote and speak in favor ;
but I think the people of Australia would turn it
down on this occasion as they did on the last. We
should have a consequent waste of the tens of thou-
sands of pounds which it would cost to take the
vote. The very best that could be hoped for would
be a win by a narrow majority. In the past the
minority has been taught to submit to the majority.
When it comes to a matter of life and death —which
Conscription involves —it is difficult to accurately
forecast the troubles which would arise in forcing
such a system upon a large minority.
The military authorities tell us that we want
7600 recruits per month. This is generally thought
to be an outside estimate. We are raising 4500 per
month. Under Conscription we would send the full
number required. Under voluntaryism we fall short
at the rate of 30,000 a year. I don’t believe that it
is worth while risking the uncertainty of a contest
and branding the Prime Minister as a man upon
whose word not the slightest reliance can be placed
for the very shadowy possibility of our being able to
throw another 30,000 men into Europe by this time
next year. Our 30.000 men are not likely to prove
the determining factor in a contest where millions of
men are involved, and, this being the case, it would,
in my judgment, be unwise to risk another referen-
dum.
All of which is quite beside the matter on
which this paper had a few remarks to make
to its learned friend recently. What it com-
plained of was that he was making speeches
prejudicial to recruiting. In supplying
Hughes with reasons why he should not
take another vote on Conscription, this
alleged Conscriptionist supplied the shirker
with excuses for not enlisting. And he did
it merely to gain a few miserable votes. This
paper, on reconsidering the matter, and in
view of the increasingly urgent demand for
recruits, is amazed at its mildness in merely
suggesting that Mr. Hall should be chained
up till Holman’s return. It thinks now
that Holman's return should make no dif-
ference.

The Armies that Stayed Away.
The fact that repatriation means more than
the delivery of a few windy speeches by
win-the-war patriots is forced home rather
fiercely by the large historical fact that
many of the world’s big armies on foreign
service never came home at all. The Gauls,
who invaded Greece and smashed things as
the Persian with an empire behind him
never succeeded in doing, passed on into Asia
Minor and stuck fast. Beyond the Danube
there were people in office talking about
repatriation for years, or even for centuries,
and no doubt a few of the sick and wounded
got home in the earlier days of the cam-
paign; but the great mass of the army
stayed close to the ancient family domains
of the Angora goat, in the region where the
present Oonstantinople-Bagdad railway
stops short to look at a mountain range
which fortunately it hadn’t punctured be-
fore the war began. Another army that
failed for want of shipping facilities or
organising capacity to get back home was
the one raised in Britain in the latter days
of Roman rule to press the claims of an
outsider to the imperial purple. The yarn
goes that on its backward march it followed
the land as far as it could, but being unable
to swim the narrow seas sat down perma-
nently in Armorica and gave the name of
Brittany to that ragged corner of France.
In very modern times an Irish legion took
to brigandage in the Italian hills because
there was no other way in which it could
get a living; and on present appearances an
Australian army corps might easily get lost
in France and forgotten until its sweetheart
was old and grey. At any rate, on all the
evidence, a very great deal more than talk
is required to place the big bulk of our Aus-
tralian soldiers once more on Australian
soil. Our troops are not enlisted for per-
petual service, and their right to terminate
their engagement might easily occur before
one-tenth of them have been placed on board
ship. Yet when the thousand home-coming
anchors rattle down in Australian ports, if
they ever do so, the greater trouble'will be-
gin. That it can be. overcome is no doubt
true. Two things properly done would
place every returned soldier in a useful job
either in industrial life or in the wider open
spaces. One is the right handling of the
Northern Territory: the other a real tariff
which would give Protection to Australian
industries. The first would add a new State
to the Commonwealth with all scope for the
high adventure which accompanies the con-
quest of a new realm ; the second would cre-
ate a demand for as much labor as could be
obtained from any source. At present, how-
ever, the parochial ideas of those who are
earnest about the problem seem confined to
the erection of a four-roomed cottage on a
vacant suburban plot, while the imperial
notions of the others soar into words and
more words and a speech on the top of that.
So there remain two large probabilities. One
is of a place called Australia somewnere in
Europe or on the road home across Asia,
which would represent in future history
the best effort of the soldier to repatriate
himself; the other is a huge commotion in
Australia itself with Billjim looking for
bread-and-butter in his own fashion and
tossing politicians out of the road in his
angry haste. Either way the prospect is less
pleasant than the application of a little
ordinary horsesense to a plain problem.
—— *
The prolonged negotiations between
Hughes and Labor about shipbuilding had
one interesting feature. The negotiations
were declared to be necessary in order that
an arrangement might be made to ensure
continuity of work. “Unless co-operation of
labor is secured we shall not go on with this
great national work,” said Hughes. “It
would be futile to do 50.... Without con-
tinuity of work we would be building on
quicksands.” Shipbuilding is only one of a
thousand industries Australia should have;
yet it is said by the Government that if the
building of ships were entered upon without
a change from those conditions which exist-
ing industries have to meet and which later
ones may have to endure, the result would
be chaos. If this means anything at all it is
that under prevailing conditions no indus-
trial progress can be looked for. There are
quite a lot of people who have been thinking
this for a long while. What a stinging com-
mentary on the government of the country
it all amounts to!

Let P.M.G. Webster have his say:—
I repeat what my statement set out —that the
charge is untrue so far as the Postal Department is
concerned. Last week The Bulletin dropped from
£10,167,125 to £1,854,271. Even the latter is
not correct, as such is included in the larger amount
against which the Department has built up realisable
assets valued at £11,000,000.
Your last paragraph, inferring that, by securing
for my electorate as a private member as efficient
services as possible, I did so to bribe them for their
votes, is unworthy, and condemns me for doing as
a private member what I am endeavoring to do as
a Minister under exceptionally abnormal difficulties,
viz., give the public the best service possible at a
reasonable cost.
With regard to your complaint about imperfect
serviee, in the paragraph on increased postal rates,
I desire to say that I do not expect to ever reach
that ideal perfection you represent; nevertheless, I
have no hesitation in saying that the service is as
good as, if not better than, hitherto, in suite of the
manifold difficulties arising- from the war.
In reply:—
The Bulletin did not drop from £10,167,125 to
£1,854,271. What it said was that “from the begin-
ning of Federation until now £10,167,215 has been
handed out of the national cashbox to the Post
Office” —that there had been shameful mismanage-
ment when the Commonwealth’s greatest monopoly
had to loaf on the Treasury for its development to
the extent of over £10,000,000 in 16 years. And
it is evidence of shameful mismanagement in very
recent times that there was a loss of £1,854,271 in
four years’ trading within this present decade. Turn
up the Commonwealth Government’s own “Year
Book”:—•

persist that the £1,854,271 is represented by “realis-
able assets” ?
The Bulletin did not condemn \v ebster for
“giving the public the best service possible at a
reasonable cost.” But it did say that part of these
postal and other millions had been spent to corrupt
the constituencies —to buy votes; and it did say and
repeats that Webster himself, at the last general
election, issued a printed appeal for support
(Webster’s photograph thrown in) on the ground
that he had been conspicuously successful in getting
postal money spent in his distriat.

From a wireless cabin: —
Re the Cumberland. From 6 p.m. Saturday till
9 a.m. Monday a wireless “barren patch” exists
along the coast, during the daylight hours, for about
80 or 100 miles on either side of Gabo. The station
at Flinders Island was designed to fill this gap, but
as the station is closed from 6 p.m. till 9 a.m., and
all day Sunday, it might as well be in Alaska during
those stretches. Had the Cumberland affair hap-
pened on a Sunday her S.O.S. would have sparked in
vain. Scores of times when in the vicinity of Gabo
on Sundays or at night I have pushed telegrams out
on spec, and on inquiring subsequently if my
chance shot, had found any targets, on all occasions
except one I have been informed that the stations
a: Sydney and Melbourne had not received anything.
A great number of our coasters swing around Gabo
on Sundays; but, the wireless operators’ union hav-
ing secured a precise eight-hours day for its mem-
bers, these vessels are in a position to receive wire-
less signals for only a third of their time. During
the other 16 hours, ships, in case of emergency,
would have to depend on ability to “reach” one
of the land stations. With the comparatively short
range obtainable in daylight (a mystery never satis-
factorily cleared up) this is a difficult matter; in
places it is an impossibility. To unnecessarily add
to .the difficulty by opening so-called daylight sta-
tions. of which Flinders Island is one, when the sun
is hours high, and closing them long before sundown,
is something more than foolish. To close Flinders
on Sundays is specially so, as on that day the
portion of the ocean it was designed to protect is
at its busiest.
Two or three thousand men who belonged to
the little permanent army of Australia before,
the war have gone to the Front. Some of
them have been returned; more will no
doubt be returned later on. Some have come
back sick or wounded ; others have been sent
home because, so it is alleged, their services
are necessary in Australia; others again,
perhaps, because they were failures. If
there are these last, what are we going to
do with them? The permanent men are
those to whom the training of the Australian
army of defence is entrusted. Should the
Fates be so unkind as to compel us to fight
for the defence of our own land, it is to
the officers in the permanent ranks that we
should have to look for leading, in the earli-
est stages of the war, at any rate. Surely,
then, we should know whether any men who
either have been returned or will be returned
in the future have been relieved of service
at the Front because they were no good for
the job. To retain their services, after those
services have failed in the practical test, will
be a menace to Australia. This country
cannot afford to pay anyone good money
for doing nothing: but it would be far better
to do that than keep failures where they
would be merely a national danger.
“M.G.”: We are always trying to replace
with the same thing—paper by paper, iron
by iron, and so on. In our we are
reduced to one meal a day, owing to the
cost of saucepans. No iron, no steel, no
aluminium to make more! Now I want to
know, if we can make concrete barges (as
we have done) and concrete ships (as we
propose to do), why can’t we have concrete
pots and pans? We have earthen pie dishes,
China plates and glass rolling-pins. What’s
the matter with concrete —with a cement
lining if it is otherwise too rough inside for
cooking purposes? What could be worse, at
any rate, than a porous, brittle, cast-iron pot or
saucepan, with a lead lining or a bad enamel?
It is time this country left off turning over
old invoices and groaning at “shortages” and
“increased prices,” and took to looking for
“substitutes” and “things just as good.”
Why, we have blue-metal enough in this
country to cook the world’s dinners if we
liked to re-consider its uses in the light of
chemistry, just as we have enough gelatine in
prickly-pear to keep us in a substitute for
linoleums, mouldings and wall-papers if we
seriously enough considered its use in such
directions. Bad as it might be at the start,
it could be no worse than some of the things
we are doing with at present. In relation
to the whole thing, why look skyward praying
for meteorites when we have a world of vege-
tation, rock and chemicals round us?
“Te Babarantiko” on Fijian land-owner-
ship :
If the first Governor had decided that all lands
within this continent of Australia were the property
of Binohi, to be held or disposed of by lease but
not by sale, one can imagine the state of mind of
the present white inhabitants. That about illus-
trates the position of land ownership in Fiji.
Fiji native land ownership is somewhat on rho
communal system, with control vested in groups of
Maori Soldiers.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Who went out with the war canoes
When Death was in the seas?
These went out with the war canoes,
And these, and these, and these!
Side by side with the pakeha.
They fought that Right might win.
Lo ! in city and lonely pa
Our pride has made us kin.
Who came in from the Maori lands
To take the soldiers’ way?
They came in from the Maori lands,
And they, and they, and they !
Big-eyed lads with the swordsmen’s wrists
And calm-eyed Maori braves —
Warm are the soft Aegean mists
That drift across their graves.
Who marched out to the battle-line
While royal trumpets blew?
You marched forth to the battle-line,
And you, and you, and you !
Smiling lips, that are grave again,
And heart that understands.
What of the homes that wait for men
Who tarry in far lands?
Who gave all for the English king.
For truth and liberty?
He gave all for the English king.
And he, and he. and he!
Chieftain’s blood in their veins ran strong
For so their grandsires died.
The winds their spirits bear along
Beyond the farthest tide.
Who went out with the w’ar-canoes
That dared the distant seas?
These went out with the war-canoeS,
And these, and these, and these!
Side by side with the pakeha
They died that Right might win.
Lo! in city and lonely pa
Grey grief has made us kin.
MAORILAND.
WILL LAWSON.

After two-score years of native land control only
a small portion is in use by the natives —a state
natural enough when rent obtained from portions
leased to agriculturists is amply sufficient for the
natives’ needs. Yet the native has become more reluc-
tant to even lease any of the surplus land since
the birth of the new cry of “Fiji for the native
Fijians.”
It is now argued by land-hungry whites in Fiji
that the Government, having the right to sell as
well as to lease native lands, should allot to the
natives land sufficient for their use, and sell or
lease the balance as it may think proper. But so far
the Government has not budged from its old policy.
How the natives would take a change of policy can
hardly be open to doubt.
Not that the Fijian white is landless. Quite a
number hold large areas of best Fiji soil, under free-
hold tenure or lease. Some use all, or portions, of
their holdings. There are geniuses, too, who have
hit upon the plan of leasing small areas to Solomon
and other Islanders, and to free Indians, rent free,
on a contract whereunder the tenant must sell his,
say, bananas, at a fixed price of one shilling per
bunch to the benevolent landowner. Others do
little with the land, while looking for green new-
chums with a little capital to put in along with
Chummy's own labor in developing the land for the
holder’s benefit. Could land but be made easier of
acquisition, speculators would have a fine time.
Considering everything, it would, then, be well
for Australia not to assist in any attempt to dis-
possess the native Fijians of their lands. The case
might be different if there was more than enough
labor available to cultivate the land already sold or
leased. But there isn't, and the labor supply is
decreasing rather than increasing. Incidentally, the
more it decreases the better the chances of agri-
culture in our own North.

The Centre of it all —the Trench.
The happenings at the Front are what count
in this business. As long as the German
armies are undefeated in the West the Allies
are not winning the war whether the Hun is
starving or not. And if the Russian armies
are not standing up to the enemy on their
side, then Ivan is not marching to victory
even though the Czar has been deposed. Mere
words about what must happen to the Ger-
man if Russia becomes consolidated are not
worth listening to. So far as we are con-
cerned, what is occurring in the trenches is
the surest evidence of what is taking place
behind them. A couple of weeks ago the
Russian fell on the Austrian and mauled
him badly. It looked like a tremendous revi-
val. But last week the German fell on the
Russian, and, up to the time of writing,
what he had been doing to the fleeing Bear
was painful to hear about. This is reported
to have “intensified the crisis.” Manifestoes
urging the army to fight have been issued.
Appeals are being made to the voiceless
millions of Russia to arise and save the situ-'
ation. Further proofs of German conspira-
cies at Petrograd have been unearthed. The
German appears to be preparing to put the
boot in over beyond Poland. It all makes a
picture of the wildest chaos. Russia may
yet do all sorts of things, but none of them
are being done now, and as the position
stands' at the moment the German has some-
thing more than a chance of not being
greatly bothered by his most numerous
neighbor. Meantime the European summer
is moving on. If there are any signs of the
German being beaten or his line pierced or
anything serious happening in the very near
future they are being kept a close secret.
Foolish talk about victory after a Big Push
or before Christmas has gone quite out of
fashion. The time was more than ripe ; for
the job we are up against is more than seri-
ous. Hifalutin’ about the birth of a new
democracy in Russia and the weariness of
the German is as futile as expecting to see
the war won by making speeches. The
struggle is boiling down to one of endur-
ance, and in such a test none can escape
being tried to nearly the last gasp. More
than one of the combatants is very near that
stage now. It is good to see a new r one
coming in with unimpaired strength on our
side. We want him.
The political situation in South Aus. has
all the grace and dignity of a midnight meet-
ing of cats, ivhere all parties scratch and
swear up to the very moment of marriage.
Last week Peake with his new Liberal team
met the Assembly and had a terrible row
with Vaughan, ex-Premier. His suggestion
that the support of the Advertiser had been
secured by recommending Bonython for a
“step” in knighthood was violently denied;
after which it came out that only the motive
was open to denial, the recommendation hav-
ing been minuted. As Peake must have
found this among his predecessors’ papers,
J. H. Vaughan, Attorney-General in his
brother’s late Government, called it “a delib-
erate and unpardonable outrage,” and
hinted that the Goi*ernor should deal with
Peake for having violated his Ministerial
oath of secrecy. Yet next day Peake was
offering to drop three of his own new team
and take in Crawford Vaughan and two
others. The offer remains open, and is to
be considered this week. Peake had to do
something, a big section of the Liberal party
being clamorous for Coalition. O’Connor,
the Parliamentary whip, had resigned his
post on that admitted ground, and well-
known citizens outside who signed their
names to strong arguments on that side
were Angas Parsons, K.C.; Holden, of
Norwood; and Hackett, ivho long ago was
law partner of that great little man, John
Bray. It remains to be seen whether another
section will not kick against any alliance
with Vaughan and his Labor policy. As
they are finding out in Federal politics,
though oil and vinegar have it in common
that they are liquids, while gunpowder is
quite different, being a solid, they still remain
oil and vinegar even though you store them
in the same cellar.
According to the annual report of Dr.
Willis. P.M.O. of the Education Department,
the N. S. Wales youngster is not getting
anything like a fair chance to mature into a
sound citizen. The State-school medical
service w r as reorganised in 1913, and about
the middle of 1916 the results of the first
round of inspection became visible. Of
247,385 children examined, 147,078 were suf-
fering from physical defects needing treat-
ment. Only 57,310 obtained treatment.
In round numbers 60 out of every 100 exam-
ined were suffering from physical defects,
and of every 100 needing treatment only 39
obtained it.
While 3987 children were found to require treat-
ment for eye defects, 3406, or over 60 per cent.,
failed to obtain treatment; of 2181 needing treatment
for ear defects, 1566, or 71 per cent., failed to obtain
it; of 6060 needing treatment for nose and throat
defects, 4306, or 71 per cent., failed to obtain it;
and of 36,331 needing treatment for dental defects,
16,343, or 45 per cent., failed to obtain it.
Obviously, medical examination —useful as
it is—does not nearly fill the health bill.
It merely discovers where treatment is
necessary,
Richard Binnie, just dead at 63. spent a
lifetime in commerce in Sydney and was a
director of the Bank of N. S. Wales and
many other affluent cos. The original Dick
Binnie was a saddler and saddlers’ iron-
monger—he started nearly 80 years ago—-on
Brickfield Hill. “Binnie’s Buildings” are on
the site of the old shop.
Perth is hanging to its reputation of being
the wettest capital on our map. On July 9
there was a bumper public meeting in the
Town Hall, convened by anti-shouters. to
pass a resolution in their owh favor. Mayor
Frank Rea and Attorney-General “Cocky”
Robinson decorated the platform, while
Anglican Archbishop Riley and Colonial
Sec. Colebatch talked lukewarm horse-sense
about moderation and national economy.
But the crowd wasn’t having any. It was
captious all through, and when his Gaiters
filially put the resolution it was jubilantly
howded down.
“The Old Soldier” : Indian Judge Richards,
who is now in Sydney, has just handed this
to a press interviewer: —
More than once since I started on my holidays I
have heard India described as a seething mass of
disaffection. Well, in my opinion there is more
human happiness among the 300,000,000 of people
in British India than in any other part of the
world.
Perhaps the Judge is right. Most Anglo-
Indians, like myself, however, would hardly
care to put it so strongly. It may be said
that the average intelligent native who can
read and write, and who has a fair know-
ledge of the history of his country during the
last two centuries, is very well satisfied with
things as they are. Less than 110 years
ago the villagers about Agra and Delhi were
constantly harried by plundering parties of
Sikh horsemen from the Punjab, and the
whole story is faithfully preserved by tradi-
tion all through the United Provinces, where
Richards is Chief Justice. But you can’t
measure all India by that.
Died in or thereabouts, Harry
Sylvester Darley, son of the late Chief Jus-
tice of N. S. Wales. Nature played a nasty
trick on Harry Darley. It gave him the .
tastes of a millionaire without giving him 5
the million ; and the idea of him making it
himself was out of the question. He was
tried in one or two jobs—as associate to his
father and as an ornament at Government
House. But he found it all a horrible bore.
One brief experience in Westralia suggested
that, had his kinsman Sylvester Browne
taken him in hand early, he might have done
well. But lie never struck the right A r ein.
Or, perhaps, the right mates.
8.M.” : It was my week for the “casual-
ties.” There was the usual assortment of
human repair jobs scattered about the out-
patients’ room—some with broken wings,
some with dislocated features and two with
damaged heads. I started bandaging one of
them when all hands were electrified by
a shriek of joy from an aged soapsuds-
duchess who had been sitting in a corner.
“ ’Ere they are!” she crowed. “ ’Ere they
are! And I thought I’d swallowed ’em!”
She held up, triumphant and relieved, a very
battered set of upper-storey teeth, which she
bad discovered in the depths of her handbag.

Mary is a nark. Having a sharp tongue
and a capacity for saying witty, cattish
things to (and about) her bosom friends, she
naturally leads Society in an English pro-
“A LITTLE BIT OF
FLUFF,” at Sydney
Opera House. Trippett
(Frank Harcourt).
vincial town. But
the husband of one
of her bosom
friends is made a
knight, and Mary,
whose husband is
a plain Mr., has
thereafter to suffer
the indignity of
playing second-
string in all Society
functions. This
annoys her so much
that she uses her
sharp wit on the
vulgar newly-
created Lady Bods-
worth. But when
she terms her rival
an “impropriety,”
the Lady threatens
to sue her for
slander. The efforts
of Mary to avoid
apologising form
the basis of Henry Arthur Jones’s
comedy, “Mary Goes First.” produced
at Sydney Criterion on Saturday. Her
triumph comes with the purchase for her
husband of a baronetcy. Considered in
another aspect, this English comedy is an
elaborate exposure of the practice of purchas-
ing English honors by large donations to
party funds. But in Australia Iv.C.M.G.’s
fall impartially on the deserving though rich,
and M's.L.C. are always citizens of culture,
genius and impeccability; so the theme of
the play is foreign to our audiences.
The comedy is clumsily constructed, setting
us wondering what has happened to Jones,
though the second act is brilliant. He
makes Felix Galpin explain the plot, with
tedious details, at the opening of the first
and the fourth acts. In the hands of a less-
wearied playwright all this would have come
to light naturally in the dialogue. The
fourth act, or epilogue, is quite unnecessary
and undramatic, since all that happens in it
is just what the audience has been led to
expect. The blame for the sympathy that is
aroused for the vulgar but simple target of
Mary’s pointed remarks lies, however, with
the actors and not with the dramatist. Marie
Tempest is the principal offender; for in the
epilogue she lacks that impulsive sweetness,
sympathy and large-heartedness that would
have honeyed poor Lady Bodworth’s defeat.
The part of Mary must have been built for
Marie Tempest’s dazzling virtuosity; it is
too much to expect that she should have in

Leslie Victor and Sidney Sterling pleasantly
fill important niches. The elaborate setting
in which the four acts take place gives a
powerful illusion of roominess.

The Gonsalez Opera Co. has caught
a fresh young Australian soprano, Emma
Franceschi, who achieved an immediate
success as an actress. Making her
debut as Santuzza in “Cavalleria,” she
had to gulp down stage fright. In the
gulping the voice, especially in the lower
register, lost much of its power, and was
buried by an importunate orchestra. But the
higher notes rang out sweet and true as the
woes of Santuzza increased, and a whole
three volumes of tragedy pierced the rafters
of- the Sydney Opera House. Of Italian
parentage, the Lismore girl has the fire of
Southern Europe mixed with the joyous
buoyancy of Australian sunshine, and her
success in opera seems sure. On Saturday
the co. shifted its baggage to Wirths’ Hippo-
drome, and will play there for a week or two.
The reappearance of Carrie Moore in “A
Little Bit of Fluff” coaxed a lot of Sydney
theatre-goers as far as the Opera House on
Saturday. The explorers found Carrie look-
ing like an elder sister of herself down from
the country. On her first entrance the
plump, over-dressed “Bit of Fluff” hummed
a bar or two of “Florodora” music to give
old friends a lead; but the slim young
Dolores of years ago couldn’t have been
detected without a theatrical finger-print
department. However, there were many bou-
quets and a lucky black kitten in a box for
old times’ sake at the end of a familiar farce
that has a fair number of laughs in it.

“A LITTLE BIT OF FLUFF." AT SYD-
