night-out with the Bit of Fluff's borrowed
£5OO pearl necklace in his overcoat pocket.
Daisy Atherton is the quiet and dainty wife
who finds the gaud and later receives it sus-
piciously as a little peace-gift from her lying
husband. Thos. A. Braidon makes most of
the fun as the sly and reluctant chapel-going
friend from the next flat who is dragged into
the domestic whirlpool and bullied into lying
for all hands when the Rajah who lent Fluff
the necklace is clamoring for its return, and
Fluff is worrying Ayres, and Ayres is trying
to get £5OO from a ’bus company on a fraud-
ulent accident claim so that he may buy
another necklace. While waiting for the
co.’s doctor to call and examine him he stays
in so little that the chapel-goer has to put
on his pyjamas and groan in his place when
the medico arrives unexpectedly. The posi-
tion then is that the claims-inspector has ex-
amined one liar and the doctor the other;
but the outlook isn’t pitch-black until both
officials arrive together. Then the farce
flies to pieces and the curtain is dropped to
prepare for a third act that shouldn’t hap-
pen only it contains a perfect type of a rude
religious servant. Before it does happen
Eardley Turner gives a true sketch of the
old-style practitioner, and the action of the
piece is interrupted twice to allow the spirited
Carrie to sing two songs.

* * * 5?
The days of “Grumpy” at Melbourne
Royal are numbered, and to-night Cyril
Maude will speak his last speech before the
curtain and bestow his heartfelt blessing on
the city that has given him such a mighty
good time. Audiences last week didn’t get
the best of “Grumpy” as a play, but they
mostly didn’t know what they were missing,
so it made no difference. Betty Murray, the
colleen who plays Susan in the ordinary way,
met with an accident to her dear little nose.
In her absence Miss Pater migrated from
the part of the Doctor’s wife to play Susan,
whilst another girl appeared as the matron
whom Grumpy detests. The management
that takes the money can’t be cordially told
to keep the change. On Saturday comes
“Daddy Long Legs.” introducing Ivathlene
Macdonell. and bringing Charles Waldron
back to Melbourne.
* * *
Frocky Evelyn Scotney and her husband,
Howard White, came back to Melbourne
Auditorium last Saturday, and a great crowd
of old admirers rolled up to welcome them.
The pair are not a great vocal combination.
Madame produces a fairly high-class high
soprano with an art that is less than con-
summate, and White is a good, honest singer,
with a distinct enunciation and a voice of
middling quality. But they sing pleasing
selections of songs and operatic numbers, and
their linked personalities are interesting. The
pretty little wife in a short, stiff muslin skirt
looks rather like a Columbine with a gauzy
tail. The husband is the very antithesis of
a harlequin, though he might be an impecca-
ble chemist who had taken to the Church.
The pair stand for harmonious discord in
style. She is diffidently gay; he makes little
speeches with grave assurance. His “Chris-,
tian war” oration, apropos a Star-spangled
Banner handed across the footlights, was the
crowning point of the successful concert on
Saturday. Among the more musical items
the Scotney rendering of “Lo! Here the
Gentle Lark,” with Amadio for flautist, was
the gem of the evening. Nor could any song
have been more appropriate to the song-
stress, for she seems something of a gentle
lark herself. The supporting solos of Amadio
and pianist Elvins shared in the supply of
customary encores. Further concerts are
announced for to-night (Thursday) and
Saturday, after which General Desire may
he expected to order another campaign.

The Tivoli’s Melbourne bill this week in-
cludes the last two acts of “Sw T eet Nell” and
new vaudeville turns. Nell Stewart plays
with all her old vim and decorative art in
Act 3, and Blakiston with the rest of the co.
makes a very good job of the final scene in
the big room at Whitehall, where the sound
of town talk never reaches. Jeffreys of
course is ultra-violent. But his explosive gur-
gling is strictly according to Australian tra-
dition. The first person to wear the long
wug in this country was an Anglicised Dutch-
man who gurgled and spluttered with some
difficulty in the English language; conse-
quently all his successors have talked as
though they had a foreign rage in their
throats. Among the new people are Clyde
Cook, who repeats the lamp-lighter business
previously seen in musical comedy, which first
stamped him as a droll performer who got his
first ideas from the late Johnny Coleman and
lost none of the humor of them. Mabel Morgan,
even handsomer than of old, displays a fine,
tall figure in a variety of gorgeous apparel,
and a voice full of head-note, Thurber and
Thurber are the funniest of mock strongmen,
working with invisible wires. Also Nelusco
and Hurley repeat their songs and conversa-
tions, in which the lady is always overcome
with merriment when she looks at the gentle-
man. She can’t start to address him with-
out bursting into laughtep, and that’s where
she seems to miss the joke. She ought rather
to burst into tears.
“Her Greatest Performance,” filmed at
Hoyt’s Melbourne house on Saturday, is a
heavy bouquet flung at a, decayed reputation.
The foremost spook is Ellen Terry, who is
sentenced to wander around a bloodstain in
the middle of the story. Dennis Neilson
Terry and other relatives accompany the old
lady, so the blot aforesaid becomes the locale
of a family reunion. Hoyt’s materialists,
however, get better value for their cash in
the new Metro offering, “The Dawn of
Love.” This amorous steeplechase shows
Mabel Taliaferro taking the fences at a
gallop.

AT POVERTY POINT.
“0.K.": Alany G. and S. characters may
be treated in a variety of ways, not pedanti-
cally Gilbertian yet not quite illegitimate.
But the demoniacal Mikado and the luridly-
haglike Katisha in the Firm’s latest revival
are over the odds. The spirit of “The Mikado”
is satirically humorous, whereas Yilliers
Arnold and Celia Ghiloni are earnestly and
appallingly ugly. Gilbert never intended them
to stand out so conspicuously from the rest of
the cast: their gruesome forcibleness is ille-
gitimately obtained. One laughs at it and
applauds the melodramatic singing, but there
have been other Mikados and Katishas who
were equally effective in a lighter way. The
best Mik. that I remember was Allan Morris
years ago (or it may have been the late
Rosevear), who made a specialty of his
seeming annoyance and disgust when Katisha
chipped into his song and commanded the
chorus to bow to daughter-in-law-elect. He
appeared as a potentate who couldn’t shake
himself free from the awful woman. Villiers
Arnold and Miss Celia act like a pair of con-
spirators in cruelty, going around together
seeking whom they may doom to a long and
lingering death.
Anatole France’s “The Man Who Married
a Dumb Wife” is not quite ready, so Gregan

Rep. performance next month of St. John

Home.” The Anatole France curiosity will
be ready in September.
The late Beerbohm Tree was first,
not long ago, in an actors’ popularity vote
in London Truth. The rest of the placed
half-dozen were Alexander, Wyndham, Bour-
chier, H. B. Irving and Martin Harvey.
Then came Hare (who built up a Maude-
iike reputation for studies of old men),
Forbes Robertson, Maude himself and Wal-
ler. Asehe was among the also-ran. He,
by the way, had a poor opinion of Tree.
“Only a character-actor,” he said. “Popular
in London, but not elsewhere. But he has
the preSs solid for him.”
“Stargazer” : The more Ko-Kos I see the
more pleased I am with the first I ever saw.
We are informed that the Ko Ko of C. W.
Workman won grateful expressions from
Gilbert. I hope it is not true. Howard Ver-
non’s grave, slow Ko Ko, dignified, earnest,
solemnly convinced of the seriousness of his
position, never admitting for a moment the
smallest consciousness of the fact that he
was a comic object, was, it seems to me,
absolutely true to the spirit of the part, and
a perfect embodiment of Gilbert’s humor.
Workman, with a better voice than Vernon’s,
presents a Lord High Executioner who is
always conscious that he is a fool, and is
always eager to impress his folly upon the
audience.
“Dry Shave”: Anyone who saw the late
Beerbohm Tree as Austin Limmason in the
dramatisation of Rudyard Kipling’s “The
Man Who Was” will agree with “Cockaigne”
(B. 19/7/T7) that the dead actor excelled
in the grotesque. Tree’s portrayal of the
human derelict was a masterpiece both in
acting and in make-up, and I’ll remember
him in that one-acter long after I have for-
gotten that he ever put the ’fluence on Trilby
or demanded his pound of meat.

Alfred Barry, who died at Parramatta
(N.S.W.) last week, aged 48, was a typical
6ft. Cornstalk who whetted his musical appe-
tite at eisteddfods, and in many Australian
States and Maoriland crowds have cheered
him for his excellent adjudications.
“L.11.P.”: Bert Bailey’s new repertoire
makes interesting reading. “Much Ado About
Nothing’’ has not been seen on the Aus-
tralian stage since the Brough-Boucicault
production in 1892. The fine cast in-
cluded Titheradge (Benedick), Percy Lyn-
dal in Melbourne and Edward Sass in Syd-
ney (Don Pedro), Edward Sass in Melb.
and Frank Cates in Sydney (Don John),
Cecil Ward (Claudio), James Cathcart
(Antonio), Geo. W. Anson in Melbourne and
H. R. Harwood in Sydney (Dogberry),
Arthur Greenaway (Borrachio), A. R. Law-
rence (The Friar), Mrs. Brough (Beatrice),
Emma Temple (Hero) and Emma Gwynne
(Margaret), Verges (Boucicault) and Leon-
ato (Brough). Spong’s scenery was some-
thing to remember; one could wish for
nothing finer than the exterior and interior
views of Leonato’s palace, and the lovely
garden set. Jimmy Cathcart, who played
Antonio, had been the Claudio of Charles
Kean’s great revival at the London Prin-
cess in 1858. Titheradge once stated that
the show cost £4OOO before the curtain rose,
and little or no money w T as made out of
either Melbourne or Sydney run. Sheri-
dan’s “Critic,” another coming Bailey pro-
duction, has not been seen for years in Aus-
tralia. “The Corsican Brothers” was last
done by Julius Knight at Sydney Royal in
the Christmas season of 1909-10, during a
very oppressive summer. George Rignold
was the last to make “The Tempest” rage on
an Australian stage—in Melbourne only. He
was a very fine Caliban. Before he came to
Australia in 1885 Robert Brough was an
excellent Caliban in a big production at the
Dublin Royal in 1878 under Gunn, whose
nephew in later years partnered Clyde Mey-
nell in Australia. Sydney had a memorable
“Tempest” production at the old Prince of
Wales Opera House (whei-e the Royal now
stands) in 1869. when Wm. Hoskins, Nat
Douglas, Clias. Young, Jas. Rainford, Charles
Hall (Caliban), Wm. Andrews, and Florence
Colville figured in the cast. Hoskins, who
played Prospero, was the producer. Alfred
Clint did some wonderful scenery for this
production, and George Leopold saw to the
dances.
Tyrone Power, from California: —
The Rep. is a fizzle over here. I have seen a
few performances at these theatres, but they are
of no consequence, and the plays generally deal with
the family lavatory or are some vague undramatic
thrust into putridity. The police were asked to
close up one of these theatres, it being regarded as
a pest-house by the unenlightened citizens.
As far as the stage is concerned things are bad ;
indeed, I regret that America reflects the English
stage. Our plays are at a pretty low level; we
glorify the thief and the prostitute. Having run
through all the moral cesspools uneffectively, we
dramatise lingerie. To be a really successful
dramatist it is almost necessary to cleverly introduce
nakedness. The great drama is dead. The public is
no longer capable of judging the art of the actor (I
apologise for employing that overworked “art”) and
no longer is interested. The moving pictures have
saved the actor from ruin. From the profit of this
labor it will be possible for him to free himself
from the chains which commercialism, ignorance and
cupidity have for too long bound and tortured his
soul.
I often think of my days in Australia, and their
vicissitudes. No actor could ever have been better
received than I was—the finest and most discriminat-
ing public, and, above all, splendidly encouraging.
I shall always look back upon my Australian per-
formances as the happiest of a stirring career, and
I still have a hope that I shall have the privilege of
repeating them.
“T. 0.1.”: The Sun in an article on Larry
Foley and his digging experiences at Gul-
gong says that in the ’7o’s Laurence met
G. V. Brooke while the latter was perform-
ing at the Bark Theatre on the goldfield.
When Brooke left Australia Larry was just
10 years old and when Brooke went down
in the London in January, 1866. he had added
five years to his age. The boxer may, how-
ever, have met Jimmy Byers at Gulgong:
I think he was the only one left of the old
or very old school Who had played there.
Byers one night put on “Richelieu.” He
stopped at the principal hotel, of course.
The landlady had a special private parlor,
sacred to the “best folk.” On ushering in
some special customer she found to her dis-
may that all her beloved antimacassars had
disappeared. She informed the police, but
was inconsolable until Jim Byers returned
from the theatre with a bundle. He had
borrowed the lot to dress the cardinal. I
met Jim and his wdfe years afterwards,
in a spring cart, doing the townships
betw'een Rlayney and Y’oung, with a song,
dance and recitation show. The little woman,
who had been a leading actress, pathetically
told me she hoped some day to settle down
in a home of some sort.

HAMLET IN A CEMETERY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He held within one hand a script,
He shook thq other high.
A passion he to tatters ript.
He gave a piercing cry,
Addressing irresponsive stone
With Hamlet’s poignant dread,
Then cast him down, and writhing prone
He maundered to the dead.
I asked: “Then is there no escape,
And even in the pit
Must man endure this frantic ape,
And wreak no vengeance fit?
\\ hy do not all these ghosts arise
With criticisms grim
From granite roots the columns prize,
And cast their stones at him?”
The actor ramped among the dust,
And none turned in his grave.
Here was no animated bust;
Here none to heed the knave]
“Their rest,” said I, as forth I went,
“No actor may annul.
The man may be irreverent,
But he, is merciful!”
VICTORIA.
S. S.

“Murkah’s” sparks :—-
As '‘Devilfish’’ (B. 5/7/’l7) remarks, wireless
“dead patches’’ result from the action of mountains
in deflecting and absorbing the waves—“screening”
is the- wireless term, and the effect is the same as
that exerted upon sea waves by an island. But as
the sea wave soon picks up again after passing the
obstruction, so does the wireless wave, for instan <,
Port Moresby and Rabaul, though practically in each
other’s back yard, have considerable trouble in com-
municating, owing to screening by the intervening
lofty New Guinea country. Yet the signals which
Moresby finds so difficult to get are easily read at
Melbourne, 2000 miles further south. Travelling
between Westralia and South Africa ships get Perth
stajion’s signals at strength 7 when 1800 miles out.
But 800 miles nearer Perth their strength is only 5,
anrj with only 500 miles to go they are inaudible.
Yet at 300 miles the operator can sit out on deck
and read them by the sound they make as they
swish through the stay's. The cause is obscure; but
the phenomenon is generally attributed to magnetic
screening. I entirely disagree with “Devilfish’s
remarks re Hobart station, which is in my opinion
one of the very best in the Commonwealth. Sigs.
from it are read daily at Bluff (M.Tj.), 1000 miles
away, in broad daylight. One night in Noumea
Harbor I heard him very strongly with only a ship’s
aerial to gather the energy. Seeing that Hobart
commonly uses less than 3 h.p. these are good per-
formances.
“Stapleton”: The following figures, relat-
ing to the white population of the Northern
Territory, are from the Commonwealth
Bureau: —
Births.
Population. , K >,
Females. Male. Female.
1912 .. 621 23 10
1913 .. 677 27 26
1914 .. 721 31 27
1915 .. 876 33 28
1916 .. 928 44 30
This shows, at the least, that the women
are holding their own, and that the “deadly”
North is not all it is alleged to be by dis-
eolored-labor barrackers. Last year one
woman out of every 12 gave birth to a_ child,
while only one woman out of every 66 died.
The death-rate for males of all ages is about
one in 35 ; but it must be remembered that
gin, black and white, round and square, is
largely responsible for the majority of these
funerals. In five years there have been 28S
births in the N.T., while in the same period
only 51 women have died, an average,
roughly speaking, of 10 deaths per year. Set
agginst 57 births jper year it shows that
women in the Territory are doing their
share in helping to guard the open door.

“J.J.S.” : These times life for the man who
stays at home is a long procession of button
days, raffles and dances. In the tram the
other morning I was reading the newspaper,
and the sight of a heading “Combating the
Social Evil” set my neighbor going. “An’
about time they did do something, too !” he
observed. “Why, up pur way they ’as about
a million socials and euchre parties a
month, an’ they expects a bloke to weigh in
every time!”
“Mud Hook” discourses on feet: —
This war has produced numerous complaints of its
own. The four I have in mind at present are
trench-feet, trench-fever, shell shock and the Censor.
The last-mentioned is a sort of malignant growth
that acts as an impediment to almost everything,
principally recruiting. Of the others trench-feet is
causing the most trouble, and so far the doctors
have not thought of a cure. The most popular treat-
ments are (1) amputation, (2) another hospital.
Often No. 1 is jibbed at; but the time comes when
the last of the series of hospitals is reached. Then
the first-mentioned treatment is resorted to in a bad
case. Of course there are mild attacks which more
or less treat themselves; but when the feet have
become badly affected, mortification sets in and
there is little hope of saving the limb. Usually, in
a bad ease, the doctors prefer to leave the foot or
toe alone, and in a month or so it cures itself by
dropping off.
In the Epsom War Hospital I took an interest in
three Australian trench-feet cases—not that they
were the only ones, for in a single convoy of 86 Aus-
tralians 59 had this trouble. That was about the
middle of winter. One of my specially interesting
cases was an abo. He had double pneumonia at the
same time, and as there wasn’t much chance of his
fighting both complaints at once, and as his feet
were easier to amputate than his lungs, he was
relieved of them. To the surprise of most of us the
rest of him recovered. Another was a Queensland
boy of about 18. His toes were all affected. When
I first saw him seven of them had dropped off,
leaving perfectly clean, healthy scars. He was
waiting for the other three to depart and then he
was going back home. No. 3 had it badly; both
feet had mortified to half-way between the ankle
and knee and had turned coal-black. They tried
electric treatment on him, and in a fortnight had
succeeded in gaining a quarter of an inch on the
blackness. But there was no hope. They both fell
off in time.
A lot of trouble is taken to prevent the complaint,
but the half-frozen mud soon tells. Plenty of clean
socks and time to change them and keep the feet
clean will go a long way towards preventing it.
Whale oil was tried, but was a failure. It isn’t
merely cold that causes it, or there would be an
epidemic in Sydney at present. There’s something
waiting for the man who can find a good preventive
and a cure, and that something includes the grati-
tude of a couple of hundred thousand Australians
and a few million English Tommies.

“Good Australian”: Midday luncheon
clubs are a feature of American city life.
Merchants, newspaper men, manufacturers,
advertising men, automobile managers and rate-
payers, as well as political parties, get to-
gether in clubs, which meet at midday lun-
cheon, to hear a “talk” by a prominent visi-
tor or somebody who brings information
about the common object of the members of
the club. The luncheon usually costs 35., and
consists of four courses, two jugs of iced
wetter and a small cup of black coffee. Some-
tipies the speeches are interesting, sometimes
humorous, but always boostful. It is the
proper thing for the chairman to praise him-
self and the speakers to praise themselves,
freely, frankly and immodestly. The chair-
man demands a “roll-call” of the members.
Each man in turn puts down his fork (all
things in America are eaten with a fork),
stands up and says, for example: “I am
Gumshoe Gabriel, shoe-purveyor ; not a Russ
or a Russian, but a rusher when I’m out to
get yarr orders.” (Loud applause.) Next
man roars in a bellicose voice : “I am Victor
Levy, garter manufacturer. I have found
out a garter at 75 cents, which will hold up
any sock on earth. It is doo to appear in 10
days, and will be a world-beater. If it
succeeds I will employ 1000 Californians in
my factory within live years. My guest is
my sister-in-law, and my wife knows we’re

we imitate this idea at home?

If you sat out a drama with a plot like
this you'd say it was overloaded beyond the
Plims@ll of probability: Villain, a German
of 94, at present in Germany, although origi-
nally exiled therefrom (in 1869) for having
fought a duel with Bismarck’s nephew;
heroine, a woman of 66 years, 46 of them
wedded, who fell in love with the villain on
the steamer by which he w r as travelling to
Australia, and, on arrival, married him
against the advice of her friends; interlocu-
tor. a friend of the husband, who told the
wife that her spouse had a wife and children
in Germany. Yet these were the people of the
play in a divorce case before Madden, C.J.,
in Melbourne, when Emily von Stranz ob-
tained a decree nisi from Otto von Stranz on
the ground of desertion. She said that when
she cross-examined him about his previous
marriage lie first said that he was married
to the best woman in the world —herself.
Then, being a Ilun, lie spoilt the compliment
by telling her to go to the Devil. She went,
however, to Williamstown, and subsequently,
after a lapse of years, to law, with the result
above set forth.

“R.S.A.”: Can any easy-chair military
expert supply me with the origin of the
expression “swinging the lead'’?
The term is used to indicate the
higher and more dignified forms of
malingering. The gentleman who
goes into hospital with homesick-
ness or headache and spins out his
holiday therein to three or four
months (and such occurrences are
by no means rare) is said to be
“swinging the lead.” The profes-
sion is brought to a fine art
towards the end of winter, w r hen
there are signs of a Spring offen-
sive. Likewise do I desire the
beginning of “getting the wind up”
or “putting the wind up,” which,
being translated from Billjimsh
into English, means putting the
fear of the Lord into anyone.
“M.R.8.” : The Postal Department has
established another speed record. Posted at
Launceston on January 15. 1904. a letter did
not reach Sassafras till April 30, 1917, thus
taking 13 years to travel 70-odd miles. Mean-
while the addressee —a schoolmaster —had
moved to East Devonport, 14 miles further
on. The Rip Van Winkle letter was re-
addressed, and took only two months more
to whirl to his outstretched hand.
THE TIDY WIFE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
’Tis Martha Bailey, trim and clean,
And hard and stern of features,
The most industrious, I ween.
Of God’s demented creatures.
She cleanses all within the place
With soap and bathbrick daily.
Then listen and you’ll hear her case
Against the other Bailey:
Don’t you tramp now on thet floor
Here — win’s thumb-print’s on this door?
Off thet heart’rug! Mind them ’obs!
Ain’t 1 got enough o’ jobs?
Lor’ iv ’eaven, hole et that:
’Ere yiv gorn ’n’ ’ung yer ’at
On the peg I shone up smart —
’Snovgh to break a woman’s ’eart l
Dame Bailey’s home is all her pride.
At daybreak she commences;
When she has cleaned the whole inside,
She washes down the fences.
She rakes the yard of leaves and roots,
The tiles she hoses straightway—
Poor Ben should always leave his boots
Outside the precious gateway.
Off them tiles there when you pass!
Mote yer walkin’ on the grass !
Why ’m 1 a slave all day ?
Put yer blessed tools away!
In the tool-house? Don’t you dare!
Ain’t I jest bin cleanin’ there?
Now yiv pushed me pots apart—
’Snough to break a woman’s ’eart!
Good Martha spreads a yellow mat
To keep the carpet’s splendor.
A rug is set for saving that;
A cover fends the fender.
They’ve seven rooms, and live in two,
So polished all their trash is;
So spotless clean, and trim, and true,
Ben longs for dust and ashes.
Wot’s that? Would you smoke in ’ere?
Light a fire? No blessed fear!
Fillin’ cliimbleys up with sut;
Wot you’re fit fer is a hut!
Gart! that cushion cover’s clean.
Look ’ere where yer feet have been....
So new doin’s ever start —
’Snough to break a husband’s ’eart!
VICTORIA.
EDDYSON.


A Central Australian Letter to the Great Medicine, CLEMENTS TONIC
One of the most remarkable testimonials of many remarkable ones given to Clements
Tonic Ltd., sent from Alice Springs, Central Australia, a distance of over 1000 miles
from Adelaide, and far from any centre of medical aid, showing the value of this
medicine to those living in remote country parts. .Mr. Hallett’s letter is here
published in full. Kead it.
Stuart Arms Hotel, Alice Springs,
CLEMENTS TONIC LTD. Central Australia. 2/4/16.
“4 few words in praise of the wonderful good your Tonic has done for me
during an illness of nearly five months, in which my legs from my feet upwards and
above my stomach were swollen to more than twice their ordinary size.
“I was almost helpless, could not stoop or do anything for myself. In trying
to lie down I had a feeling of strangulation, and was in a terribly bad way, and over
1000 miles from Adelaide. A lady friend who herself nursed me through that trying
time strongly recommended Clements Tonic. She had some in the house, and wired
to Adelaide for a supply. After the first two bottles I felt a slight improvement;
Clements Tonic Ltd., Rozelle, N.9.W,
the feeling of strangulation became less, my appetite increased, and I hare kept on
ini proring.
“I have taken three small and 12 large bottles, and am note so far recovered
that I am leaving Alice Springs to take up my duties as Foreman of the Over-
land Telegraph repairing party, which / have held for nearly thirty years, and
am well known from Darwin to Adelaide.
“/ may say lam still taking the tonic. All my acquaintances had no hope of
my recovery; even a trained nurse pronounced it a hopeless case; but Clements
Tonic built me up, enriched my blood, gave me natural sleep and strength, ami I
am thankful to say l feel almost as well as ever 1 did in my life, and the swelling
has all disappeared.
“(Signed) GEORGE HALLETT.”
Witness: Mrs. M. E. Jones, Alice Springs.
The original letter may be seen at the offices of Clements Tonic, Ltd., Rozelle, N.S.W.
Clements Tonic should always be a part of every home equipment.
Get it and get well to-day. All Chemists and Stores sell it.
The statement of Billjim’s mother: —
I am a widow, and my small daughter and I
were depending on my two sons, who went away
with the first boats to leave for the Front. One
was killed in Lone Pine on the 6th August, 1915.
He was reported missing for nearly six months prior
to being reported killed. He left me 4s. a day, and
after his death the Military allowed me 4s. 6d. per
week pension, afterwards calling in my hook for
alteration and increasing it to £l. Now they say
they find they have paid me his allotment for longer
than they should, so they are asking for over £3O
back. They have taken the £l5 due on his deferred
pay, and are now demanding back over £l5 more on
account of this son.
The other boy left me 3s. per day, and when
some rule was passed that every man’s pay had to
be raised to Bs. per day and the extra paid to his
dependents, they increased my allowance to 4s. per
day. I understood that last October they made
another rule that he was to receive 10s. ; anyhow,
they raised my allotment to 4s. 9jd. Now, two
weeks ago, they have come to the conclusion that
I am not entitled to that extra money, and are
demanding it all back, over £35, on this boy’s
account alone. This makes £65 on account of the
two boys.
The Military now say they will only allow me
£1 2s. per fortnight until they get this money back.
Had Billjim’s mother known that she was
getting too much money the Department
would be completely justified in making the
deduction now. But, so far as The
Bulletin can ascertain, there is not the
faintest suggestion that she did not believe
that she was entitled to all that the Depart-
ment was paying her. Of course, the money
has been spent; so that, in insisting upon a
deduction from future payments, the Depart-
ment pinches this unfortunate woman in
order to recover losses made through its own
blundering.
An Australian artist in London, who had
a commission to paint the portrait of a lady
whose husband has become eminent in
trade, talks of the manner in which the food
shortage affects the upper classes :
I saw a whole string of poor women waiting for
the privilege of buying potatoes as I motored out.
Then, two hours later, the sitting was interrupted
while my lady fed her cat, a gorgeous Persian,
barbered and manicured to perfection, its claws
dyed a deep pink, a little gold chain collar about
its neck, with a small platinum bell attached. The
cat’s dinner was brought in a small porcelain bowl
on a tray, and my lady cooed to the sweet thing as
she fed it with—what do you think ? —fresh oysters
at five shillings the dozen!
And for us the worst of it is that some mad-
dened proletarian, knowing that such things
happen in England, will bring his bitterness
to Australia and start to raise his wild yells
of revolution here.

The. other day a Melbourne drapery firm,
advertising a bargain sale, got more than it
looked for. A particularly showy hat with a
lancer-plume, which for some days before
the sale had been featured in one of the shop-
windows at a fancy price, was “marked
down” to what was by comparison a mere
trifle, and the fact was put in bold type in
the newspaper-advts. A horde of women had
NOT MUCH OF A VILLAIN.
"My wife believes everything I say."
"You ought to be ashamed of yourself!”
"Oh, I wouldn't go that far. You see, I don't get a, chance to say very much."
seen and hungered for that hat, and the new
price fixed ’em. They were down Bourke-
street bright and early on sale morning, long
before the doors opened, swarming around
the shop-front until a young man with a
sad eye cautiously threw back the barrier.
With one accord the bunch tore for the first
floor, where the Spring poems in women’s
headgear bloomed, and demanded the Hat
in a long, feverish shriek. It was a really
passionate Thrift-movement; but it was
thwarted by its own momentum, for in the
struggle the hat was battered to pieces and
became nobody’s darling. It sounds like an
iEsop’s Fable.

“J.C.M.”: “A.R.” (B. 12/7/T7) is in
error when he says that the signals showed
the crossing safe on the day the Candidate
capsized on the Port Macquarie (N.S.W.)
bar. On the contrary, the stand-off signals
were hoisted for the benefit of
three vessels outside —the Ada
and Eva, Empress of India and
Candidate. As there was an
easterly gale blowing at the time
they disregarded the signals and
attempted to cross in. The two
first-named vessels made the bar
safely, although the Empress of
India came very near sharing the
fate of the Candidate. Caught on
a huge sea she broached, but the
plucky men on her wheel got her
straightened up before the next
sea caught her. The Candidate
was caught in a race of seas. She
broached on the first, was caught
by the second before she could be
straightened up, and capsized
three times before she finally took
ground. When she righted the
second time the skipper was still
clinging to the deck. The only
attempt at life-saving was made
by the big Newfoundland dog
belonging to the ship. He was
seen swimming shorewards w T ith
somebody clinging to him. A
large sea. however, parted them,
and the dog was the only one of
the whole complement to reach
the shore alive. After regaining
his wind he made another plucky
attempt to save some of his ship-
mates, but was thrown back on
the beach exhausted.
“Billzac”: The “spit-and-polish” mania
gets some generals down. A few days after
we desert Anzacs had done a big job and
been patted on the back for it an order was
issued that the G.O.C. would inspect us.
The G.O.C. duly inspected, condemned and
blasted the -whole brigade. He “considered
it a damned disgrace that he should have
been allowed to inspect such filth ; he con-
sidered there was not one clean piece of iron-
work in the brigade.” In fact, he was bit-
terly annoyed and humiliated that he should
be the commander of such a mob. Our
attention was called to the fact that only a
few yards away was the perfectly polished
gear of the Yeomanry. Apparently the only
things that the G.O.C. could not condemn
were the rifles and other fighting equipment
-—including us. The men who explode in
this fashion are nearly always either officers
who have been in the habit of training
Britain’s standing army or others who think
that because the “regulars” and Australian
“permanents” do that sort of thing it is the
right thing to do. Lots of us don’t like be-
ing sworn at; lots more of us don’t like to
hear our own officers roared at for things
that don’t matter after they (or we) have
just done something that does matter. And
we are looking forward to the time, under a
system of citizen training, when the loud-
mouthed ruffian even though he wears
crossed swords will be dealt with just as
freely for this sort of blackguardly conduct
as a private is now for insolence to bis
officer.

It is not often that a newspaper editor has
a personal letter in a local rival paper;' but
there was one by Langdon Bonython, of
Adelaide Advertiser, in the Register and
Herald the other morning. ’Tiser and
Register are both on the Liberal side ; but,
all through the negotiations between
Vaughan’s “National” party and Peake’s
plain Liberals, Bonython has urged a local
Fusion, while the Register steadily opposed
it. Peake took the Register view, and
went out of his way to suggest in the Assem-
bly that Bonython was after a further
“honor.” (He was made a Iv.B. long ago,
in Kingston’s time, and local gossip has
always credited him with wanting it im-
proved to a K.C.M.G.) Vaughan at once gave
an assurance that none of his Ministry had
ever been “approached” on such a subject, and
Bonython repudiated the suggestion "with in-
dignation and profound astonishment. .. .
Never in my life have I asked a Minister for
anything for myself. I have too much re-
spect for journalism. In this connection I
call Mr. Peake himself, Richard Butler. Mr.
Verran and Mr. Vaughan as witnesses. They
have all been Premiers.” Naturally Peake
“caught it” in the Advertiser, which
described him as “a political adventurer”
who had “traded shamelessly, caring nothing
for the public interest,” and owning no
regard worth mentioning “for the decencies
of public life.”
It is just 50 years ago since a “break-
fast” was held by the Wesleyans of S. Aus.
to “promote the building of a college on the
site secured facing the east park lands.” The
same week the coming visit of the Duke of
Edinburgh was announced and tenders were
opened for the new Adelaide G.P.O. The
royal skinflint, who afterwards preferred to
be a German, arrived in time to lay the
foundation-stone of both buildings, and the
college took his then name, Prince Alfred. It
has thus had a career of nearly 50 years,
with Frederic Chappie—-lately retired —as
headmaster for 40 of them.
“Rob” : A reference to the Kaiser’s hunger
for the great, land-locked harbor of Vavau
(B. 12/7/T7) reminds me of the look I had
at the place in June, 1914 —a month before
the lid flew off. Our steamer, carrying a
Maoriland official party, anchored at the
bead of the long sound on the birthday of
Tonga's Majesty, and the town’s supply of
bunting fluttered loyally among the palm-
tops. Three out of every four of those flags
was the tricolor of the Hun. A Maoriland
Minister aboard humorously challenged our
skipper’s navigation, saying we must have
got into Friedrichshafen by mistake. The
Governor of Vavau, a highly-educated
Tongan aristocrat, invited us to a toast-
drinking function. AVe imbibed only German
liquors, nearly all the whites present were
linen-clad, close-cropped squareheads, and
most of the conversation was in Hunnish.
In spite of Tongan courtesy we were made
to feel like aliens. We left Vavau quite
satisfied about that island’s place in the
Hun’s scheme of future things. There was a
fete day, also, at Nukualofa, while we were
there, and large quantities of German bunt-
ing and lager were contributed to the revels;
but, unlike Vavau, Nukualofa’s British
seemed to be holding their own.

THE SWEETHEART OF THE TRENCH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Dearest, no girl was ever loved as you.
No woman, finger-fondled in the doze
Of summer, under boughs cool-kissed with dew,
Felt such caresses as your slim waist knows.
Bride of delight, your little white breast leans
Warm to my mouth, and all the red night clears.
I woo you gently, and my memory gleans
From your dear thrall the rose thoughts of old
years.
Your slender body is a poet's dream.
Your fragrant breath, incense of ecstasv.
What though the blazing rifles redly gleam,
Fearless, undaunted, you will charge with me.
Close-bosomed friend of long, unholy nights!
Lancelot may take false Guinevere to mate;
But, little sweetheart of the hell-hot heights,
Y'ou will be true to me, whate’er my fate.
Dearest, when wounded Time gives up the fight.
Be sure your darling help none will forget.
You soul of sanity, soft, soothing light
Of love and home, my little cigarette!
N.S.W.

“Base Records” in 1914 was not what it
is now, and there will be trouble in deciding
who was the first Billjim to enlist. But as
far as Victoria is concerned there seems to
be little doubt that it was Bugler William
Henry Groves, of Myers-street, Geelong,
lie was a painter, aged 21, and was sworn
in by Captain F. J. Jenkins on August 12,
1914. He is still in the thick of it.

General Bethune is likely to wear a cocked
hat at Sydney Government House. Bethune
(he pronounces it Beaton) is at present
Director-General of Territorials in Fogland.
His wife’s father was another Scotch gen-
eral—Lockhart—and there is one daughter.
Bethune lost his right hand while a major
in India, but not in action. He was cast
for the part of John Wellington Wells in a
garrison production of Gilbert and Sulli-
van’s “Sorcerer”; and at the dress rehear-
sal the chemicals of the love philtre which
Wells mixes in the teapot exploded, taking
off the sorcerer’s hand.
A pair of twins celebrated their 80th
birthday in Melbourne last week. One was
Mrs. Watson, sister of the late Archdeacon,
and the other Miss Nichols. Mrs. Watson
was born in Tassy, and (come to think of
it) so was her sister.

vofb. He has also

Groperland’s new Agent-General,-J. D.
Connolly, goes- to England via Canada and
the U.S.A. Mrs. Connolly and five daugh-
ters accompany him as far as Quebec, where
they will stay and put in time learning to
par'ley-voo until the submarine scare slackens.
Connolly, who made his pile contracting, is
a mild-eyed, elongated Australian bloke, and
looks more like an autumn poet than a suc-
cessful politician. He was member. for
Perth, and Hon. Minister in the late Wilson
Administration.
Edward Stone, Lient.-Governor and re-
tired Chief Justice of Groperland, and his
wife {nee Shenton) had their “golden wed-
ding” on July 13. The “six families”
turned out in force to tea and music, and
reminisced, between bites, anent the pre-
goldfields days when Perth was a select
sandpatch. There is a large Stone family;
one daughter is married to Tasmanian Judge
N. Iv. Ewing.
“Waihoi’s” send-off: —
Reefed his last sail, old Charlie Gore, well known
in Darwin and most parts of the Territory for close-
up 50 years. In the early ’7o’s he was storekeeping
with Dad, but before the present generation came
to- light Charles took to the water and was cutting
off corners or the northern coast in all sorts of
crafts, from dug-outs to antediluvian steamers. No
one could couTe within coo-ee of the old boy in
knowledge of mudbank and stray rock between Thurs-
day Island and Derby, rivers included. The old
Warrego, now a hulk in Darwin Harbor, had been
Gore’s home for the past year or so, and he made
it a floating library, for he was a tremendous reader.
Finish-up was heart failure when in a dinghy making
for Darwin’s one and only jetty—the jetty • where
the wily crocs, wait for the drunks from the Govern-
ment pubs to fall overboard.
Colin Travers, a young Gallipoli veteran
with a distinctively Australian sense of
humor, died last week- in Fiji as the result
'of a fall from a horse. Going to the war
from Sydney, he lost his stripes in Egypt
through inventing one of the best jokes of
the war. Marching his squad along the
road one day, he noticed an A.S.C. waggon
loaded with beer approaching. He promptly
halted his men, formed them up. and as the
beer swept past it got a Royal salute. A
wooden English staff-officer noticed the little
turn-out and wasn’t sport enough to grin
and forget it. The joker was reduced to
the ranks. Now the whole joke is over and
Roseville (Sydney) Rifle Club mourns for a
good shot.
Jock Garland, N.S.W. Solicitor-General,
orating in Sydney on France s Day, an-
nounced :—■
It is the intention of the Government to hand over
in perpetuity to the French Government the land at
La Perouse which contains the bones of the r rencn
navigator of that name.
There isn’t any such land. The monument
on the shores of Botany Bay, as its inscrip-
tion testifies, was erected _by the crews of
two French men-of-war, lying ih Port Jack-
son in 1824 or thereabouts, to mark the spot
from which the last news of La Perouse s
expedition was received. From relics found
40 years later, it seems probable that La
Perouse and his companions were ship--
wrecked at Vanikoro, in the New Hebrides;
but their actual fate is a mystery.
Edward Russell, Customs statistician,
who has died in Melbourne at 59, was a
kindly, dapper citizen with one interest in
life outside his business. That was daughter
-Una, who had leanings towards the stage.
In her chicken days Una studied toe-waving
and Voice-calisthenics, and father helped all
lie knew to theatrical success. He had many
pals able and glad to do something in the
way of press-publicity, so the clipping-book
grew fat. The biggest ad.., however, came
when yoUng Chia laid his prospects and
Wardrobe at the budding stage-star’s feet and
then upset things at the Chinese Consulate.
After the disaster, Mrs. Chia went off to
England, and it was her late dad’s intention
to join her there next year, oh his retiremeiit
from the service.
Benjamin Lee, the old Sydney “stipe” who
died a few days back, aged 91, came to Aus-
tralia in 1829. The father was soldier
who fought in the Peninsula Var and
in America, and returned to Europe just iff
time to miss Waterloo. The old chdp, who
lived in Parramatta for 50 years, and died
there, had a very large family, 14 of whom
arrived at the age of 21 years. Benjamin,
junior, started commercial life on the Hunter
and in 1864 was M.L.A. for West Maitland.
He sat until 1874, when he resigned to
become police-magistrate at Bathurst. Later
he was at the Water Police Court, Sydney,
till Dick O’Connor, then Minister for Jus-
tice, gave him a pension to get rid of his
increasing eccentricities. In Parliament he
never did anything to remember—except to
be one of the parties to an exciting scrap on
the floor of the House.
That eccentric citizen, Lord Clifford of
Chudleigh, Count of the Holy Roman Em-
pire, whom the Tasmanian elector firmly
and consistently turned down during his resi-
dence on the Speck, and who came in for
his title last year, recently unloaded a
sample of his eloquence before the House of
Lords. Judgment was delivered by Curzon,
leader of the shop: “I have seldom noticed
a wider gap between the subject on the
paper and the speech dealing with it.”

Thackeray’s Phil-Fogarty, of the Fighting
Onety-Oneth, has relatives in Australia.
Tom, otherwise Thomas B. Fogarty, a Mel-
bourne solicitor, has just enlisted, being the
fifth of the family to join the colors. Lieut.
A. C. was killed in Gallipoli; Joseph P., a
regimental medical officer, won the M.C. in
France and the undying gratitude of two
brigades for the manner in which, regardless
of self, he dressed their wounds; Lieut. Paul
is with the British Army ; and Jim is one of
our own boys in Frarfce.
Missing since the raid on the British lines
in the Belgian sand dunes on July 10, Cap.
tain H. K. Ward, R.A.M.C., son of the
finest set of whiskers in Australian journal-
ism. Six years ago Capt. Ward went to
Oxford as N. S. Wales Rhodes scholar, and
at the last moment displaced Keith Heritage
—afterwards killed in action in Franco after
SENATOR “PADDY” LYNCH (W.A.), one
of Australia's most earnest Conseriptionists.
he had won his three stars and Military
Cross—in the Olympic sculling contest at
Stockholm. Ward gained his commission at
Aldershot, and in 1916 acquired an M.C.
and a smashed collar-bone in France. His
grand old father, by the way, fought in the
Maori War before he put on a clerical collar.

The original Roseby, Thomas, a monumental

much astronomical wOtk; the Other, John,
now dead, followed bis father’s business in
GeOrge-stteet, and incidentally represented
Slioalhaven in the Leg. Assembly in 1877-
1882. The new Water Board secretary is
his son.
A hard-doer walked into Melbourne Town
Ilall recruiting depot the other day and
asked the M.O. to -give him the eyesight test
before submitting him to the physical one.
He got through on his right eye, and then
had a go with his left. “Bottom line?” asked
the M. 0., pointing to the chart. “Yes, sir—
APHLZ.” H’m. Now the second line—
backwards.” Then came disaster and the
discovery that the left eye was a glass one.
The lad was a returned Anzac anxious to
rejoin his pals. He had spent two days
learning the card by heart, like Admell
Drake, the hero of a similar tragedy in “The
Picaroons.” “Never mind,” he said, when
he was turned down. “I’ll give it a go some*
where else.”
Just died at Few (Vic.), old Matthew
Macalister, after whose father, Captain
Macalister the explorer, was named the rivet
on which stand the two Gippsland toWns of
Mafifra and Sale. Matthew, who was a life-
long friend of Allan McLean, once
of Australia, was a leading figure in the
stock and station trade. He and his wife
were called to England as witnesses in the
Tichborne case.

(B. 12/7/T7) reminds me of another man in
the same brigade who has been doing fast
time towards his field-marshal’s baton. He
is Corrigan, from the bush behind Zeehaii
(Tas.). Turned away from the first lot
owing to lack of previous military eXpei'l
ence, he got away with the original 15th
Battalion, and left Australia with them as
Corporal Corrigan, age 21. Some months
later, all that was left of the th Brigade
passed me on their way to the beach, Lem-
nos, and rest. With them, in charge of his
battalion, was Captain Corrigan. A few
months ago it was Major Corrigan who was
wounded in France.
Since the war began the N.S.W. Leg.
Council has lost by death no less than nine
members—lT. C. Dangar, Henry Gullett,
ITolborow, Kethel, Maclaurin, C. E. Pil-
cher, F. B. Suttor, W. J. Trickett and F.
E. Winchcombe. By the death of Mr. Dan-
gar, Dr. Creed and Henry Moses become
“Fathers” of the Council. They were
appointed on August 31, 1885. Dr. Mackel-
lar was appointed on the same day, but he
resigned in October, 1903, and stayed out-
side for a month.
THE YOUNG LIEUTENANT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The young lieutenant’s face was grey.
As came the day
The watchers saw it lifting white
And ghostlike from the pool of night.
His eyes were wide and strangely lit.
Each thought in that unhallowed pit:
“I, too, may seem like one who dies
With wide, set eyes.”
He stood so still we thought it death,
For through the breath
Of reeking shell we came, and fire,
To hell, unlit, of blood and mire.
Tranced in a chill delirium
We wondered, though our lips were dumb,
What precious thing his fingers pressed
Against his breast.
His left hand clutched so lovingly
What none might see.
All bloodless were his lips; beneath,
The straight, white, rigid clip of teeth.
His eyes turned to the distance dim ;
Our sleepless eyes were all on him.
He stirred; we aped a phantom cheer.
The hour was here!
The young lieutenant blew his call.
“God keep us all!”
He whispered softly. Out hq led;
And o’er the vale of twisted dead,
Close holding that dear thing, he went.
On through the storm we followed, bent
• To pelt of iron .and the rain
Of flame and pain.
His wan face like a lodestar glowed
Down that black road,
And deep among the torn and slam
We drove, and twenty times again
He squared us to the charging hordes.
His word was like a hundred swords.
And still a hand the treasure pressed
Against his breast.
Our gain we held. Up flamed the sun.
“The ridge is won,”
He calmly said, and, with a sigh,
“Thank God, a man is free to die!”
He laughed at this, and so he passed.
His secret prize we knew at last,
For through his hand the jewel’s red,
Fierce lustre bled.
VICTORIA.
EDWARD DYSON.

The loss ratio “is just over 10 p.c. lower
than the lowest previously experienced and
not much (if any) above half the average
normal loss ratio for the whole fire-insur-
ance business of the Dominion.” “It would
appear,” the general manager observes on
this, “that the war has had a marked influ-
ence on the destruction of property by firer*
I trust that this influence may lie perma-
nent.” The Bulletin doesn't quito know
whether to Say “Amen” to this pious remark
or not. On the whole the thing is a pretty
severe reflection on Maoriland. Two reflec-
tions, in fact. In the first place, Maoriland
is gorging on war money. Poor old J. Bull
is paying the Dominion millions for wool
and meat on the basis of war prices. Then,
though Maoriland is thus made fat, it has
the hardihood to tap J. Bull for the loan of
millions, for war purposes. Instead of bor-
rowing, in the circumstances Maoriland
ought to be lending. That is the first
reflection on Ward’s Dominion. The second
is that in the same Dominion things are, on
the average, only half as inflammable when
times are very good as when they are very
bad. That the general manager of the State
Fire Insurance Department should put this
on record surely calls for inquiry into the
crime-detection methods and machinery of
the islands.
i Of the past year’s revenue £77,609 was
the net premium income. The funds are
therefore an almost exact 200 p.c. of the
year’s premiums; and that is a position of
much greater strength than the average
front-rank British office discloses. But that
is, of course, only part of the strength of
this office; for behind every policy stands
the whole taxable wealth of the country and
the whole credit of the country as well.
Which ought to be good enough backing for
any policy-holder. The actual funds include
£65,254 in cash balances, £68,196 in Gov-
ernment securities, £25,991 in freeholds and
about £7OOO in mortgages and sundries.
Every penny of this has been accumulated
out of the profits of the business. Assuming
that £31,000 is required to re-insure current
risks, there is still left £125.060 to represent
the profits made out of 12 years’ trading on a
capital of £2OOO. For £2OOO was all that the
office ever asked for of the authorised
£100,0*10; and the trifle was paid back long
ago. Yet, though it is able to show this
amazing profit, the office insisted a year ago
that, as the result of its appearance on the
scene, rates had been so reduced that the
country had saved “considerably over
£2.000,000” in premiums in 11 years. But.
granting that the State office has done much,
whether it is wholly responsible for all this
is quite another question. As a matter of
fact, Maoriland had some very bad years
prior to 1904 and it has been having, on
the whole, a very prosperous time since;
and, as the general manager of the State
office points out, the calls on fire-insurance
offices in Maoriland (and other places too)
are governed largely by the circumstances
of the country —whether it is prosperous or
otherwise. It may be. quite true that had
fire offices charged M.L. the same, rates as
they had done previously, they would have
collected £2.000.000 more in 11 years. But it
is equally true that on what they did charge
previously, many offices showed a loss on
their M.L. business for,some years prior to
1905. From which the conclusion is that,
with a turn of the tide of prosperity in the
I >ominion, this office might feasily find that
its balance had slipped over to the wrong
side.

general-manager Ralston has been transfer-
ring £lO,OOO or more pgr half-year to his
formal reserve fund; which meant that there
was at least £10,00(1 le§S available for
repaying the old private 'depositors. This re-
payment is theHkey. to th& position so far as
dividends are concerned. The private (as
distinguished from the Government) deposits
were divided into two parcels when the boom
burst and there was obviously not enough to
pay everybody everything. One lot of 15s.
in the £ \yas represented by interminable in-
scribed stocks: there was supposed to be
enough assets tb represent, that much. The
other os. was supposed to be lost; it had
been advanced on boom values that had dis-
appeared. This ss. was therefore to be pay-
able only out of future profits, and no divi-
dend was to be paid to shareholders until it
was all wiped out.
The g.m., however, had ideas beyond the
payment of this five,bob. He settled dowh_
to build up the bank; wherefore he has been
hanging, on tqyprofits in order 1 to give him-
- Self amt an int'erual 'reserve,
Now, apparently, he reckons that enough has
-been dotre that-way-; at any rate, that it is
no longer necessary to appropriate any of
the disclosed profit. So from this onward,
it seems, unless something unforeseen hap-
pens, all the disclosed profit will be avaifi
able for clearing the way to dividends.

That is practically 17s. in the £ paid or pro-
vided for; and by the end ot next year the
shareholders may expect that the last hair of
the tail of the dead horse will have been
swept off the premises. And then-— —-
The capital is £480,000 in 160.000 shares
of £5, of which £2 is uncalled. The author-
ised capital is £2,000,000. In other words,
340,000 new shares may be issued. The
average profits in recent years have exceeded
£lOO,OOO p.a.; and the management is quite
satisfied that this does not over-state the
case. You get a straight tip to that effect
in the fact that, almost without a break,
each half-year’s disclosed profit is just a
little bit better than that of the one before.
Now, £lOO,OOO on a capital of £480,000 = just
over 20 p.c.; and no Australian bank with a
good knowledge of the way the cat jumps
will pay more than 10 p.c. nowadays. That
would absorb less than half the profits. The
question then arises whether the directors
should issue more capital, which would
strengthen the bank while incidentally ab-
sorbing more of the profits, or do the
strengthening by putting the whole of the
surplus profits to reserve, while leaving the
capital where it is. The capital should, in
this paper’s judgment, be increased. Apart
from the interminable inscribed stock
liability (£3,091,669), the Q.N. owes
£7,362,826: and capital and visible reserves
combined are only £702,000. English banks
certainly do have the use of £l2 or more of
the public’s money for every £1 of their
own ; but in Australia, where banking risks
are greater (because banking business is
different), 8 to 1 is reckoned a fair thing;
and an issue of £240.000 is fairly called for.
The 10 p.c. would then require £72.000 a
year; and on present appearances, as the
£240,000 of new capital would be earning
some of its own keep, the Q.N. could easily
do this. .... '* -
So far as eurfent business is concerned
the bank is well holding its own. Apai
from its large Government deposits it has
£5.644,389 of new private deposits, or nearly
half a million more than when the war
started. Advances are, however,
smaller (£0.310,393 as against £6.488.0nb
as at June, 1914). The money got m on
both sides has all gone to the Government
through one channel or another.

account, in the past year £4500 is carved out.
Apparently this bonus fund is for the benefit
of the staff —is. in short, in lieu of salaries
or wages ; wherefore the amounts that have
been set aside for the purpose have in the
table been deducted from profits. Even then
there is a profit much in excess of the divi-
"dend. The wide margin is partly due to the
fact that £86,509 of the capital calls for only
5 p.c. This, of course, is cumulative pref.
capital. Probably one or more of the old
interests in the business supplied this money.
That the return was fixed at 5 p.c. indicates
how solid a concern it was in the opinion of
those who knew most about the inside of it.
They evidently hold the same opinion still,
because, in spite of the change in the value
of money, recent quotations have been above
par,
It is only about a couple of years ago that
the co.’s stock was quoted on ’Change; and
at that time a balance-sheet was prepared to
exhibit the position as at April 30, 1915. The
reserves, as the table shows, were then a
very solid £81,924; and that was exclusive
of the bonus and provident fund before men-
tioned. But in a business of this kind visi-
ble reserves are not everything. Stock in
hand and in transit is valued in the accounts
at £229,402; and it is obvious that a tre-
mendous difference of opinion may exist as
to the fair value to put on paper and
stationery stock at a time like this. Stuff
that three years ago was worth £l2 per ton
is to-day worth, say, £3O. What ought that
stuff to be valued at? The usual formula is
“cost or under.” But how much under?
The very conservative manager puts it this
way. Stuff that cost us £l2 we have sold
for £3O. But we have in stock now stuff
that we bought at £3O, and may have to sell
for £l2. Therefore,, be will say, the probable
loss is likely to wipe out the apparent profit:
at any rate, he has to prepare for something
of the kind. Another manager may argue, with
a lot of forefe, that £3O is a fair value, and
that no provision need be made for losses on
realisation, because prices may be still
dearer, and that, anyway, they will not fall
suddenly. Where there are merchandise
stocks, and especially stocks whose price has
been largely affected by the war, it is, then,
obvious that the declared profit isn’t much to
go on in these times. The investor needs to
either get behind the counter and see actual
figures or find out the general reputation of
the management. What he will find out in
this case is that the last thing it is likely to
do is to forget to provide for contingencies.
There probably never has been a time in the
history of this trade in Australia when pro-
fits have been so large; and that E.D. and
Co. hasn’t shown more is unquestionably only
because it has been making internal provision
to protect itself against a possible but im-
probable sudden fall in the value of stock in
hand. If those contingencies don’t arise,
then of course the now undisclosed profit will
have to be shaken out some day. And the
day will probably come when, in consequence
of heavy taxation, a little bit up the sleeve
may be very handy indeed. Not so much
for this co. as for others which haven’t so
wide a margin between profit and dividend.
The co. has a capital of £254,231 in ordin-
aries and £86,509 in 5 p.c. cumulative prefs.
The reserves now exceed the pref. capital,
which means that the whole of the capital is
now available to earn dividends for the
ordinaries. The liabilities (trade creditors)
amount to £69,492. On the other side in
f ?. f , lc ij, t i°, r l. to . sto< ' k f: 229.402 and sundry debtors
£189,540. the co. has £80.598 in freehold and
£38,7 05 in cash and sundries.

Wentworth Hotel. Ltd. (Sydney), was
ormed in April, 1912, to purchase the free-
hold of a well-known city hotel, extend the
premises and carry on the business. For
goodwill (including license) £8750 was paid
to the vendor, and £lOBl went in preliminary
exes. Ihe co. thus started with £9831 of
unrepresented by tangible assets.

The bigger part of the trouble is that the
first-class hotel in Australia gives too much
for the money—or, rather, doesn’t get enough
money for what it gives. Actually the wages
and other exes, of running a hotel here are
greater than in London and almost as much
as in the average American city; but the
Australian tariff is only from one-lndf to
two-thirds the English or American for equal
accommodation and service. This co. had
either rotten luck or rotten judgment. Mr*.
Maclurcan had been making a very fair
thing out of the Wentworth for years—the
figures were in the prospectus. On the
strength of those figures the co. came in just
at the moment when exes, started to climb
up. hand over fist. On top of that fell the
war. which stopped the oversea traffic out
of which the Wentworth got nice pickings.
“Until after the war,” the directors say, “no
hope of reasonable profits can be held out.”
In this paper’s judgment it will take some-
thing more even than the stopping of the
war to produce reasonable profits.
One handicap is that the co. is, for its
size, pretty heavily loaded with liabilities:—

In’ effect the co. has about £21,500 of pref.
capital, on which it has to provide a divi-
dend. It has done this, and in three years
has, besides, reduced the liabilities by £8177.
Meantime, however, furniture, etc., have
been depreciated by £4164. Which means
that, taking the reduced value of furniture
into account, the co. lias fallen about £lOOO
short of making enough to pay interest on
its debts since the war started.

more than 6 p.c. for the next year or two.
Among its handicaps is the fact that it is
paying double income tax. "British and
colonial income tax and land and absentee
faxes” took £5916 last year, say 42 p.c. of its
income. This year, on an equal gross revenue,
it will probably be 50 p.c.
* * * *
G. D. Meudell writes from San Francisco:
. Moving amongst oil men one hears daily predic-
tions about the coming shortage of oil—of crude
petroleum and all its products, from petrol to lubri-
cating oils and tar. California is producing 40,000
barrels a day less than it uses, so the shortage is
1,200,000 barrels of 42 gallons each per month. In
10 months from now there will be no oil reserves
in California to draw upon to supply an increasing
demand. The oil-well people say they cannot in-
crease present supply because they have not the
proved oil lands to drill. The shortage of oil is so
acute that two of the great railroads which use oil
on their locos, are turning back to coal, perhaps
finely pulverised coal, the new fuel here. No
new drilling of wells is anywhere in progress except
on a limited area of proved land. The Federal autho-
rities withdrew large tracts from use on the plea
that the oil contained would be needed for the
American Navy. The Government has also been
suing for the return of certain oil areas, and litiga-
tion has frightened drillers. Petrol is certainly
only lOd. a gallon, and kerosene in bulk only
4sd. a gallon. But these prices may easily double, and,
if they do, so will the prices of petrol and kero-
sene sold retail in Australia. Therefore this ques-
tion of boring Australia thoroughly for oil becomes
one of grave national importance. Leading oil
operators, oil chemists and oil engineers, as well
as oil geologists, affirm emphatically that petroleum
ought to be found in Australia. There is nothing
against its existence geologically. Surely, then, the
opinions of three or four State geologists who have
never seen an oil field or a modern oil drill ought
not to be allowed to block the search.
And meantime what about the shale-oil
deposits in the N. S. Wales Blue Moun-
tains? They have not to be “proved.” They
are known to be not only the largest but the
richest in the world. It is a national dis-
grace that, practically, nothing is being done
with them.

Mount David (N.S.W.) is just about out
of wind and has consequently secured a three
months’ spell to discover whether it will re-
cover or snuff out. Its last div.—s p.c.—
was handed round in 1903. Only -Jd. per
share remains to be called up; but if the
show succeeds in raising a little more cash it
will resume activity in the lower levels.
Meanwhile there is nothing doing in shares
at 2d.

Bourke’s Hill Tin (N.S.W.) announces
a sixpenny after a three-years’ drought. The
last clean-up was 10 tons 16cwt. oxide for
five weeks, following 6 tons 19cwt. for four
weeks. There are 45,000 shares, so the six-
penny will cost £1125. The troubles have
been increasing costs, the slump in the metal
market at the end of 1914, and scarce labor.
At the very moment when the N.S.W. Gov-
ernment was spending public money in build-
ing monkey-cages to keep men employed,
this co. had one of its plants shut down for
want of workers. But of course you can’t
get to a picture-show every evening at
Bourke’s Hill.
Mahon, lately Prime Minister Hughes’s
colleague, but always Hughes’s mistruster,
lias, so it is said, been put into a comfort-
ably-lined job—that of administrative officer
of a new Mine Producers’ Assoc. What its
job is to be isn’t clear. It can’t be to fight
the Ministry to get things on behalf of the
big mine producers; for the big mine pro-
ducers are also the smelters, and even smelt-
ers, whose appetites are famous the world
over, couldn’t reasonably ask for more than
has been thrown at them. Who is the Mine
Producers’ Assoc.—or whatever its name
may be?
From Westonia :—•
Edna May’s financial position is not so good as
might appear from the accounts made up to April 30
last. The profit on working account is given as
£47,582; but as £2500 was spent on the main
shaft during the half-year and included in the bal-
ance-sheet as an asset, the actual profit on working
account is clearly £ 2500 less. Incidentally, assets
oieated out of profits, such as the recent additions to
this main shaft, are liable for income-tax. But if the
expenditure is charged direct to working account it
is not taxable; and the compiler of Edna May
accounts might bear this in mind in future. After
abowing for bank debit and credit balances, sundry
debtors and creditors, and manager’s cash account
and gold in transit, the co. had on May 1 approxi-
mately £4830 (less say £3750 for Federal and
State taxation) with which to meet the May dividend.
A noteworthy feature is the value of stores on hand
£5711.

Langi Logan South has a report from
George Bryant that the move to stop calls and
shut down on development won’t work. So
Bryant has to recommend rushing into
development and calls, or else (though he
doesn’t say this) shutting-up shop. On what
is ahead he writes: —
Since entering wash the number of fathoms
obtained from the different wash drives and extrac-
tion works total 2636, which yielded 26460 z. of gold,
average loz. per fathom. I have therefore no hesita-
tion in saying that with a continuation of present
values you have a profitable mine, which when
thoroughly opened up, drained and worked on system-
atic lines, with a full staff, will return fully 20 p.c.
profit.
Nicely put! First prove you have ounce
ground, next drain it, thirdly work it on
systematic lines, and, lastly, get a full staff.
Then 20 p.c. profit. But 20 p.c. on what?
On the loz. per fathom got when everything
is 0.K., or 20 p.c. profit on the expenditure to
that date. Whatever it is it all depends on
the “continuation of loz. ground.” That has
to be proved ; and 2600 fathoms tested from
a gutter 600 or 700 feet wide is a mere speck
on a mountain.
* * * #
Mining for magnesite has begun in Vic.
in a small way. The mine or quarry is at
North Redesdale, north-east of Kyneton; and
the possibilities of the business have made
Minister of Lands Lawson sit up, Redesdale
being in his electorate. He has arranged
for a departmental mining-expert to inspect.
Last year (1916) Cloncurry produced
10,908 tons copper valued at £1,149,328.
This great mineral region is developing
splendidly. Most important changes have
lately taken place in the Hampden and Mt.
Elliott’s Hampden Consols, where extensive
basic ore has been proved in the two adjoin-
ing leases at 500 ft. and 480 ft. In the Con-
sols the ore has been developed northwards
towards the boundary of the Hampden, and
a crosscut in one place to the hanging wall
discloses a splendid make. The stope over
this crosscut shows 50ft. of good stuff in one
place.
The chief point of interest at Mt. Morgan
(Q.) at present is the sintering of the con-
centrates. Recent experiments with the
Dwight-Lloyd mechanical sinterer are favor-
able, in spite of gloomy prophecies.
The establishment of electrolytic smelting
works in Hobart by De Bavays. which is
already producing metallic zinc from Broken
Hill concentrates, and the fact that the Read-
Rosebery co. will shortly erect a plant at
Zeehan for the treatment of local ores, has
stimulated interest in zinc properties on the
west coast of the Speck. There are many
large sulphide and zinc-blende lodes that
were useless under old conditions; but now
that cheap power has encouraged the intro-
duction of electrolytic methods, many of these
lodes might be profitably worked. The Gil-
lies process, claimed by its always-optimistic
backers to be far better than the Anaconda,
which will be used by the Rosebery-Read and
L>e Bavays, should shortly be started at
North-West Bay, near Hobart. This co. has
lately taken over the old Jupiter mine, near
Rosebery, one of the richest sulphide bodies
on the Read-Rosebery line. The mine has
already been fairly well opened up and
formerly supplied large orders to the Tas-
manian Smelting Co. at Zeehan, when that
German institution was running. De Bavays
have also taken options over zinc proper-
ties at Zeehan, although they originally pur-
posed dealing with Broken Hill concentrates
only, and met with some opposition in con-
sequence. But it won’t be a good thing if
this crowd gets control of many mines.
There is plenty of ore in Tasmania to keep a
custom smelter going, and it will be far bet-
ter for the producer to have a choice of
buyefs.

Mount Lyell, which is fathering the co. to
work the Hercules-Primrose group, expects
to get power from the King River. Surveys'
have shown that at least 20,000h.p. can be
harnessed up in the gorges of the King, and
the Government will this session ask
authority to carry out the work.
* * * *
The boom in iron is making the Speck
scratch its head and wonder why it didn’t get
busy in time ; A good start was made with
smelters at York Town, on the lower Tamar,
back in the 70’s. These turned out about
20,000 tons of iron which sold very well in
Victoria and elsewhere. Then they began to
find that there was too much chrome in the
ore and the industry died. There are mil-
lions of tons of ore thereabouts still. More
recently about 40,000 tons running 66 or 68
per cent, of metallic iron was shipped from
Penguin, on the NAV. Coast; but that busi-
ness dropped off, too, and Tassy’s iron is still
waiting for someone to come and take hold of
it.
* * * •
As a sort of send-off to its managing direc-
tor, Malcolm Newman, who, having returned
from Malaya, has gone into the Engineers’
camp in Sydney, Larut Tin Dredging. Ltd.,
publishes a report covering his last 12
months’ work. To the end of May 478.000
cub. yds. had given 228 tons oxide = 1.0711 b.
per yard. A first div. of Is. 6d. was paid on
April 30. Newman, in a very informative
report, remarks that he is “no great believer
in the fetich of costs per yard, believing that
low costs do not necessarily mean maximum
profits.’ He is known to be dissatisfied with
Malayan recoveries in general, and has
aimed at improving them. On an average
of 85,000 yds. per month he estimates the
Larut outlay in Malaya at 2£d. to 3d. But
some costs are beyond their control. “The
Malayan Government lately considerably in-
creased the royalty on tin, the increase being
for patriotic purposes for the duration of
the war. We are paying £BOO to £9OO per
month in taxation as‘royalty to the Govern-
ment.” The smelting cos. have also put up
rates: “We pay more than half as much
again for the smelting and realisation of
our concentrates.” Labor (Chinese) troubles
are likewise becoming more frequent, “and
demands for increased wages., are by no
means uncommon.” Also “conflicts between
the rubber-planting and dredging interests
seem to be unavoidable” ; but, being a mining
man, Newman naturally hopes that “the fact
that even a one-dredge mine like Larut con-
tributes so largely to the tax collections will
not be forgotten.”
**• * •
Another effort is to be made to work the
terraces and flats on thie Yodda goldfield
(Papua). The latest scheme is to instal
elevators and sluice the gjround, which to a
man who has been over jthe field seems a
sensible plan. The old Effort. was to tote
several hundred tons olf * in-
cluding a boiler that scaled Jive tons, over
the track that leads for its first five miles
through the swamps behind Buna Bay, and
even if they had shifted the bulky machinery
through the 50-odd miles of flat country
there would have been the Jump Up to nego-
tiate—a range of mountain over 1600 ft.
high. Water-power is assured by several
permanent creeks, a large area is available
for treatment, and the gold is known to be
there.
Westraha employed 10,903 in its mining
industry last year, or about 1600 less than
in the year before. Gold mining absorbed
9824, of which East Coolgardie (Kalgoorlie)
found work for nearly one-half. Murchison
headed the other fields with 1125, and Gas-
coyne was whipper-in with 6. The remain-
ing 1079 men were engaged in winning min-
erals other than gold, and of these tin
employed 235, copper 113, lead ore 244, coal
458 and miscellaneous 29.
Last year the Westralian mining industry
“accidentally killed” 22 men and injured 874
more. About half of the fatalities occurred
at Kalgoorlie and Boulder. Falls of eartn
and miscellaneous tumbles accounted for
most of tne victims. No estimate is given
of hbw many were stricken with miners'
complaint or how many funerals it brought
about. No estimate ever is given.
