Sydney, July 23, 1917.
My Dead Alum, —
Chief Justice Henry George Richards, of
the High Court of the North-western Pro-
vinces of India, who has been getting some
winter sun in Sydney, is the third genera-
tion of his family to sit on the bench. His
father was Judge of the County Court,
Mayo, Ireland, and his father, John
Richards, grandfather of the Indian Judge,
was one of the Judges of the Supi'eme Court
of Madras. He took that wig off in 1835 to
become Solicitor-General for Ireland. Later
he was Attorney-General, succeeding Sir
Michael O’Loghlen, father of the late Sir
Bryan, of "Melbourne. In fact, by a remark-
able coincidence, John Richards was the
immediate successor of Michael O’Loghlen
as Solicitor-General, Attorney-General and
Bai’on of the Exchequer. When Baron
Richards reached the bench his colleague
was Baron John Leslie Fostei’, who was
grandfather of oxir own late Justice “Water-
jug” Foster.
Lady Newdigate Newdegate, two young-
daughters, a mountain of baggage and soine
maids are expected here this week. They’ll
go overland to Melbourne —unless there’s a
revoke —and catch the first boat to Tassy.
Slim, boyish-looking Clive Miller, son of
Denison, of the Commonwealth Bank, has
beeix killed in France. Enlisting as a driver
he got away in Octobei’, 1914, when Aus-
tralia was so new to war that it hardly
knew how to order its campaign boots.
Having been through all that was best and
worst of the Peninsula, his eggshell-china
appearance when he got back to Sydney
marked him out as a fxxture non-combatant.
The A.I.F. declined him for further sendee;
but he presently went to England, got a com-
mission. married Miss Chubb (of the safes),
and put in about seven months with the
guns befoi'e his bullet found him.
Richard Binnie, who died the other day,
was a wealthy city man with big interests in
N. S. Wales station property. His home,
Linlithgow, on the brow of Point Pipei’, was
one of the most picturesque of Sydney’s man-
sions, with a view that seemed to pierce the
sunrise on the Pacific blue. The handsome
widow was a Tasmanian girl, Miss Toby,
and a nurse by profession.
Dr. Gilruth, reappointed Administrator of
the Northern Territory, was in Sydney last
week saying good-bye; by this time he is
sailing past the coral reefs. He isn’t “His
Excellency” any more; but it wasn’t a
wrench for the long horse-doctor to shed that
superfluity. Mrs. Gilruth has been living
at Darwin while successive Governments
have kept her big Scotch husband hanging
about on departmental doormats down south.
Stella Power, the Melbourne girl who has
been under Melba’s wing at the Conserva-
torium in the southern city, trills like a
thrush. She sang at the Madrigal Society’s
concert (Arundel Orchard in charge) and
furnished amazing surges of music. The
voice is pure, and light with the lightness of
thistledown. It seems to blow like a puff of
melody xxp and down the vocal complications
of “Bel Raggio.” After the war Stella is
likely to do something ti’emendous. I don’t
know what, but certainly tremendous.
One of the season’s prettiest brides, Enid
Lloyd, stepped up St. Philip’s on her
father's ai’m the other evening. She is a
fair-haired ci’eam-and-roses sort of girl, and
looked like a lily in her foamy white drapery.
No wonder it was a vei’y proud young man,
Lieut. Rollo Williams, who produced the
ring! Rollo is the stepson of Dr. Creed.
M.L.C., and, as I told you, was so damaged
on Gallipoli that a home-defence job seemed
to be his future lot. But he is a sound man
again, and off to the Front shortly. Brides-
maids Dorothy Lloyd and Phyllis Blaxland,
in apricot finery with black-velvet millinery,
were supported by two youthful soldiers.
Lieuts. Springhalt and Little. A strip of
purple silk on the ’groomsman’s khaki repre-
sented the Military Cross. Yet he looked
like a boyish cadet playing at soldiers in the
winter holidays. After church the party
walked across to Petty’s. Colonel and Mrs.
Bob Owen came from Bowral for the event;
and they and Commander Dunne, R.N., and
his nice-looking lady, the “Sapper” Lees and
other old fi’iends crowded l’ound to wish the
hei’oine good luck.
Mrs. Hope Osborne, the handsome widow
of this State, is well on her way to Monte
Video to marry the gallant captain who has
a job in the embassy there.
Captain Glossop, who whacked the Em-
den, had to reply at a civic lunch to the
toast of his health —which appears to be ot
the best —as proposed by Acting-Premier
Fuller the other noon. A slender, fresh-
complexioned sailor, he nervously cleared
his throat, shifted his feet, and anchored
himself to the tablecloth before he got into
action. Then he covered the Australian
navy with glory, and (incidentally) heaped
coals of fii’e on the anti-Australian jingo
who once spoke with scorn of a “tinpot”
fleet.
This mid-week Gwen Hanbury Davies
marries Firleigh Greig, whose headquarters
are Bombay. The busy father —a great Red
Cross worker —will make time to give his
girl away at St. Mark’s.
Brenda Walkeden, who is now a Canai’y
Cottager under the name of Brenda Hope, is
the pretty daughter of a strikingly handsome
mother, who used to be Clara Cunningham.
Three of the Cunningham brothers are the
beauteous Roland of “Merry Widow” fame,
the tenor Robert, and the literary Di\ Ed-
ward, who prescribes for Melbourne Aegtjs.
Brenda owns a fine dramatic soprano voice.
Scene, a stationary hospital in Boulogne
(France). Time, about 8.45 p.m., just a
quarter of an hour before the youthful and
flirtatious V.A.D. was going off duty. She
was hanging l’ound the telephone evidently
expecting a ring. At 8.45 the bell tinkled.
But the matron happened to be passing at
the time and answered the ’phone in spite of
the V.A.D.’s offers. “Hullo, is that you,
dai’ling?” said a very military voice at the
other end. The matron looked round at the
uncomfortable V.A.D. “Probably not,” she
snapped, and put down the receiver.
Emma Franeeschi, the Lismore doctor’s
singing daughter, made her first slither in
grand opera last week. Santuzza, in “Caval-
leria,” is a slippery part for a debutante,
and the Italian temperament makes no
allowance for beginners. Thus it hap-
pened that Degradi, hurling her to the
ground, did it so heartily that the
novice—much lighter in weight than the
lady who usually takes the part —slid
along the stage towards the footlights. This
was her worst expei’ience. Except nervous-
ness. That horror she seemed to clutch with
a trembling hand gripping her bodice. As
an emotional actress Emma probably put up
a record for a first appearance. And she
sang sweetly enough, too, but for the imp
that caught her throat. , A crowded house
shouted its approval; and the stage was
abloom with flowers when the curtain rolled
up after the climax. Two white doves flut-
tered out of one posy, and with great com-
posure retired under a canopy of wattle.
This dark-eyed lady
is Mrs. C. J. Dennis,
who recently ac-
quired the Senti-
mental Bloke. She is
an attractive missus,
and the bard calls
her —no, not Doreen ;
Madge! After the
’mooning up North
the Dennis pair will
return to a Victorian
bush-home.
Rupert Furber, son
of that T. F. Furber
who carried out most
of the trigonomet-
rical survey of N. S.
Wales, has received
the D.S.O. He and

“Willy” Dunlop, one of the managing
principals of the Edwards-Dunlop firm,
leaves for England and khaki this week: he
intends to have the new suit on within a
fortnight of landing. One day last Week
half the “heads” of Sydney’s wholesale
houses had a long, long lunch with the ever-
smiling William P.; and that was only one
of half a dozen send-offs. For years and
years and years he was president of the
Tennis Assoc., and that body had to say he
was a jolly good fellow, too. And a fellow
with an amazing memory, by the way. If he
hadn’t been born to sell paper by the ton, he
might have developed his photographic brain
and been now doing a turn at the ’alls. He
goes via U.S.A., and Mrs. Willy goes with
him as far as Honolulu.
The War Trophies exhibition, with North
Sydney’s V.A.D.’s to pour out tea, stayed
open longer than it was first scheduled for.
One of the minor thrills was a German
moustache binder. Now we know how the
Kaiser gets his spiky insignia of Hate. He
puts it to bed o’ nights in a little curved cave
that is stuck on his snai’ling upper lip. The
ti*ophy, snatched from the war zone, was
actually used by a Hun officer the nig it
before a battle.
Mrs. R. R. Dight and daughter Roberta
ai’e in Chelsea- —home of many palettes.
Nearly every male member of the artist claxi
capable of active service is on one or other
of the Fronts. Mrs. John Walker, wite o
the Scotchbyterian parson now in Ballarat,
was a handsome Miss Dight in the long ago.
Bhe had five sons in khaki, but the number
is cut down to three, I fancy. The other
two have answered the last roll-call.
Here is a May
Moore impression of
the Sentimental
Bloke, now travel-
ling with his own
Doreen towards the
top of Australia. It’s
a lean, earnest coun-
tenance, with a pair
of honest grey eyes,
that C. J. Dennis
turns to a world
which throws him
encores every time.
He confesses that he
hoped to sell 500
copies of the Bloke,
and wanted to pub-
lish it by subscrip-
tion —the first resort
of the man who
under-estimates his stuff. But it didn’t
happen that way. Fifty thousand copies
have been passed round in two years, and
the prospects are that it will spread like
thistledowm when the world is less busy with
munitions.
One day last week the Palladium buzzed,
afternoon and evening, for the Returned
Blind Soldiers’ Fund. The carnival was
organised by Phyllis Hopwood-Foldi, who
sat near the throne (on w 7 hich Prof. Andy
Stuart held the sceptre) in tricorne mil-
linery and raven drapery. Mr. Rich having
introduced the king of the Medical School,
the latter took most people’s breath away by
giving 11 as the number of blinded soldiers
in Australia. This proportion of our men
who have lost their eyesight in the greatest
fight of all the ages is so small as to be
almost unbelievable. Yet Professor Andy
doesn’t make many errors in arithmetic.
The show itself was an amazing success, and
the public, with France’s Day just behind it,
surged round the raiders. Nellie Stewart
dressed a doll for the affair, Melba signed
her name to a picture, and Tempest
gave an exquisite little Louis XIV. fan.
The lady in chai’ge of this treasure hinted
darkly that, though a reserve of a mere £5O
was on the little ivory sticks, they hoped to
auction it for £lOO. George Marlow lent
his aid as treasurer and hon. overseer, and
R. Thomas was hon. sec.
Mrs. R. Windeyer and a couple of daugh-
ters send a cooee from the other side. They
are watching two fine young sprats—Cadet
C. R. Windeyer (now at Cambridge) and
his brother, who is at the Royal Naval Col-
lege, Dartmouth, in training to catch mac-
kerels.
R. W. Sandford, son of the old ironmaster
of Lithgow, has ascended to the height of
flight-commander. Seaplanes are his love.
The Anzac Buffet has received so many
testimonials that it is up to the Mamma
State to give Ada Reeve the £5OOO she’s
after. Victoria handed out that amount
quick and lively. N. S. Wales has been too
eager for France’s Day to do very much yet
for the “little bit of Australia” where our
men on leave in London get a good meal
for nothing, unless they choose to put some-
thing in the till as a gift. But part up now!
Ada will show you how to do it at her
Anzac matinee, Tivoli, on Friday, Aug. 3.
At the Consei-vatorium the stage was
abloom with flowers after pianist Winifred
Bui’ston’s recital. Beethoven, Brahms and
Cesar Franck were served up with such
relish that even the Highbrows were moved
to demand a second helping. Dymphna
Kirkaldie, an elegant yoxxng person who floats
into song (she had some lessons awhile ago
from Melba), was the Voice.
In the suburbs France’s Day is waning
towards twilight. Paddington had its fling
in a juvenile matinee ball on Saturday.
Consxxl Chayet handed over the prizes, and
mamma Brunton looked on while daughter
Dorothy axxd Mrs. H. D. Mclntosh, adjudi-
cators, l-etired exhausted from their labors.
It is no _picnic to hand the cake to the best
oxxt of 500 or 600 restless kiddies. But
there was one feature that left no
doubts. This was a fascinating little
maid, Baby Clonan, as a caged canai’y,
with little Ken Hogarth as the cat
in attendance —one of the best fancy-
dress efforts of the season. A special prize
went to the bird. Miss Rose Lyons, the hon.
organiser, will have a handsome cheque to
hand the French-Australian League.
A Mosman lad, Lieut. Dimond, of the
Field Artillery, is amongst the latest to win
the Military Cross. He spent a few days'
leave in London recently with his pre-war
“skipper,” Charles Lloyd Jones. The twain
were once shipwrecked together in Sydney
Harbor on the yacht Thelma.
The Engineers and Signallers’ committee
dames have a special attraction at the
Sports Ground on Saturday afternoon. Ada
Reeve kicks off at a football match, Engi-
neers v. Returned Soldiers.
Eadith Sullivan, the N.S.W. girl who
won the Woolley Scholarship (1917-20),
has settled down to work at the Royai
Academy. She’s attacking the British piano.
“Hop’s” impressionist portrait of George
Reid, done on the back of a wooden spoon,

dow this morning for one guinea. France’s
Day Fund benefits. Mr. D. K. Inglis held

The Cheer-oh Girls, in magpie rig out. did
their best’the other night for the Ist and
10th Field Artillery Christmas boxes. It is
a dreadful rush, for the word has gone forth
that the little luxuries must be ready for
transport early in August. Hence a
blizzard of “gift afternoons.” The Reper-
tory held a snug audience, and damsels in
check pinnies did a roaring business in
sweets. Dorrie Ward, the backbone of the
little company, is well supported by breezy
amateurs.
The Cherniavsky trio give four concerts
in the Conservatorium before packing for
the States. Business has been so good that
manager Scully—he is the man who toured
England as “Babs” with Brandon Thomas,
the parent of Charlie's Aunt —is steering
the extra appearances. ’Cellist Mischel
marries Mary Rogers, daughter of a Van-
couver sugar king, when he reaches Snow
Land.
St. Peter’s, in Woolloomooloo, an Angli-
can tabernacle obscured from public view by
much bricks and mortar, is celebrating its
jubilee. The foundation-stone was laid on
May 1, 1860, by Sir John Young, who was
afterwards Lord Lisgar. The space around
was then open, but by ill luck the frontages
to William and Bourke streets did not come
to the congregation, as was hoped. The
church was consecrated by Bishop Barker in
the presence of 24 clergymen, of whom Arch-
deacon Giinther, the ancient shepherd of
Parramatta, is the sole survivor.
Mrs. Vernon, lion. sec. of the Ist Light
Horse, is rounding up stragglers for a big
event at Aston, Bellevue Hill, July 28. The
4th D.A.C. are helping.
The North Sydney Comedy Club wants
your ear—just for a moment. Having
rushed into the fray for the Kangaroo it will
play “One Summer’s Day” at the Repertory
every evening from July 28 to August 4.
After looking into the pockets of some of
the new models I wonder who is going to
wear the skirts that are mere accessories.
Surely someone stout of heart and lean of
thigh. They are bags with a gaping mouth
that might be the entrance to a tunnel. Worn
on each hip, they are submerged towards the
base by flat box-pleats. No doubt there’ll
be something extra for the stuff they gobble
up.
Simple souls, blessedly innocent of snob-
bishness, are the Italian opera people. The
principals mostly hang out in the Darling-
lnirst penny-tram section. They go home
like a family party, he who is not a strap-
hanger with a great brown-paper bag under
one arm. Balboni travels o’ days with a big
parcel that you might suspect was the
week’s washing if you didn’t know 7 that it
was a whole eighteen-penn’orth of apples
and bananas.
Mrs. J. H. P. Murray and her daughter
are stuck fast in England. Hungry?
TTm-m well, sometimes.
Wonder if the plethora of fur coats will
be offered on the altar of some war-fund this
spring? They’d make a mountain of pelt if
the owners would give them up when the
warm weather comes. Think of the tons of
moth-balls all these beastie skins will take
to keep them intact! D’you remember the
leopard-spotted craze a few years ago? Then
the yellow horror fell into the dyer’s vat, and
turned out an unspotted bear or Russian fox.
Probably War Chest Day will collect both
leopard and stone marten. Joffre will catch
the dachshund later.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

MELBOURNE CHATTER
Melbourne, July 23, 1917.
My Dear Yarrie.—
Presbyterian-parson P. J. Murdoch, of
Camberwell, who has two sons in the firing-
line and one serving as war-correspondent, is
wearing a bright smile because his youngest,
Lieut. Alan, has won the M.C. The other
soldier-son, nuggety Ivon, is also a lieu-
tenant. Keith, the war-correspondent, rep-
resents the Melbourne Herald in England
and France.
Chemist Norton Grimwade is on his way
home from a visit to Switzerland, where a
flying son has been interned for many
months. The youngster had a bad time while
a prisoner in Germany because he gave the
Hun officers no satisfaction when they tried
to get the address of the British Fleet. “Go
out and find it.” replied young Grimwade.
And the Hun didn't like the nasty way he
said it.
The late General Bridges’ elder boy, Major
Noel, has just got a second “mention,” and
a signal from the Fog says that tall Lieut.
Moncton Bowes Kelly has also figured in
Hair’s budget “for devotion to duty and
gallantry.”
When the Ross Soden widow’s son Harry
got ashore from the torpedoed Ballarat
almost the first man he met was his medical
brother Jack. The overjoyed pair casually
turned into the village inn for a yarn and a
feed, and found themselves greeted with ring-
ing cheers of welcome by a party of khakied
diners. Whilst the brothers waited and
wondered, a Baillieu boy w’ho had spotted
them at the door and started the tumult
rose and beckoned them in.
In a letter home, Matron Ann Kidd Hart,
of the Royal Australian Nursing Service,
tells of her decoration by George V. with the
Royal Red Cross. She left with the first
nursing unit, and was in Egypt until last
January. Then she went to London, and
became matron at Cobham Hall, in Kent—
Lord Darnley’s convalescent home for Aus-
tralian officers. What most struck her at
Buck’n’ham was, not King or Queen, but
the wonderful collection of china.
It was socks last month. Now it is
“Tommy cookers.” The Sydney War Chest
having collected 126,000 pairs of soldiers’
socks, as a result of its appeal for 100,000,
Melbourne is hurriedly putting in the last
rows to make up its quota; and meantime
the L.M.P. League (on the same job as the
War Chest) has shaken out £lOOO towards
the £5OOO cooker-appeal made by the Com-
forts Funds reps, in Europe.
Justice Duffy’s family has sorrowfully gone
over to Sydney for a time. They are all
much cut up by the death of the brilliant
youngest son, a promising medico, who had
been rejected for active service. This bereave-
ment follows the death of the eldest son in
France. The only surviving boy is in the
firing-line.
There is no end of things the right kind
of woman can be queen of—in wartime. Up
at Porepunkah, on the roof of Victoria, a
Miss Bartlett has just been crowned “Queen
of the Dredges”—gold-dredges, of course. The
incidental competition found £6O for the
usual purpose.
Matron Turnbull, of the Hobart General
Hospital, has resigned, after 33 years’ con-
nection with the institution, to which she
came as nurse in ’B4. She was appointed
matron in ’96, and has held the position ever
since. The little lady, who belongs to a mili-
tary family, is something of a martinet, but
has done excellent work during her long
term of office. “Matron Turnbull’s nurses”
are famed throughout the States. She hands
the reins (still somewhat tangled by the
recent doctors’ strike) to Adelaide Gluyas,
who for the last two years has been matron
of the Broken Hill Hospital.
A smart team of visiting riflewomen from
Bananaland shot into Melbourne last week.
They are recognised as soldiers by the
Bananaland Government, and have their
official badges. Mostly they are earnest-
faced eitizenesses in business-like khaki
tunics, very short skirts and high leather
boots. Their hats are khaki and banded with
white puggarees. Mrs. Moore is captain.
She is a sonsy little lady, and one of the
leading recruiting agents in Brisbane. The
members of the team are crack shots, and
the Malvern Women’s Rifle Club is very
proud of having beaten them in a test match
by five points.
The Hoad dame gave a morning tea-party
at the Alexandra Club last week to the
Bananaland riflewomen. The guests after-
wards went on to afternoon-tea with Mrs.
Rogers Thompson at the Wattle, where
tables were set in the shape of a T for
sociability. The Forrest lady was there;
also Mrs. Polack, captainess of a local rifle
club. Mrs. Bowes Kelly had a northern
woman-soldier on either side, and Miss Holtz
talked scores over the buns. Miss Elliot,
secretary of the visiting team, was away at a
dentist’s. She is one of the crack shots of
the team, but nursed a toothache all the way
from home, and could not shoot. Dame
Forrest explained that she is commandante
of the women soldiers in Westralia, and that
her lot were the first to work the empty-
saddle idea, riding through Perth leading
spare mounts for recruits.
The Emmertons lately turned their place
upside-doWn for a big supper party to tenor
Lois. The abstemious young Italian warbled
time and time again, but when it came to a
matter of conversation passed his friends
along to little Mrs. Lois. “/S he spik !” said
the singer proudly to one and all. Mrs.
Emmerton showed off a Parisian gown of all-
black, and was eloquent about son-in-law
Norman Brookes, who was recently on a tor-
pedoed ship.
The sad little widow of Prout Webb is
home after a long sojourn in the Apple
Island with her daughter. They are work-
ing for the Red Cross.
Sunday night before last the Ross Soden
widow invited half a hundred guests, headed
by actor Cyril Maude, to.supper at Grong
Grong, followed by dancing. Lady Doris
had regretfully declined. Rumor says the
G.-G. and Lady Helen don’t approve of war-
time dancing in general and Sunday-night
two-stepping in particular.
On Thursday evening the William Dal-
rymples had an evening party, with Cyril
Maude and other theatrical stars to light the
conversational way. Mrs. Dalrymple wears
an anxious look now even at parties. _ Son
Sydney is flying for his country, and airmen
are having a hot time this European summer.
The W. J. T. Clarke girls gave a petti-
coated luncheon on Wednesday to welcome
home Noel, youngest of the Ken Brodribb
family. Three years ago Noel went off to
Shanghai for the usual orange-blossom cere-
mony with a young Scotsman named Stew-
art. This is the first trip home since the
wedding, and there is a baby Stewart to in-
troduce to everybody.
The autographed flag brought out from
England and France by mining-man Tewkes-
bury at the beginning of the war is to be
raffled at 2s. a member. The signatures of
Kitchener, Haig, Joffre and several of the
British Cabinet are on the banner, and you
can get the tickets on personal application to
Tewkesbury.
The doctor is the most frequent caller at
the Langlands Thomsons at present. Mrs.
Langlands T. was in a hansom that became
the target of a bolting lorry in the city last
week. She is on the retired list with a slight
concussion. And her husband, John Thom-
son, M.H.R., was lately made a casualty in
General Winter’s offensive.
The thumping of feet was heard again in
Toorak the other Wednesday night, when
Essa D’Ebro hostessed a two-step revel
which lasted until morning. Dancing men
being scarce —or shy—elderly married hacks
threw aside their matrimonial winkers and
waltzed the girls around.
Miss Adelaide’s collection :
The Victorian estate of the late John Robertson,
of Struan, in the South-east, has been sworn at close
on £94,000. A will made about a fortnight before
he died bequeathed £2OOO to various Narracoorte
institutions and legacies or annuities to a good many
of his employees. Which reminds me that over
£6OOO of the estate of the late William Parker, of
Mena Murtee station, who died of wounds in France
in February last, was in this State. He came to
Adelaide from Broken Hill to enlist, bringing several
of his men with him, and was wearing sergeant’s
stripes when he went west. The bulk of his property
was in N. S. Wales.
After all, Tommy Ryan is not going to bother
about seeing the session out before moving to Mel-
bourne. His vacant seat in the Assembly (Sturt)
will probably be contested by Lieut. H. S. Hudd,
who represented the district for a time in the Liberal
interest when it was part of Torrens. Anyhow, he
left his nomination behind him when he set off for
the Front.
Mrs. J. H. Smith, wife of a former Railway Com-
missioner of S.A., died in Melbourne this week. She
was in Fitzroy Gardens, unaccompanied, when she
collapsed owing to heart failure.
It has pelted for three days, with the result that
the Torrens is running a banker, almost up to the
roof of its bridges. Orchards and market gardens
along the course have been practically washed away,
the low-lying suburbs have been submerged, and for
a time the trains and trams to Henley Beach and
the Grange were stopped. Residents of those locali-
ties could either suffer temporary divorce from their
families or get the train to Glenelg or the Semaphore
and walk home along the beach. It is the record
flood for years, and so much debris was piled against
one of the bridges in Thebarton that it was closed
to traffic. The Western Suburbs kept an anxious eye
on the weir, but it stood the strain, though the over-
flow at its northern end scoured a good deal of the
earthwork away from the abutment. Nursery gardens
in the low-lying ground between the city and Henley
Beach fared badly, and one heart-broken man puts
his loss down at anything from £lOOO to £l5OO.
The only comfort we had came from the Meteoro-
logical Bureau. It informed us that we had had “by
no means exceptional rains” !
The inter-collegiate football match at the Oval on
Saturday resulted in a win for Prince Alfred’s, who
scored 14 goals 16 behinds to St. Peter’s 4 goals 6
behinds. The Governor enjoyed the game and handed
the cup to H. G. Prest, captain of the winning team.
Director Bailey is getting to work promptly at
the Botanic Gardens, where shrubs are to be thinned
out, dead and badly-placed trees removed, and the
area of asphalt paths reduced. Best of all a com-
prehensive Australian section is to be formed in the
eastern part of the grounds.
Some of our fellows can’t wait to get to the 1 rout
for decorations. Sergt. Whitlock Jones is one of
them. A few days before he left in Oct., 1914, he
dived from the Glenelg jetty, in full kit, and fished
out a man and a boy from the hungry gulf. The
Royal Humane Society’s certificate is now waiting
for him. Another customer of the society is W illiam
Wennerborn, a railway porter, who snatched a man
from the very hoofs of a galloping train. Bronze
medal for him.
The Nursery-Rhyme and Fairy-Tale dance
for children (proceeds to the Red and Purple
Crosses) was a confused success. Miss
Montgomery and her sister took the roomy
St. Ivilda Town Hall, and even then had to
stop the sale of invitation tickets a week
before the event. The Very Naicest children
attended, and all were dressed magnificently.
Mrs. Clive Baillieu came in a powdered wig
and a blue gown, with Jean and Jeannot.
Mrs. Eddy Manifold had her two boys up
from Geelong Grammar. Mrs. Henry Holden
brought daughter Molly, dressed as Oranges
and Lemons, with the Bells of St. Clement’s
round her neck, the famous Four Farthings
in her hand and the Chip-Chopper dangling
from her waist. Dr. Ewing’s four-year-old
was a Red Indian. Mrs. Geoff Syme was
eclipsed for once by her eldest daughter in
a ballet dress and curls. Mrs. Eric Con-
nolly’s three-year-old stayed long enough out
of bed to win a prize. She was chief in
the parade in a fluff of white tulle and a
wreath of roses. Sister Iris copied a D.
Brunton dress of silver and turquoise. Con-
sul Eles’s bright little flapper came as her-
self.
Grown-ups were supplied with tickets to
come and amuse the children. But a lot of
them came to amuse themselves, and, as soon
as they could, they hustled the little people
aside and took the floor. The poor young-
sters dodged under their elbows .and some-
how escaped being trampled on; but their
dainty clothes were torn about and their
pretty banners broken. It made me think of
elephants breaking up a fairy revel. Miss
Montgomery, in a powdered wig and a tur-
quoise velvet frock, bravely tried to clear
the corridors; but it was a job for a prize-
fighter, with a megaphone and several burly
assistants.
Lesley Madden came, also Mrs. Woolcott
and mining-man Tewkesbury and his
daughter. The indefatigable Timmins was
there, and Olive Godwin arrived with a big
bunch of grown-ups. Cyril Maude looked in
after theatre hours and recited some nursery
rhymes to the children —all that were left
of them.
The Lois concert happens on Wednesday,
August 15, in the Auditorium. The Dalrym-
ples, Emmertons, Pohlmans and Hidgecocks
are making the tickets fly, and Lee, of the
Allan Wilkie co., is managing.
A Melbourne University concert, with
Laver at the wheel, is going to beat the
unlucky number on its saddle-cloth by issu-
ing its “invitations” two months ahead, and
so catching the early bird. It is the thir-
teenth of its series, and is booked for Melba
Hall on September 12.
Singer Evelyn Scotney arrived with the
usual mass of clothes for her Australian in-
vasion ; but the poor lady was so wretchedly
seasick when she landed that any parade
was postponed until her first appearance on
Saturday. Also welcoming tea-parties and
talk were off until she could rest and forget
the wintry sea.
Betty Paterson makes her well-prepared
d6but as a singer on Thursday, and I’ll tell
you all about it next week. Vice-regals,
mayors, knights’ dames, brave men-at-arms,
the V.A.S. and the Caledonian Society are
all interested, so it should go with a rush.
Betty is relying on her voice, though the
gifted young person could fiddle her way to
fame if she liked.
The free Saturday-morning snacks offered
by the Uni. Conservatorium are never
rushed, though the fai*e is good and sound.
One reason may be that hardly anybody
knows about the arrangement; another, that
Melbourne is mostly busy on Saturday morn-
ings earning a dollar or two to burst up on
a “musical” comedy in the evening. Still,
for those who want it, the spread is there
every week, with capable, mostly-Australian
musicians to hand around the tack.
Coombe Cottage, where Melba spends her
leisure in the midst of the country where she
also spent her youth, was not the lady’s
first choice. When she decided to settle in
Australia, her agents made an attempt to
secure Killara, the Herbert Syme estate far-
ther up the Yarra valley. This was at one
time owned by the late David Mitchell, and
over its rich acres Melba rode and shot
rabbits as a girl. But a cash offer of £60,000
failed to get the property, whereupon Melba
fell back on the lovely acres she now owns—
though not so lovely when their new pro-
prietor first took charge.
The Saturday Pops are minting money at
the Assembly Hall. Gertrude Johnstone
was Saturday’s star. She is a local girl with
a fine voice, and she arrived on the platform
in an old-fashioned muslin gown all frilled
and furbelowed—cut square at the neck and
frilled at the elbow sleeves.
Evelyn Scotney got a good house on Satur-
day at the Auditorium. She has so many
clothes for the distraction of the fluffy-
minded that she has pledged herself not to
wear the same frock twice. Saturday’s gown
was made of oceans of lily-white tulle. It
frilled to her knees and then cascaded
afresh for a few inches. It ended there in
front, but astern there were two tulle trains
—one to starboard and the other to port
It took some careful navigation to avoid
treading on them, and the wearer wasn't
successful every time. That dream will g 0
into dock before it is worn again. Evelyn
topped the gown with her ash-blonde hair
arranged in a peak, and she carried a huge
scarlet feather fan. Then she stood on a
scarlet carpet in white satin shoes, and so
had the colorists and dress critics exclaim-
ing before she loosed the first note.
Private George Cowley, a Melbourne bari-
tone w 7 ho chucked music for war two years
ago, has been singing in England and France
at hospital concerts. He gave a programme
at the ugly Brighton Pavilion, and the press
spouted ink in praise Of him and Mabel
Downs, the Australian composer of some of
the songs. Captain Cahill (Bananaland) is
another Anzac who has been singing himself
into musical despatches.
Florrie Young lately provided a mild,
afternoon-tea spree for her faithful gallery
girls. About 40 of the Permanent First-
nighters’ Club sat down to afternoon chatter
and told Flo how they loved her. They have
a regular campaigning kit of camp seats and
compact tea-hampers to sustain them during
the long waits. The poor devotees are gener-
ally so overjoyed when the performance
actually shows signs of beginning that the
earliest orchestral player is welcomed with a
cheer. They are extremely valuable to
people who live on applause.
Young billiardist Grey has arrived back
from New York with his bride —Miss
Sheehan of not so long ago. The buxom,
dressy young lady landed a pyramid of
trunks full of clothes to last her till next
trip.
The peaceful Arts and Crafts are still
battling against the war waves. They pro-
pose to hold a big exhibition of objects of
household use made of the baser metals.
Some will be antique examples. The show
will open on Friday, September 14, and con-
tinue until September 28. Entries must be
in by August 25, and the society decrees that
no exhibit will be accepted that has been
shown at a previous A. and C. general exhibi-
tion. Or in a shop.
Dorothy Meeson Coates, our clever minia-
ture artist, is working at the Anzac Buffet
in London, while George helps at hospital
work with Streeton. The delicacy of their
painter-hands and their artists’ knowledge
of anatomy make them invaluable in wound-
dressing. George Coates painted a picture
for the last Academy that created a sensa-
tion. It showed an Anzac in war-worn
clothes and a battle glory in his wide Aus-
tralian eyes.
The Austin Hospital for Incurables —the
sad place at Heidelberg which holds more
cheerful patients than almost any other place
I know —is to have an additional pavilion for
wards. The donor is Henry Meeks, hard-
ware merchant, brother of Alfred, of Sydney,
and the foundation-stone is to be laid at 3.30
on Saturday, July 28.
At Flinders-street station last week a
well-known Toorakian bachelor tried to help
a young mother and her baby into an Essen-
don train just as it was leaving the platform.
He got the infant all right; but the railway
porter sternly commanded the mother to
“Stand back there,” with the result that the
bachelor went on with the baby to Spencer-
street. where he got out and blushed miser-
ably till the next train brought mother along.
Yours sincerely,
JOHANNA.
Any Billjim know anything about the

via Ivempsey (N.S.W.).
“Rio” ; Why are we so squeamish on the
question of reprisals for air-raids? For half
a century at least the murders of traders
and missionaries in the Islands have been
“avenged” by British warships shelling and
burning villages. As a result women and
children in no wise responsible for the mur-
ders suffered. Yet we are told that
the same principle applied to Germany will
be “un-British and un-Christian.” There
may he good reasons against reprisals, but
don’t let us be hypocrites about it.

ABORIGIN
ALITIES
“The Amateur Birdologist”: The “falcon
shrike,’’ alluded to in Bulletin 21/6/’l<, is a P"
parently the same as the crested shrike-tit, one of
the most remarkable of Australian birds. Though
no bigger in the body than a sparrow, it has a bill
as heavy and strong as that of a butcher-bird -a
fact that old John Gould abruptly discovered 50
years ago when a wounded bird, fastened affection-
ately to his hand. In the big-timber country the
presence of this hefty parishioner may be detected
by its strenuous belting at bark in pursuit of stay-
at-home insects; accordingly, most youngsters know
it as the bark-tit. The nest is one of the most
beautifully constructed of the world’s bird-homes.
Closely matted of fine yellowish bark ripped from
green trees, it is soft, silky and wonderfully
modelled for a bird with such a heavy beak. The
fact that the cosy dwelling is invariably placed at
the top of a tall gum accounts for Gould’s failure
to find a sample. There are only three members in
the tribe, the second being the white-fronted shrike-
tit of Westralia, and the third the yellow shrike-tit,
a recent discovery in the far Nor’west of the con-
tinent. They differ only in color.
‘E.S.S.” : Many country people make pets
of snakes,, mainly harmless pythons such as the
carpet and diamond, either of which can beat any
cat in keeping a place clear of mice and rats. They
don’t get in anybody’s way intentionally, but they
have an unpleasant habit of exploring the premises
at night, and for that reason they are usually kept
in the shed after hours. A station-master and his wife
had just gone to bed when a blood curdling yell from
underneath frightened the lady into a faint. The
next moment out scrambled a total stranger, who
fought, jumped, kicked and performed various frantic
contortions before bolting into the outer darkness,
leaving a big snake behind him wondering what all
the excitement was about. The burglar had hidden
under the bed to wait until the inmates of the
house should be asleep, and all that the tame reptile
wanted to do was to climb up the leg of his
and tickle his fifth rib.

“Wart”: “Nick Bottom” nominates the
magpie to outsing “E.S.S.’s” butcher-bird. Let
me nominate the grey-tailed thickhead (lately pro-
vided with the more appropriate name of Australian
whistler) to outdo the best efforts of either. The
other morning four grey-tails were challenging each
other from the tops of neighboring trees, while a
shrike-thrush filled in occasional pauses. Call
answered call, trill answered trill, and gurgle
answered gurgle, while every now and again the
shrike-thrush remarked that he was “pretty, pretty
Dick,” or did a cadenza on his own account which
rivalled the grey-tails’ efforts. The concert lasted
for two hours, with hardly a pause. In my opinion
there is no bird with notes to equal the flute-like
melody of either grey-tail or shrike-thrush, especi-
ally at this time of the year. I admit that the
magpie more often gives choral performances, but
just at this season it is quite usual to find anything
up to half a dozen grey-tails challenging each other
to charm an unobtrusive lady who acts as judge of
the competition.
* * * *
“Tropicus”: The islanders of Torres
Strait are great on churches, and to build a tin
bethel larger than the other speck’s is the ambition
of every Binghi. Before that ambition stirs in
them the young bucks pass their days in glorious
idleness, swimming in the warm waters off the
snow-white beaches, lazily sailing about the smooth
blue-satin plain of the sea, and canoodling with
velvet-skinned brown maidens in brilliant moonlight
beneath silvery rustling palms. But when the call
comes the young men leave all these things behind
and go to work for 1.2 months in the shelling boats
of Thursday Island for from £1 to £2 a month,
including food and clothing. All through the year
Biown Brother sweats in the burning sun combing
the reefs. And when his time is up he takes his
pay and goes back to his island to put most of it
in the collection-plate to buy a new church or
additions to the old one. There is an occasional
gleam of humor in the business, when the congrega-
tion falls out with the missionary and lets the church
to some mercenary trader as a store for his copra,
as happened on one occasion, to the unholy joy of
the wicked white men. On one island a great tur-
moil arose when a prowling brown deacon caught a
canoodling couple on the porch of the church. The
whole island was in an uproar, and there was talk of
capital punishment for the unluckv couple But the
elders and deacons held a council and made the pun-
ishment worthy of the offence. The boy was caught
and half of his head shaved to the scalp, and the
same torture was inflicted upon the girl The
penitential shaving was done with bits of glass from
the trader's dead-marine heap. For weeks after the
unhappy twain were the butt for all the abuse the
village could pour out upon them, and they had a
miserable existence until their hair grew again.
“Jacaranda”: Like to try a Hunnish dish
from the ex-German Pacific Islands! A variety of
pigeon which is plentiful there has a very fleshy t
breast. Ordinary people slice this meat, fry it with
onions, and in a land where fresh meat is scarce it
makes a good substitute for steak and onions. But
the local Huns have a different way. By them the
breast meat is put through the sausage-machine raw
and piled on a dinner-plate in a circle ; a couple of
raw eggs are broken into the middle of the circle, a
fringe of sliced raw onions outside it, and it is ready
for table. The diners shovel the raw mess down
their throats and then ask fondly for pig’s-blood
sausages.
* * * *
“Coffin”: I was distributing softgoods,
hardware, tobacco, etc., to people scattered in the
Gippsland wilds wh n I took the wrong turning the
other evening, and found myself hopelessly en-
tangled in the wilds. I had determined to camp
for the night, when a faint glow off to the left
attracted me. I stuck my lamp on the roof of the
waggon as a harbor light, and struck out for the
luminous spot in the great waste. As I drew
nearer I caught the strains of music, and presently
came upon a cleared space where a camp fire was
burning briskly, rug and bluey were spread for
bed, and a small, be-whiskered busnman in
a shirt and underpants was dancing vigorously
to the tune provided by a wheezing phonograph on
a stump. The bushman danced a reel on the grass.
Then he reset his phonograph and danced a jig.
He was involved in a waltz when I screwed up
courage and broke in on the revelry. His Whis-
kers merely explained: “A bloke on his own has to
do somethin’ t’ pass the evenin’s.” Before we
parted he asked me politely: “Howja like t’ jine me
in a polker?” We polkaed sedately for five min-
utes, and parted on excellent terms.
“Hay”: B. E. Minns draws Bingki’s feet
exactly as they look, with a wide spread between the
fiist and second toes. This is caused by our heathen
brother s habit of picking up all sorts of articles
from the ground without stooping to do it. He
can lift anything in this way from a small grub to
a ten-foot spear and pass it to his hand without
varying his stride or bending his head. Mrs. Binghi,
when coming into camp, picks up the firewood with
her toes, deftly catching the sticks with her hand,
and lays them crossways in the net slung on her
back, until she acquires an immense load of timber.
I remember an old Central Aus. buck in the
McDonnell Ranges who owned a pair of feet that no
.°° ever ma de would fit. The soles of the extremi-
ics were quarter of an inch deep, and the proprietor
could have walked over broken bottles with perfect
safety. *

BEAU BREECHES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ben Brewster lived out Redgum way,
Where all the chicken-farmers dwell.
He had few hens, yet strange to say
Had always many eggs to sell.
But Mr. Brewster
Had a rooster
Whose lay produced a wondrous lot.
Not black leghorn nor wyandotte
Considered as an egg-producer
Could come up to that gay seducer.
Ben Brewster never breathed a word
Of Breeches’ special arts and graces;
Of that superb and guileful bird
He tried to cover up the traces.
Yet Mr. Brewster
Groomed his rooster,
And prinked and polished up the Beau
Until he made a wondrous show —
A bird of breeding, and a whopper,
All black and gold and polished copper.
Beau Breeches would with subtle craft
The laying hens inveigle nigh,
For every little hen went daft
If Breeches merely winked an eye.
All day the rooster,
Worked for Brewster,
Cajoling hens to come and lay
Around his barn and in his hay.
If Breeches was at all attentive
That was a good enough incentive.
At Matty Foley’s fence he’d speak
A loving word, and shake his comb ;
The hens with an ecstatic squeak
Would up and follow Breeches home;
And then when Brewster
Heard his rooster
Emit a crow he’d stir his pegs
And gather in a dozen eggs.
He levied, too, on Green and Nation,
So great was Breeches’ fascination.
But once Mat Foley called to say,
With hate and irony intense:
“How much are Foley’s eggs to-day ?”
Said Brewster lightly: “Eighteen pence.”
Mat battered Brewster,
Slew his rooster.
Now Benny wants the old age pension,
And Redgum hens, with strange intention,
In mourning for the gallant Breeches,
Lay only eggs as black as pitch is!
VICTORIA.
EDDYSON.
“Rami D.’’: It was on one of the tracks
leading into Morven (Q.). We pulled up at a
wayside shanty for a drink and found the local Bung-
in bed with rheumatics. His offsider assured us, as
she searched for the rum bottle, that she didn’t
know much about the business, and had all she
could do to keep the house free of goats. However,
she found the bottle on a topshelf, and poured out
the two stiff nips ** *. For two days we lay in the
scrub, as weak as so many yards of pump-water,
neither of us having strength to build a fire. When
we did manage to struggle back to the shanty it
was with the firm intention of lighting one under
the bar. But Bung had recovered by then, and met
our frontal attack with unruffled whiskers. “Got the
bottle from the top shelf, did she?” he queried
with mild interest. “Ah! Her mistake. That’s
some stuff I keep for a big buck nigger who’s
bo’nd’ry-ridin’ over on the Lower Warrego. He
comes in for a jag every couple of months and
makes a nuisance of himself unless he’s kep’ quiet.”

Sold Everywhere
“Steve Hart”: I’ve got a good word for
the starling. Recently I was travelling with some
lambing ewes on the Lachlan that were badly struck
by the blow-fly. Flocks of several hundreds of these
birds came along with us and practically wiped the
fly out.
“Captain” : In the early days of the New
Guinea occupation a company of Australians were
stationed at Herbertshbhe. One afternoon a couple
of hard-doers broke camp, and at midnight word was
brought in by two natives that “two fella soldier
making plenty noise” in a cemetery two
miles away. The O.C. despatched a sergeant
and two privates to bring them in. The pair
were found well “lathered up” and challenging all
hands and the Devil to arise and fight. The ser-
geant announced their arrest, and, on one of them
asking for trouble, the N.C.O. delivered the goods
to the point, knocking the recipient clean out. As
he hit the ground an unearthly cry rent the night.
“Blime,” said one of the escort, “you’ve broke his
neck, sergeant.” The sergeant rolled the victim
over. The haversack, viewed by the light of a hurri-
cane lamp, was bulky, and the investigator put his
hand in to ascertain its contents. Then there was
a howl and a curse —and out crawled a parrot, much
dishevelled and short of breath. The bird was
promptly made a regimental mascot, but the escort
always averred that not in its wildest screeches did
the fowl ever again make such an unearthly yell as
it did when its owner took the count in the
cemetery.

“Jobber”: “Ses” (B. 15/2/’l7) talks of
hatters. I met an ex-Australian in London re-
cently who told me of a 'cove he had struck out in
nor’-west Queensland. When my friend rode up to
the hut the old man darted inside and wouldn’t
budge. My pal poked in and asked him why in
thunder he’d bolted. “Oh, I’m goin’ Outback,” said
he. “This country's getting over-run. You're the
third man I’ve seen this year.”
“Dead Finish” : Here’s a useful tip. Re-
cently I came across a draught mare dead. 1 left
her to get ripe, and then opened her and smeared
arsenic inside. Result, millions of dead flies in a
very short time. Incidentally, if every defunct beast
was burned it would help to reduce the fly-curse
greatlv by doing- away with breeding-places. This
would be next to impossible on big runs, but the
cocky could do a great deal at no very alarming cost
or inconvenience.
“Munjong”: An animal which I have
neither seen nor heard of previously has taken
possession of an old sparrow’s-nest under the iron
roof of my hut in company with his better-half.
His fighting weight would be about the same as that
of a medium-sized rat. The fur is long and a reddish-
brown, turning to a rich golden color on the belly
and feet. In g-eneral appearance he is a
cross between a fox and a mouse, but I can hardly
believe this to be correct. These little creatures are
inclined to be friendly, though extremely timid.
They are grass-eaters, and ignored several biscuits
that I offered them. As high-jumpers I have not
seen their equal. On several occasions whilst play-
ing they have jumped from the ground to the top
rail of the fence with apparent ease, and they are
marvellously quick-climbers. I have been in most
parts of Australia, and have lived in the bush all
my life, but as far as my previous knowledge goes
these are the Adam and Eve of the species,

“Billy T.”: Billy Tregaskis, shift-boss,
told me this yarn. Peter Spence, aged 30, a smart,
square-set, good-looking, Australian-bred devil, was
a face-man at the Aurora mine, Eagle Head. He
had a vagrant fancy in women, and among others
upon whom he cast a roving grey eye was Amelia
Hill, daughter of the new shoemaker. That Amelia
should have received any attention from him at all
was proof of Peter’s liberal fancy, Miss Hill being
a tall, big-boned, flat-chested, shambling girl of 22
who, in all probability, had never before provoked
the smallest interest in a presentable male. Peter
went beyond mere compliments and, frivolous flirta-
tion ; and amazement at her good fortune possessed
Amelia all day long. Her elation was visible to the
neighbors, her infatuation a subject of common talk.
Then came the “cave” in No. 3 level at the Aurora.
It happened at 10 o’clock one night. Two men
were entombed, and the work of rescue was begun
at once. It was carried on at a terrific pace for
hours. There was no stowage, so the reef had to
be trucked to the plat, and whirled up to the sur-
face as fast as trucks could be filled and run, while
the hauling engine worked at top speed and the
cages danced up and down. Small attention , was
paid to individual workers in the sw’elter of the
dimly-lit drive; but one among them toiled in a
kind of frenzy—a lumbering youth in the customary
clay-caked pants and blue shirt of a trucker.
Nothing stopped him. Drenched with perspiration,
gasping in the bad air, he tore at the shattered reef,
hurled fragments into the trucks, then, running
three loaded trucks, was always on the heels of
those before, expostulating, mad with the fever of
haste. And the Aurora rails were none too true,
and there were many bad pinches between the fall
and the plat. Others fell out, done up, but this
trucker went on at a scarcely diminished speed, with
hands blistered and broken and a face so white it
might have been pipe-claved, the shift boss said.
The last of the broken logs was torn aside, and
Jones and Howell were pulled through the gap,
sound and well, and hauled along the drive by the
cheering miners. The white-faced trucker remained,
leaning on the wet side of the drive. He clutched
at Tregaskis. “But there’s Peter Spence!” he whis-
pered. He pointed to the gaping blackness beyond
the tumbled reef. “Peter’s in there,” he said. Tre-
gaskis laughed. “Not much !” he said. “Peter Spence
changed shifts with Howell to go to the show-dance
at Cockatoo to-night.” The white trucker clung to
Tregaskis’s sleeve. “Who did he go with?” he
asked. He seemed to be pleading, and for a
moment the shift boss blinked in surprise. “1
dunno,” said Tregaskis, “but I got an idea it’s Con-
ley’s girl from the post office.” Tregaskis followed
after the cheering crowd, and all went up. Nobody
noticed th(> absence of the trucker. Several hours
passed before men went into the broken drive again,
and then they found the tall trucker, whiter than
ever. Apparently he had fainted, and, as luck
would have it, the water had risen in the incline.
The poor wretch was drowned. The trousers and
shirt he wore were Sid Bennett’s. Sid had left them
in the changing-room when he knocked off after the
day shift. Under them were a petticoat and sii- >-
bodice. Tregaskis seemed to find much satisfaction
in the fact that Peter Spence subsequently married
a girl who, as his wife, gave him pnticular
Gehenna.

“L.” : My parents employed a gin who hud
two daughters. The elder one married a blaekiellow
and had a baby boy within a year. She died when
the piccaninny was three weeks old. The old gin
put the infant to her breast,' and it drew milk and
for 18 months was suckled by its grandmother,
although she had not had a child for 15 or 16 years.
* * * *
D.t. : Strabo, the Latin geographer,
said that in his time, a couple of thousand years
ago, many parts of Spain were nearly eaten out of
house and home by rabbits, and that the people of
the Balearic Islands had sent a petition to Rome
alleging that the bunnies were driving them off their
farms. But the pest vanished, and no one hears
anything about a rabbit curse in Spain these days.
So if the afflicted Australian squatters and cockies
only hold on for a few hundred years they mav be
all right. In Strabo’s time ferrets were reckoned
the best exterminator.

“Yambaooona” : “Montalex” (B. 17/5/T7)
and “Rodent” (B. 28,/6/’l7) want to know’ if the
blue-black rat has made good in Sydney. The blue-
black is, at the "present time, about equal in num-
bers in Sydney to the grey Norway or sewer rat;
but “Rodent” is wrong when he says the former is
wiping out the Norway. The fact is that the Nor-
way, by reason of his water-front and sewer pro-
clivities, has a bigger campaign waged against him
than his black or blue-black brother, who skulks in
warehouses. As to plague, the last and biggest local
outbreak was directly traced to a few short-tailed
Indian sewer-rats, and not to the blue-black, as
stated. These short-tailed rodents are very seldom
seen on ships arriving here, and inhabit principally
Ceylon and India. They are a compact, fluffy mass,
more like a young kitten than a rat, and wear ex-
tremely short tails, whereas the ordinary ship’s rat
has a particularly long appendix. I saw two of the
Indians caught on one of the Billy Hughes ships a
month or so ago. Talking of rats: (1) What is the
biggest one-night catch on record in a confined area,
and (2) the largest number of young discovered with
an ordinary ship or sewer rat? In the first instance,
let me enter 520-odd caught a couple of months
ago after fumigation in one of the larg’est boats
trading to Australia, and in the second case I’ll
back 21 young-—-found twice on vessels in Port Jack-
son. From 18 to 20 is a fairly common number, but
22 and 24 take beating.

IN PIKER’S BAR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“They were long, long tramps, those tramps o’
mine,” the lonely swagman said,
“And old Disgrace walked just behind, and black
Despair ahead.
The past, the bitter, jeering past, was pictured in
the skies,
And etched across the desert’s rim, where’er I turned
my eyes,
Alone, yet not alone, I tramped”—his tears were
hard to hide —
“Through all my lonesome journeys she was walking
by my side.
“For days and days, month after month, when all
was sad and weird,
I had my strange, my silent thoughts—the thoughts
that seldom cheered.
Through blinding heats and desert-glares, past half-
way tanks gone dry,
I trudged and trudged; no man, I swear, seemed
lonelier than I.
And yet 1 did not tramp alone; with patience wake-
ful-eyed,
She cheered me as we trudged along, together, side
by side.”
’The poet-chap, in Piker’s bar, he took the swag-
man’s hand.
“flow beautiful the sentiment!” he said. “How truly
grand !
And, deem me not impertinent—was she a sweetheart
dead,
Or wife, or child —a bonny child ?”—the poet
dropped his head. 1
“Oh, no; you’re on a branch-off track,” the lonely
man, replied;
“Through those long tramps my cattle slut was walk-
ing by my side” !

Business, Bobbery, etc
“Loani’-; At Waga Waga Experimental
Station in Milne Bay the Papuan Govern-
ment has some three acres devoted to vanilla,
and experts declare the beans to be equal to
if not better than those from Tahiti. The
orchid (for vanilla is the fruit of an orchid)
seems to have acclimatised itself, and there
is no doubt that, given proper culture and
right preparation for market, the beans can
be produced in great quantity and of uni-
form quality. The profits from only a few
acres should provide a handsome income to
the grower. A five-acre patch should produce
about £250 a year profit. Such manual labor
as is required can be done by a couple of
“boys” at £6 a year each, excluding food and
raiment, or a total of £l2 per boy. Land is
obtainable in blocks of one hundred acres
absolutely free of rent and survey fees for
the first 10 years; and about £2OO would be
enough to improve 10 acres in excellent style,
and provide the holder with a decent home.
Of course this estimate does not provide for
the living expenses of the grower until his
first crop is marketable, about a year from
planting.
“Condenser” ; The peculiarities of wireless
pointed out by “Devilfish” are well under-
stood by practical men. The fact that Hono-
lulu’s signals grow weaker when approaching
that station is no doubt due to some hill or
mountain lying in a direct line; the more
metal the natural obstruction contains the
greater will it attract the oscillations. From
certain points Hobart is hard to hear even
at a handy distance; but the same can be
said of every land wireless station in exist-
ence. Melbourne, for instance, grows very
weak to a ship under the lee of Wilson’s
Promontory, which is practically Melbourne’s
front fence. Yet I have read the same fine
station whilst lying at Fremantle.

“Ashton”: Sly-grogging is an art. I
lately examined a “still” in the side of a
creek where the smoke escaped through the
hollow trunk of a gum-tree. Also, one where
the opening to the cellar containing the still
was through the fireplace. The fire had to
be removed before entrance or exit could be
made. The smoke drifted up through cun-
ningly-contrived apertures and the old wife
saw to it that the fire above was kept well
stoked while the still worked. In both in-
stances the informer was the tinker who
mended the worm. I knew a woman whose
flower-bed borders were ornamented with
apparently dead marines. When a trifle of
“Border Blend” was wanted a full bottle
was pulled up and an empty one substituted.
A jealous admirer put her pot on.
“Copabella” : An ex-employee of the New-
port (Vic.) shops received a weird communi-
cation last week. The note came from the
Railways Department on a printed memo,
form, was typed, numbered, “copied,” in-
dexed for reply, and signed by the sender -it
had probably gone through four or five hands
—posted with a penny stamp and enclosed in
a printed envelope. It ran: “Please note
that when leaving Newport you were over-
paid the sum of 2d. Kindly forward at your
earliest.” What sort of system is it that
allows this sort of idiot to waste stamps,
paper and several persons’ time in this way?
One of the plainest arguments in favor of
interference with the present system of fruit-
distribution is to be found in the recent
admission of the Vic. apple-grower that he
would be satisfied with a penny a pound for
a normal crop. Even in the brightest times
he declares he doesn’t average this, though
there is no period of the year when really
decent eating-apples can be bought in Mel-
bourne shops for less than Bd. per jxound.
To the apple-consumer a middleman’s com-
mission of 200 per cent., added to a prime
cost of a penny a pound or less, looks more
like robbery,' etc., than business. When is
the Department of Agriculture going to do
its duty in organising these people co-oper-
atively ?

The North Coast Co-operative (N.S.W.),
Australia’s biggest butter-maker, had last
half-year “a record in output and turnover.”
It made 4648 tons butter, or 1617 tons more
than in the corresponding half of the pre-
vious year, and got an average price of
16.1 d. Also, it handled 19,855 pigs (5225
more) and paid “an average price of 7id.
per lb. for A 1 pork.” For statistical pur-
poses the company cuts up its terri-
tory—speaking broadly, the country north of
the Clarence —into three districts, and this
comparison shows how each has grown : —-
April, 1916. April, 1917.
Byron Bay .. 1,606,9021 b. 2,348,9531 b.
Lismore .. 2,058,236 „ 3,692,298 ~
Murwillumbah.. 3,125,218,, 4,370,155 ~
The progress of the Murwillumbah or north-
ernmost (Tweed River) section is the most
astonishing. Seven years ago the figures
were: Byron Bay, 3.080,7671 b.; Lismore,
3,084,8621 b.; Murwillumbah, 2,221,8251 b..
and that was an exceptional season- _p?e
total amount paid by the co. to supphe
the butter department during the last bail
year was £636,386; to suppliers m the bacon
department, £58,676. This co. does
one-half of the butter business of the xNoitu
Coast, so apparently the butter cheque that
went to that wonderfully fertile distnc
the six months was a little over a million
and a quarter. Add the bacon cheque ; and
now, especially on the Tweed, the ban
cheque is becoming of even more importance.
It looks as though the three industries will
dovetail in splendidly; for the pig ’"ill go
three-quarters of the way on the refuse fiom
the banana plantation and the other quarter
on the refuse from the cow-mill.
“T. 0.1.”: Just demolished in Pitt-street.
Sidney, to make way for the new building
of the London Bank, the old premises ot
Richardson and Wrench. In the first yea rs
of last century the site was occupied by
Mathew Kearns, who had been a soldier in
the Rum Corps, and was granted one of the
20 pub licenses authorised to be issued for
Sydney town. In the first ’6o’s Richardson
and Wrench came on the scene, with the
Metropolitan Hotel on the south. The
house was the home of John Hosking, first
Mayor of Sydney. The first licensee was
John Hampton, who had been a balloonist
in England; he subsequently kept the Met-
ropolitan, where now stands the Daily
Telegraph office, and the Oxford Hotel,
where he died.
* * * *
“F.W.W.”: Some of the gents who “put
through” wheat scrip for the eockies are
sharks. The procedure is to insert an allur-
ing ad. in the country rags. When Cocky
writes in for a quote he gets an ambiguous
reply. If the market hardens, along comes
a contract with a peremptory demand for
the scrip ; but a declining market brings a
dead silence. If Cocky personally interviews
the sharks he gets a verbal quote; but any
mention of a written contract is treated
with scorn as unworthy of a transaction
“between friends.” It’s up to the Govern-
ment to put this traffic on a decent footing
or end it.

“H.V.E.” on a possible “little” industry;
Post-bellum industries will assuredly include the
culture of many medicinal plants and herbs, which
were formerly supplied to British drug-manufacturers
for the main part by Germany and Austria. The list
of plants of commercial value which may be grown
in Britain, according to the prospectus of an estab-
lishment known as an “herb garden,” situated in
Buckinghamshire (Eng.), includes over 500 species,
and these plants should certainly flourish in the
cooler parts of Australia. Amongst them are acacia
Senegal (gum arabic), monkshood (aconite), chamo-
mile, snake-root, belladonna, buchu, foxglove
(digitalis), mustard (for the seed), senna, meadow
saffron (colchicum), gentian, liquorice, aniseed,
jalap, blue flag and iris (orris root and iridin),
juniper (oil of juniper), linseed, peppermint, mace,
opium poppy, ipecacuanha, Turkey rhubarb, castor-
oil plant (ricinus), nux vomica (including the alka-
loid strychnine), squill and valerian. A specimen
of the nux vomica plant grows, or grew
some few years ago, in Sydney Botanical Gardens.
For strychnine there is always a large demand,
while the supply is diminishing. A large proportion
of it came from Germany. Other plants of commer-
cial value include American ginseng, angelica, worm-
wood, English lavender (for perfumery), sage, mar-
joram, thyme, savory, tarragon, capsicum, brooklime,
hyssop, etc. Brooklime, by the way, common
enough in many parts of Australia and lauded as a
bush “simple,” is not quite the innocuous remedy
that it is supposed to be. A country medico records
a case in which the patient, acting as his own
physician, had reduced himself to mere skin and
bone by over-indulgence in brooklime tea. As regards
the culture of culinary herbs, such as thyme, sage,
etc., ante-bellum importations of these in a dry
state into Australia cost about £lO,OOO annually,
while the few local growers now operating estimate
the returns from such plants at from £4O upwards
per acre. With respect, however, to the culture, of
medicinal plants, local demand alone is unlikely to
be large enough to make the industry worth an Aus-
tralian grower’s while. He must look farther afield
for his chief market. But against this stands the
fact that several of the great English and American
drug manufacturers have branch houses or agencies
in most Australasian capitals, and to these locally-
grown drug material, if of good quality, might be
disposed of. Such an industry, if established in
Australia, might give remunerative employment to
persons physically unfitted for hard labor. Aus-
tralians interested may obtain information as to
prices of seed or roots of various medicinal plants
and other useful information by addressing the
Principal, AVhin’s Herb Garden, Chalfont St. Peter
Bucks., England.

‘D.F. : Matthew Flinders reckoned the
number of mutton-birds in one mob he saw
m Bass Straits in 1799 at 220,000,000. You
don t see so many nowadays; nevertheless a
single family working on the islands is said
to have killed and salted down 30,000 birds
for export last season.

CONCRETE! ships.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Well, shiver me timbers!” the old tar said,
As lie thoughtfully scratched his hairless head.
“When I was at sea, a ship was a ship
And a church was a church! I gets the pip
When I hears how they builds the ships of stone
And sends ’em to sea with a sailor’s groan,
To waller an’ heave on the ragin’ biller.
It’s like goin’ to sea in a Queen Anne viller!”
“When / was at sea,” he said with a lurch,
“A ship was a ship and a church was a church” —
Then he gave his trousers a sailor’s hitch —
“Now I’m bunkered if I know which is which!”
N.S.W.

“Viator”: I’ve been trying to classify the
sounds made by motors. Here is the list to date:
Hog’s grunt, bullock bell, bull’s bellow, billygoat’s
bleat calf’s agony, cow’s moan, baby hippopotamus’s
gurgle, foa-horn, frog’s clank, elephant’s shriek,
peacock’s laugh, locomotive whistle with cold in
throat horse’s cough, steamboat sj' ren, hyaena s bark,
tiger’s snarl. Who will deny that the motor hasn’t
made life more beautiful!

“De Oriel”: At Eskdale (Vic.) my oppo-
nent at billiards was a one-armed man. He used his
old felt hat as a cue rest and played well. In Gun-
bower (Vic.) the marker had one leg only. I played
a one-eyed man in S. Aus. This artist used to sight
down the cue like a rifle and he was hard to defeat,
But 1 give the oil to a dwarf in Melbourne. His
head just reached the .edge of the table, and he was
compelled to use the jigger tor eveiy shot. On
account of his natural advantage of observation he
made some good breaks.
* * * *
Owing to the rumor having got round that
the Sportsmen’s Thousand, now recruiting in Mel-
bourne, is in need of a mascot, its city depot is a
rendezvous for people with wallabies, native bears
and opossums in sufficient numbers to stock a men-
a"-erie. Bitter complaints have been raised by the
tenants of the building, whose outcry, mingled with
the noises of the live stock, has prompted a member
of the recruiting committee to yell that the only
animals it needs are Men.
* * * *
“Barcoo”: In North Bananaland the black
swan, the duck and other wild fowl mate and begin
to breed immediately upon the coming of the mon-
soonal rains. In a good season the old birds will
mate again in four months, keeping the young ones
company in their first courtship. The increase in
feathers is enormous, especially in the remote parts
of the Gulf. As gunshot is practically unknown, the
game is easy to approach, and an evening's bag is a
sportsman's dream. The “whistler” is the most
numerous of the tribes. Usually it is classed as a
duck, but it is really a goose.
• * * *
“Corio”: “Toby C.,” who says that Sca-
mander (Tas.) is the best fishing spot on the map,
forgot Laurieton (North Coast N. S. Wales).
Winter and summer, in the shoals of sand-spit and
islet of the river-mouth, you can yank out great,
thumping flathead, whiting, snapper and bream. Dur-
ing the warm harrier tides the sea-tailer cake-walks
on to a hand-line, the lobster insists on being your
friend for life, and huge crabs of the edible variety
seem to delight in imprisoning themselves in the pots
prepared for their capture. And in the retail shops
kept by the cousins of Constantine you can easily
catch a whole mullet with ss.
* * * *
“Sinker” : The most exciting catch I ever
saw was made on the North Coast (N.S.W.). The
engineer of a bar-bound coaster, having cast his line,
went to look at his engines. A seaman pulled the
line up, tied on the lid of a kerosene tin and dropped
it overboard. When McAndrew came back someone
tugged the line. “Got him,” bawled Mac and began
to haul in hand over hand. How- that tin-lid did
perform! It darted about like a thing of life.
Passengers and crew rushed to the side and cheered
the gleaming white object fighting on the line.
“Snapper, snapper!’’ rose from the length of the ship,
and Mac pulled like one possessed. At last he saw
his catch! And then the real excitement started.
* * * *
“Liles”: “Montalex’s” recent revelations
about trick shooting are interesting, but there is one
statement I want to contradict. Shooting within 15
inches of a lighted candle will not put it out, unless
you use a shotgun. I can assure “Montalex” that
the “wind” of a bullet cannot be depended on to put
a candle out even if it passes within fiin. of the
flame. Mention of spectacular shooting reminds me
of “Curly” Bell, or “Professor” Walsh, as he calls
himself sometimes. Most people in Victoria know
him. He usually travels with a portable gallery, and
it is child's play for him to shoot the ash off a
cigarette. No fake either; I’ve been smoking the
fag myself frequently. He will shoot a hole in a
cigarette card, turn the card on edge, and “cut the
hole out”—that is, shoot the card in two so that no
trace of the first hole is left. He has done that at
night-time with the aid of two flares.
“Bill R.” : The liveliest fish that anglers
take are the giants of the mackerel family—the tar-
pon, tuna and tunny. The first (called also the
silverfish) grows to six feet long. He frequents the
Gulf of Mexico and seas of the West Indies, where
his huge, silvery scales, used in fancy work, are a
staple article of trade. He is the speediest thing that
swims, and fishermen that hook him either
hold their rods under water till he takes a pull or
have an automatic spray playing irpon their reels
to prevent: the line burning off as it whizzes through
the rings. To kill one inside two hours is considered
a feat to write home about. The tuna attains to
half the size of the tarpon. He adds to his speed
the trick of bouncing 20 or 30 feet from the wave
crests in his wild efforts to break from the line. He
is the dandy of the species—blue, with red-brown
lateral stripes, and is plentiful on the American
coasts, handy to the millionaire tuna-fishing clubs.
The tunny belongs to the rock-bound coasts of the
Mediterranean, but in recent years the fish lias
appeared in Australian seas. Less agile than the
other fellows, he makes up for that deficiency by
weight—-up to 10001 b. Any sport going to hunt for
one should try the deep water near rockv cliffs, be-
cause the tunny. is a rock-fish feeder. The only reli-
able bait is a live rock-fish with a small hook, clove
hitched to the big line, anchored to his tail, and the
tunny hook swung to his gills with a thread.
* * * *
“Bad Australian”: One hundred years ago
General Josfi San Martin and his citizen army left
Mendoza for the Pacific Coast to undertake the cam-
paign that freed South America from the Spanish
yoke for ever, and this pueblo (Mendoza) has been
busy for the last week (February) celebrating the
centenary of that famous expedition. And while the
bands blared and the politicians blithered and the
proletariat, full of the bad red wine of the province,
howled “Vive la Patria” and can-canned and tangoed,
Lieutenant Zami, of the National Flying Corps,
climbed on to his monoplane and lit out in the
hope of crossing the Andes. But at Las Vacas he
was caught in a strong air current and whirled like
a scrap of thistledown back down the canon he had
previously traversed. The machine Was, smashed to
pieces, but the pilot escaped unhurt except for a few
bumps and scratches. Zami (known as El Gato—
The Cat) does not seem much discouraged by his
failure, and threatens to make another attempt
shortly. What I wrote (B. 30/’4/T4) when the ill-
fated George Newberry proposed to make the first
attempt at crossing the Andes on a flying machine,
holds good to-day—the man who does it in an aero-
plane will have to climb up to about 16,000 ft. and hold
to that altitude for 250 kilometers, otherwise he is
sure to be turned over by the strong cross-currents
which blow over the crest of the Cordillera and down
the frequent, canons on either side of his line of
flight. Zami’s 140 kilometers is the best Andean
record so. far, but there remain 100 kilometers and a
further rise or possibly 9000 feet before the

“Nerves”
Our nerves are similar to an intri-
cate network of telegraph wires.
Controlled and nourished by a
portion of the brain —known as
the nerve centres —the delicate
threadlike nerves radiate in all
directions throughout the body.
So long as the nerve centres are
capable of continually supplying
nourishment to the nerves, the
nerves will remain strong and
healthy. But directly the nerve
centres become weakened by
overwork, worry or anxiety, they
are unable to transmit the
necessary nourishment, and the
nerves become worn out and
“on edge.” Then it is that a sud-
den sound makes you “ jump ”
—you get irritable —you suffer
from neuralgia—you are rest-
less and depressed. In this con-
dition there is nothing to equal

force, stimulates and revitalises
the whole nervous system. Try
‘Wincarnis’ for ‘Nerves.’ It
is wonderful. Over 10,000
Doctors recommend it.

WRINKLES
How the Japanese Banish them for ever.
Accept this great offer made to intro-
duce in this country a totally new
method, guaranteed to perma-
nently remove even the
deepest creases within
one week.
Nothing even remotely resembling this
new Japanese method has ever been heard of
in this country before. That is the only
reason why so many women now have
wrinkles and still consider them incurable.
I want one thousand
ladies in this country
to remove their wrin-
kles by this method
and agree 'to recom-
mend it to their
friends AFTER it has
done all I claim. This
is frankly an advertis-
ing offer, but the names
and addresses will be
treated as strictly con-
fidential and not used
in any way. I prefer
applicants to be from
forty to seventy years
of age, and the more wrinkled their faces are
the more pleased I shall be to make them
look ten to thirty years younger.
Remember I am not asking some ridicu-
lously exorbitant price to try this method,
nor do I charge you for any “instruc-
tions.” I bind no one to secrecy, and
do not claim ability to remove wrinkles
by means of any sort of “flesh food,”
face powder, plasters, lotions or pastes,
prescriptions, medicines, steaming, ban-
dages, masks, electricity, exercises, mas-
sage, apparatus or any mechanical appli-
ances whatsoever. In fact, I have no toilet
articles of any kind to sell you for removing
wrinkles, and I do not recommend any com-
plicated “treatment” or “system,” either
duplex, triplex or any other sort of “plex.”
I guarantee to hold this offer open to all
applicants for three months from the time
this announcement appears. SEND NO
MONEY, but, if convenient, three penny
stamps may be enclosed for my posting ex-
penses. There is no obligation of any kind.
Merely address Mme. Y. Miyako (Suite
23A), 4S Dover Street, London, W.L,
England, and your letter will receive prompt
attention, under plain, sealed cover. I agree
to return even your postage in full, if you
are not surprised, astonished and delighted
by what I send you.
