POLITICAL
POINTS
Optimism : A poisonous tree cultivated by
censors for the shelter of slackers.
Looking at the difference between Aus-
tralian political promise and performance,
it’s not so much a matter of Winning the
War as of Hughesing the War.
It’s a forked tongue that clamors for com-
pulsion to raise all Australia’s, commercial
forces to exterminate the Hun in the trade
war, and yelps against the principle when
Australia’s blood is the stake.
N. S. Wales is still the shocking example
in the matter of big estates. Of all. the
unimproved land value held by Ma State
people 4G per cent, is in lots of over £5000;
Vic. comes next with 38 per cent.; Wes-
tralia is third with 33 per cent; S. Aus. and
the Speck tie at 32 per cent.
Cocky has been so often bumped between
two stools that at last he seems almost
awake. For a start, his N. S. Wales con-
ference has just passed a resolution in favor
of substituting Elective Ministries for the
Party Government foolishness, with prefer-
ential voting, proportional representation
and the initiative and referendum.
The S. M. Herald expresses disgust:—
It is an unfortunate thing that in most, if not
all, British communities we have not learned to
grasp the abstract conception of the State; to us it
represents a group of politicians, changing every
few years, squabbling continuously among themselves,
and absolutely incapable of inspiring in us either
confidence or the spirit of service.
Well, if Party Government is as bad as that
—and it is —why doesn’t the Herald help to
change it for something more decent?
What should Germany- want with naval
battles in which both sides are likely to get
hurt? The destruction of the British Fleet
by internal explosions is only a matter of
time, apparently, and the blowing up of one
anchored Dreadnought and DX) seamen is a
fair-sized victory in itself. Why should the
Hun command attempt to do openly and
riskily the very thing which the forces of
treachery are almost offered facilities to do
unobserved ?
There is a yell at the Commonwealth Gov-
ernment to let the public have the wheat it
has bought instead of making a dead loss by
letting mice eat it. But, as a matter of fact,
the Government isn’t losing anything. It is
the farmers who have to stand all the loss.
They get paid not for the wheat that goes
info the pool, but for what comes out of it.
If’there is any kick due to the Government
it is for taking the wheat out of Cocky’s
control, as it did, and making him responsi-
ble for what happens to it afterwards.
“Copabella”: When a proposition was
made to add a stinkery or two to the Yarra
at Richmond-Hawthorn the press printed
many columns about “the march of pro-
gress” and the “pollution of our noble river.”
Melbourne City Council now proposes to
make the equally noble and even nearer
Maribyrnong River at West Melbourne the
noxious-trades area of Melbourne. But only
the common herd lives out that side —and let
’em be stunk out: who cares? Anyway, I
have heard no yell from the press this time.
There is probably some s'gnifieance in the
singular unanimity which distinguished the
two candidates concerned in Vic.’s latest
Assembly by-election—a struggle for the
safe Liberal, Western District seat of Pol-
wartli, vacated by Johnstone, of Colac.
James McDonald, who grows apples at In-
verleigh, fought it out with Charles Mere-
d:th, a Colac farmer. Both advocated what-
ever it is that the Economy Party advo
cates. Both had “sound” views regard ng
labor. Both couldn’t get in ; and it merely
happened to be McDonald who was in the
lead at the end.
“The Oldest Inhabitant” : Oh, these poli-*
ticians! On France’s Day two of them,
Ministers of the Crown in N. S. Wales,
captured some public applause by announc-
ing that the Government intended to convey
to the Government of France for ever an
acre of land at La Perouse, where there
stands a monument to the unlucky French
navigator. Now, nearly 100 years ago this
very land was set apart by Governor Bris-
bane—in 1824, to be exact —ns a gift for
ever to France, and surely this must have
been known to the two Ministers who made
the announcement.
The Council of the Australian Protection-
ist Association (Vic.) has been censuring
the Federal Government for its failure to
prevent the dumping of luxuries. Up to the
end of May. laments the A.P.A.. 11 months
importations of apparel exceeded those for a
similar period ending May, 1914, by more
than £4,000,000. Prior to the May elections
the Ass. made a few solemn protests to tne
Government, and quoted yards of alarming
figures; but when it came to an actual show-
down the Ass. and its supporting press quite
failed .to pull or push anybody or anything
in the desired direction. The incurable pot-
tdrers of the concern tamely ended up by
relying on the piecrust promises of the place-
hungry Prime Minister, and the usual thing
happened
Wilhelm’s submarines have given Aus-
tralia a further instalment of Protection.
Before August, 1914, we used to get most of
our lamp-glasses from Bavaria ; the rest came
from Amurrika —especially the glass for the
hurricane-lamp that Billjim’s old man
carries when he pokes round the stable at
night. For two years the prices in Mel-
bourne steadily rose till the ordinary lamp-
glass —the thing with a 3in. bottom and a
crinkled top—got from 6d. to Is. retail. To-
day it is 9d., wholesale 6s. 6d. The explana-
tion is a sizeable factory out Essendon way
—a factory whose owners are still waiting
to hear that Parliament has supplied them
with post-war Protection. If they don’t get
that, they know what will happen. We shall
be employing the foreigner to make lamp-
glasses while supplying our own unemployed
with relief works paid for with lOU’s.

A recent Tasmanian experience shows up
the idiocy of the Parliamentary party sys-
tem —the system under which Smith must go
on voting with Jones because he agrees with
h.m on one point, though cord.ally d.sagree-
ing with him on 50. When six-o’clock
closing was put to the direct, non-party vote
in Tasmania the other day it was carried
overwhelmingly. Yet when, encouraged by.
that vote, Coid Tea butted into recent by-
elections. it ran nowhere. Which means
that, though the referendum showed a huge
Cold Tea majority, CVd Tea would have no
hope in Par Lament. because there the mem-
bers’ votes are determ.ned by other consider-
at ons altogether. And that is why every
reformer should hold fast to the referendum,
with initiative.
A recent request by Victorian bakers to be
allowed to increase the “fixed” price of
bread was met with a suggestion that these
gentlemen should improve their organisation.
Twelve uproarious youths on as many carts
career down the street delivering each" a pint
of milk to 1G or 18 customers, and an hour
or two later another dozen equally noisy lads
hasten along handing a small loaf or two to
each of 18 or 20 housewives. This process
is repeated in dozens of streets all day long.
The grievous waste didn’t matter quite so
much in days past, when horse-feed was
cheap and milk-ohs could be hired for a half-
sov. a week ; but now that the distributors
have to be paid wages approximating those
of a skilled mechanic it ought to dawn on
the baker —and a lot of other tradesmen —
that only a congenital idiot would indulge
in such a caper.
“Edo”: Pearce, Minister for Defence, has
once more distinguished himself. Early in
the year, when the cry for recruits was
louder than usual, he countenanced the en-
listment of men up to 50 years, trustfully
accepting the recruits’ word ’for their ages.
The result was a crop of toothless, rheumy
old fellows at various centres, particularly
at Blackboy (W.A.). For quite three months
these oldsters were kept in camps, ted,
clothed, paid ss. a day and supplied with
false teeth. Then the brilliant Pearce dis-
covered that men over 45 were useless at
the Front, and the old fellows, mostly enthu-
siasts, were sent back to their homes at
Government expense, with their mouths full
of Government teeth.
A dark shadow is hanging over all men
interested in industrial metal-mining in Aus-
tralia —the possibility of a practical stop-
page of shipping. With so large a percent-
age of the world’s mercantile marine engaged
in war work, and the submarine sending its
quota to the bottom, the position is precari-
ous, and for the first time Australia is begin-
ning to realise that a continent which
obtains most of its manufactured articles
from abroad, and pays for them, together
with interest on its huge loans, by exporting
enormous quantities of raw material, is in a
parlous condition when the supply of ocean-
carriers peters out. Metals are a high price,
and the demand for them across the waters is
urgent; but if they cannot be exported,
cabled quotations are only tantalising. Our
problem is essentially quite different from
Britain's. Britain has turned from manu-
facture to food-production—that way lies
the solution of her problem. Our problem is
how to absorb in manufacture some at least
of the industry which has hitherto been
devoted to the production of food and raw
materials. And that means, first and fore-
most, a guarantee of Protection for new in-
dustries after the war; otherwise capital
will certainly not be available to establish
them during the war.
The N. S. Wales Treasury has just discov-
ered that, though £34.000 was voted for the
new Zoo, the amount actually spent has been
£70,000. This is merely another instance of
the sublime contempt of departmental officers
for. Parliament; and, of course, that is
mainly the Parliament’s own fault, because,
as a rule, the passing of estimates has been
a mere matter of form—frequently the money
has been actually spent long before. Nobody
will, however, ever know what the Zoo has
cost altogether, both directly and indirectly.
But this thing is quite certain: from
the very outset it was a political scandal.
The work, which never ought to have been
touched in wartime, was undertaken for one
reason, and one reason only; to provide
emplovment for various men who were of
present or prospective value to certain poli-
ticians. Further, a thorough inquiry ought
to be made not only into past but into cur-
rent expenditure.
Ma’s Labor Minister Beeby had a brain-
wave about finance the other day and poured
the details out at Dubbo. His idea is that
the public debt should be cut up and appor-
tioned to various revenue-earning undertak-
ings, the transactions of these concerns and
future borrowings being kept in water-tight
compartments. Thus the railways would
be debited with so many millions, and if more
money was being looked for it could be ob-
tained by the issue of railway stock giving
the lender direct collateral security for his
cash. There are possibilities about this
notion which might profitably be investi-
gated. mainly for the purpose, not of sim-
plifying the issue of new loans, as Beeby
seems to think, but of getting public under-
takings on more respectable legs than they
have been supplied with hitherto.
From over the Tasman Sea: —
The howl for closing Bung at six o’clock is loud
and long all over Maoriland. As this is about the
only thing a howl can be raised about without frac-
turing the War Regulations, and as the community is
bursting with anxiety to hit something, the noise has
probably got a punch behind it. Anyhow, it is a
noise that makes Bung wake up with a start in the
middle of the night and wipe the sweat off his neck.
To some of him it sounds horribly like the banshee
calling. Meanwhile there has been more fun in watch-
ing Bill Massey trying to dodge McStiggins. Wil-
liam can handle the affairs of Empire and Mowhango-
painuiki with the easy poise of a statesman, but
when it comes to handling Booze and the water-
waggoners, he holds himself firmly in reserve. It
looks as if the front doors are going to bang, all
the same; and what may bang after that Heaven
alone can tell. McStiggins seems to be getting his
blood up.
Once more Trethowan, M.L.C., President
of the Federated Cockies, has been publicly
holding his nose and calling for chloride of
lime while he mentioned recent State land
resumptions in N. S. Wales for “repatriation
purposes.” Simultaneously Lands Minister
Ashford has been denying that he could smell
anything, and calling upon Trethowan to
make “specific charges.” This paper does not
know what the Boss Cocky has up his sleeve.
But it knows what the resumption of estates
means as a general thing. It means the
conversion of inflated values into public debt
—the burdening of working farmers and the
taxpaying public with a ruinous load. If
we are to escape social disorder the taxation
of land values must come, and come quickly.
That alone is a reason why these always hole-
and corner schemes of resumption should be
given the boot —-and why almost every com-
mission agent and monopolist on the con-
tinent is an enthusiastic x'esumptionist.
In the Bill just introduced, earnings from
pastoral pursuits are exempted from the pro-
posed Commonwealth war-time profits tax.
Why? These pastoral people are much less
entitled to any excess profits than, say, the
manufacturer or even the shopkeeper, because
abnormal receipts from cattle and sheep have
been in no way due to extra energy or the
risking of more capital. Squatters have
made their huge extra profits wholly and
solely through higher prices; and those
higher prices have been wholly and solely
due to the war. Nobody in all this country
is less entitled to escape; nobody, perhaps,
is more surprised than they are themselves
that it should be proposed to let them escape.
Altogether the list of exemptions is absurdly
long. It is, in fact, very doubtful whether
there should be any exemptions at all—that
is, any exemption of profits di" to the war.
An exception may have *to be made in favor
of new industries; but even those would
hardly be entitled to it if they were assured
of adequate Protection after the war.
“E. Due” : There are about SOOO students
in the secondary schools of N. S. Wales,
7000 of them being in the State High
Schools ; and there is a great fear amongst
this army that it will not be possible to get
the necessary text-hooks from England for
next year. As far as language works are
concerned, it is criminal of the authorities
to think of taking up valuable room on the
boats with such things. The text of the
books required is. nine times out of ten (say,
for example, in French), too stale for copy-
right to hold good. These surely could be
printed here. As they are required for only
one year, cheaper editions than are usually
supplied would do. The authorities could
then make sure that the type was suitable
and unlikely to cause eye-strain. The
teachers could be supplied, if necessary, with
annotated copies from abroad, if it was not
thought desirable to go to the expense of
printing the notes as well. If all the other
States would see that, as far as practicable,
the same hooks were set for study, money
would be saved and a few Australian printers
kept busy. As for the subjects other than
languages, if all the States would agree on
the books required, there is no reason why
they' should not be written as well as printed
in Australia. Present conditions do not give
a show to anyone who puts a text-book on
the market, i can vouch for that; so can
my publishers.

MELBOURNE, VICTORIA,
The runners-up in the two Nationals had
air outing at Caulfield last Saturday, and each of
them was a hot pot for his race. Caibye, starting at
2 to 1, landed the Steeple easily enough. A surprise
in this event was' the forward running of Union
Arms, a gelding, imported by the Tyes, that was
getting 461 b. ilbm the winner. The fall of Waimai
was a greater surprise. Seeing that the Maoriland
champion had jumped the National course when he
had only just arrived at Flemington, it seemed safe
to back him to stand up at Caulfield a week later.
But half-way in the journey the expert fencer made
a mistake, which goes to suggest that the change of
climate is not suiting him too well at present. Bun-
dong, a 7 to t favorite for the Hurdle, finished second
for the third time in succession. Colonel Light,
carrying nearly as much weight as the vaunted favor-
ite, led from start to finish. Bundong nearly caught
him at the last hurdle, but the outsider was first to
land and he seemed to have most steam for the run
home. Anyhow, he won by a length, so the alleged
brilliancy of the disappointing Bundong is at last
under grave suspicion. Three times lie has been
advertised as a certainty, and each time he has failed
at the finish over hurdles. He jumps with unfailing-
correctness, and is apparently quite equal to steeple-
chase fences. If Bundong can’t win the big Hurdle
Race at Caulfield on August 4, he has a chance.of
doing better in the long and slower event a week
later.
It is remarked of Old Mungindi that he shows
more intelligence in racing than the average nag,
and where there are many obstacles in the way a
bit of, brains is a big 'consideration. It keeps the
possessor out of mischief where many another fellow
might come to grief. Carbine had more brains than
most of his kind. Poseidon was conspicuously intel-
ligent in a race. He could be seen threading his
way through the mob. The jockey used to take hints
from the gee. And Old Mungindi has probably in-
herited his father’s mental powers. He “knows how
to take care of his blanky self.”
* * * *
Everything that ran prominently in either
of the Nationals is given a flattering show for the
Australian Hurdle and Steeple, which will be worth
£l2op and £l3OO respectively to the winners.
With a lot of latest and trustworthy form to go
upon, the Caulfield handieapper can hardly be caught
napping when he serves out weights for the big
August events; and now that it is the rule to
refrain from crushing the top weights, or penalising-
the National winners vindictively, punters get plenty
of scope for reasonable argument. At present there
are at least a dozen in each race with an obvious
chance to do as well as they did at Flemington, or
better. 'Bundong, Merunqua, Pistolier, Nick-a-Jaek,
Lord Grey, Van Homreigh, Royal Precedent, Obi,
Mac-naughty, Coronatus, King Mostyn and others are
available for the Hurdle, and the handieapper has
been unkind to none of them. For instance, the
ancient Obi has 71b. less than he carried into second
place last 3 r 6ar, whilst Aan Homreigh, being much
less apeient, has gone up 81b. for beating him. The
sturdy Van is a first-class fencer in private, so he
may be given a chance against Caibye, Old Mungindi,
Waimai and the rest of them in the Steeplechase
where veteran Booligal is let off with 61b. less than
he ran second with two years ago. Caibve is allowed
81b. for his defeat by Mungindi the other Saturday.
The writer reckons that the winner is the more likely
to improve between times and is therefore the more
attractive investment of the stylish pair. On the
other hand, it is probable that Waimai, not long off
boat and train when he ran third to them at Fleming-
ton, will be- the most improved of the trio next
month. He gets a further concession of 41b. from
Caibye, and receives five from Mungindi instead of
giving seven. But in appearance the Maoriland horse
is quite a commoner compared with the other two,
and he was only a poor third to them in the Grand
Nat.

Most of the winners at Rosehill (Sydney)
on Saturday are listed in bigger races to come
shortly. Among them is Two Blues, weighted at
7st. 71b. in the Epsom and 7st. 61b. in the Metro-
politan. This nag won on same course a year
ago, but has done very little since. He started with
Bst. 61b. in the Flying at Canterbury Park on June
30 at any old price, and ran accordingly. On Satur-
day he was solidly backed in the Carlingford Mile
with 9st. 61b., and made no mistake about it. This
horse may be heard from again. Another winner was
Aorbury, appropriator of last Newcastle Cup, who
in a moderate field with Bst. 81b. was backed down
to evens for the principal race. He ran according
to specifications. Norbury is allotted Bst. in the
Metrop. and 31b. Jess in each of the two big Clips.
The Antonio-Gay Girl filly Amber Rose,
who won over six furlongs at Rosehill last month,
was on Saturday again in the same place at the end
of the same distance on the same course. This time
she carried 9st. and had a sharp tussle with a new-
comer named Tom Splendor. Tom is a youngster
that has not been knocked about and looks like devel-
oping into something useful. Amber Rose is one
of the few of the rising generation who may be
suspected of being able with increasing age to staj r .
She is a very nice filly.
Isn’t it about time one or other of the prin-
cipal racing clubs tried to find out what the handi-
cappers are up to, what they are paid for, and to
what extent their work conforms with the club’s
ideas? In theory the business of the handicapper is
to bring the nominated horses together—to give all
hands a chance. What actually happens is that after
a horse has won a race or two the weight-fixer says
to himself “No more for you,” falls on the wretched
animal, and reduces it to the- monotonous contract
of starting in multitudinous races without a chance,
in order to qualify for an opportunity of winning
again later on. There is something horribly wrong
about all this. One result is to produce a lot of
inferior nags as winners and to keep better-class
horses toiling painfully in the rear. Up to a certain
point w T eight matters very little. Condition and dis-
tance matter most. But when the public sees a horse,
after winning over say six furlongs a couple of
times, handicapped in a mile-and-a-half event as if
the races had been over that distance, and when it
knows that the animal could not see the journey out
with only the saddle aboard, the episode creates an
impression that someone has been shooting in the
dark. The object of a racing club should be to
unearth the best horses of their kind. Handicapping
does nothing to help this. A far more rational plan
would be to divide programmes into events for differ-
ent classes of horses, into which entrance would be
gained by performance, and run them all on weight
for age. The result of that would be to clear out a
lot of mongrels early in the proceedings, put a
hurry-up argument behind the waiter, and make the
racecourse test a true one. To see a couple of third-
raters winning what half a dozen good, honest horses
are prevented from acquiring is one of the pathetic
features of every race meeting, and the disastrous
consequences are sprinkled over hundreds of pedi-
grees, giving to them a significance entirely false.

“Mujik” : The interested individuals who
were asked to formulate a scheme of sport-curtail-
ment in N. S. Wales for the Federal Government’s
information have brilliantly solved the problem by
practically denying that there should be any curtail-
ment at all. They contemplate a cut at the Wednes-
day pony meetings, but horse racing is to progress as
merrily as before, with ample provision for trots.
If the Government has an idea that the prevalence
of race meetings and other forms of “sport” hinders
it in winning the war, then its duty is obvious—
to do what it believes to be the right thing and take
the responsibility. But responsibility does not rest

chairman of the
Tas. Racing Clud.

is far too ranch racing

really sporting in-
stinct among racing

the bull’s-eye. There

Melbourne has lost its polo-ground. The
Kooyong Club, which didn't have its 20-acre block at
Kooyong, but away at Caulfield, lias lately found the
value of its land greatly increased by the arrival of
an electric-tram line. As most of the active club
members are playing the bigger game the time
seemed ripe for selling out to a subdividing syndi-
cate which had been clamoring for permission to buy
at a good, round figure ; so the club has taken what
the Fates sent and closed the deal —with some thank-
fulness. Government House will now have to practise
in its own back yard, of course; but the change will
mean a considerable saving for a good many people
who only stuck to the concern because the other
fellow did.
Lieut. Arthur Donnan, of the Maoriland
Forces, killed in France, was the first Herald and
Mail scholar at the Hawkesbury College (N.S.W.).
Afterwards he was appointed Agricultural Inspector
and lecturer to the Technical Collegp at Auckland
(M.L.). Two brothers are in the battle line ; They
belong to the Donnan family well known in Aus-
tralian cricket.
The Red God has claimed another good
Tasmanian sport in France —Charlie Stewart, a few
years back among the best oarsmen on the Denvent.
He and his brothers ran the ferry at Risdon, and in
their spare time made themselves into one of the
best four-oared crews that ever feathered blades on
Tassy’s waters.
Starting from France Lance-sergt. Stuart
Chambers, formerly of Elbow Valley and latterly of
South Brisbane, has followed his brother Arthur, a
major, who was killed in Palestine. Stuart Chambers
was a 20-year-old machine-gunner, and was well
known in Bananaland as a Rugby Union footballer.
The family has two more sturdy sons on the job—
Alec a corporal and Howard a sergeant.
Major Jim Cassidy, who died last week,
aged 87, was a native of Bathurst (N.S.W.), and
the oldest auctioneer in the Ma State. He was the
last of the Western 22 which played against
Stephenson’s All England Eleven in 1862 at Bathurst.
“Grammar’; How about this as au exhibit
to beat the story of “Benno” (B. 19/7/’l7), con-
cerning the experiences of five members of a Sydney
cricket club? In 1915 the “Shore” (Sydney Church
of England Grammar School) second crew in the
Great Public Schools Regatta consisted of J. Black-
wood (bow), J. Small (2), N. S. Griffin (3), R. C.
Milton (stroke), P. C. A. Fornachon (coach). For-
nachon enlisted in ’l5 and has been missing since
soon after he landed at Gallipoli. Jack Small, Jimmy
Blackwood and “Beach” Milton have all been killed
over there somewhere, and the lad who rowed 3,
after being rejected half a dozen times, was at last
accepted and now wears three stripes and the snake’s
skin round his hat with the Light Horse at Menangle.
This page has struck the champion fool-eon-
tributor. He put the word “Obit” before a par con-
cerning Jack Whittle, V.C., the Speck’s heavyweight.
Now he explains that “Obit” is merely his pen-name,
and that Whittle isn’t at all dead. The Bulletin
accordingly resurrects Whittle and kills the con-
tributor.
* *• * *
Wave Geike blew into Sydney Stadium
from Bananaland on Saturday determined to keep
close up against Sid Godfrey and punch his ribs. But
Sidney objected with straight lefts, which kept Mr.
Geike at bay, and then smacked him on the jaw
as an inducement to go on remaining there. The
northerner showed plenty of courage and endurance
and great lack of originality. By the sixteenth he
was very weary, whereupon Godfrey rapidly poked
a couple of lefts into his countenance and sent a
swinging right into the vicinity of the ear. This,
being topped off with another swing which the stricken
Waverley intercepted on the lower mandible, neatly
terminated the proceedings.
Tlie confusion between Owen Cairns and
Sol Jones for the Bananaland Middleweight title,
held at Brisbane on Saturday, was mostly wrestle
and rope-walking. As Cairns had a heavy cargo of
tallow aboard he set about convincing Sol with the
least possible exertion. It didn’t need much. Solomon
is not as young as he was, and a few meaty argu-
ments in the bingy quickly appeased his appetite.
By the ninth clash he was sitting down from sheer
lack of support, and in the next round suddenly
remembered an appointment. The referee, however,
wouldn’t excuse him until he had taken a couple
more helpings.
* * * *
Mick King, one-time middleweight cham-
pion, returned to Sydney last week from an unautho-
rised trip to America and was asked to explain the
matter to a magistrate. It transpired that Mick had
not noticed the proclamation calling on men of
military age to register, and had forgotten to get a
passport, and disappeared on a steamer without get-
ting a band to play. Whereupon the Court ordered
Mick to find £lO and told him it might have been
a century.

“Friar Tuck”: Believers and disbelievers
in premonitions of death can shape up and have
another go with the ghost of Larry Foley as the
referee. About 10 days before he died, and when
apparently in the best of health, Larrv sent for his
old-time opponent, Sandy Ross, saying he would
like t° have a final chat with him, as he felt he
would not live much longer. Sandy (who at 76 is
chucker-out at a suburban racecourse) accepted the
invitation, and the final chat duly took place.

Brisbane is to see a revival of footracing
in a week or two. The business will be run by th
men who have been behind the proceedings at Tlenf
ington (Vic.), and as they have kept things in order
there with an axe the weapon will probably be used
up north. Anyway, unless it is the revival win
quickly qualify for the crematorium, for the pro
moter of running who does not stand behind the
proceedings with a handful of sharp ironmongery
lost. A match between old man Postle and Gerald
Taylor is on the opening programme.
James O. Anderson, whose play in the
N.S.AV. Lawn Tennis Championship Tourney gives
him a claim to he considered the State’s singles
champion, goes into khaki this month. Anderson is
another of the younger generation of crack athletes
whose hopes of getting, to the top are blotted out by
the war. In halcyon times he might well have been
speculating on his chances for the Davis Cup team.
J. R. Hooper (holder) permitted 6. B
Shailer (challenger) to have only a brief look-in for
the N.S.W amateur billiard championship (3000 up)
at Sidney last week. Hooper wound up the first dav
with' 602 against 377, and the gap went on widening
until the champion ran out 866 ahead. It was one
of the biggest majorities in the history of the event
Hooper seems to be the smartest amateur the State
lias known since J. B. Belfield made his great splash
in the early ’CD's. ,

R. Leech, the Sydney professional cyclist, .
is having a great season. At each of the last three
week-ends he has won the double—mile handicap
and scratch- event —sandwiched between the football
halves; and he performed the trick once previously
Leech is easily the best pedaller in Sydney profes-
sional cycling these lean days.
* * * *
At Bathurst (N.S.W.) recently a number
of shooters took on the job of slaughtering pigeons
in aid of France, and £lOO was paid for admission
to the ground. The shoot produced a lady. Miss K.
Carr, who won the Novice Handicap, killing seven
out of nine pigeons with the first barrel. It i s
claimed that Miss Carr (who also handles a rifle
well) is the only lady pigeon-shot in Australia. This
pag» hopes so.
“C.J.W.”: It is not often anyone hears of
a fox killing a dog, but it happened a few weeks
ago, on McDonald's place at Little Plain, up Armidale
way (N.S.W.). While McDonald was riding through
a paddock he sighted a fox and despatched two grey-
hounds, which were accompanying him, to do their
worst. By biting one on the nose the stranger forced
it to stop for repairs. The other dog (evidently of
sterner stuff than his mate) followed the fox, but
omitted to return. Next day he was found dead, with
a chewed nose and severe bites on the skull.
The pairs championship in N.S.W. bowls
has been won by Williams and Young from Woonona,
down Tllawarra way. They outed some strong metro-
politan couples, and in the final won easily from
Harrison and Maclaren, of Ashfield.
* * * *
“Konini” : They come from the ends of the
earth to angle in Maoriland’s streams and lakes, and
some of the affluent tramps, particularly the English,'
make it a whole-hog outing. Recently a Johnnie
landed 1060 fish (mostly trout) in two months ! The
average was 41b. and the heaviest 111 b.
N.S.W. sporting bodies begin to substitute
War Loan Stamps and Certificates for hard cash as
prize-money. The A.J.C. makes a start by notifying
that during the coming season half of the added
money will take the form of certificates; and as the
club’s added money totalled £65,000 last year
Bigjohn Forrest is likely to say “Hear, hear!”
Among Sydney rifle-clubs the innovation goes still
further, and the whole of the prize-money will be
paid in war certificates.
WHEN KIND WAYS BROKE HIS NECK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN]
He’d a gentle eye and manner, and he never raised
a heel;
He’d respond “like a pianuer” when his rider’s touch
he’d feel;
But no stable he’s adorning and our dreams all came
to wreck
On that cold and foggy morning when old Kind?’
Ways broke his neck.
At his best the poor old neddy seemed a trifle out
of date;
Kveryone who saw him said he couldn't pull a load
of hate,;.
There was nothing of the charger in his make-up, you
could see,
And his eye was mild and larger than a horse’s eye
should be.
Now some hundredweight of kindness from a world
of rage and care
Has departed in its blindness with its four hoofs in
the air; "it* ...
Sleeping sou fidly at the ;knackcf’s, to all .joy it was a
check;
Shadows fell on Peace’s backers when old Kind Ways
broke iiis neck.

Why LooK Old?
You need not use hair dye to conceal grey hair
Grey hair adds years to anyone’s appear-
ance. The objections to dyeing the hair are
too numerous and too obvious to mention.
Few people are aware that a simple, old-
fashioned recipe, that can be made up at
home, will quickly turn grey hair back to a
natural color without injuring the hair in
any way. One has only to get two ounces
of concentrate of tammalite from the chemist
and mix it with three ounces of bay rum.
With a small sponge apply the lotion to the
hair for a few nights and the desired change
will have been neatly accomplished. This
simple formula has given great satisfac-
tion for generations to those in possession of
it. — Advt. [3

“Norbell”: I agree with “Middle Bine”
(B. 12/7/T7) that Lizette Parkes is a very
conscientious artist and a reliable “study.”
I have played with her and know. But to
absorb a fresh part every week when playing
weekly changes is nothing out of the way.
I trouped with a dramatic co. in Rockhamp-
ton (Q.) a few years ago, and we put up
two dramas a week, and kept it going for
over four months. We changed the bill on
Wednesdays and Saturdays and perpetrated
anything from “Camille” to “Robbery
Under Arms.” We never missed a night, and
we never —well, hardly ever —needed the
prompter. This is probably an Australian
record for quick studies, but I’d rather go to
work in a cow-cocky’s studio than tackle it
again.
“Melb ”; I’ve been musing on some inci-
dents of Melba’s “discovery”—Marchesi’s
exclamation to her husband, “I’ve found the
star!” This wms after Sullivan and Carl
Rosa had been contemptuous. Strakoscli
heard Melba the pupil sing in an adjoining
room. He started up and exclaimed, “Pretty
or ugly, tall or short, she’s the One!” After
our Nellie’s success in “Rigoletto,” at Brus-
sels, the French opera “Lakrne” was put in
rehearsal. The directors argued that Melba’s
French was not good enough. “Never mind,”
exclaimed Delibes. “I don't care if she sings
in Chinese or even Australianese! Nobody
but Melba!” And she knocked ’em again.
But miserably dull London —well, it doesn’t
matter now.
“C. Ockney”: Ibsen is in the ascendant in
London. The success of “Damaged Goods”
and the licensing for public performance of
“Ghosts” has created something like a boom
in the Norwegian dramatist’s works. Now
“Rosmersholm” has been staged, and is
drawing big business. Soon there will be a
rush to play “The Doll’s House,” “Hedda
Gabler” and “The Wild Duck.” After years
and years of revue Londoners had almost for-
gotten what real plays were like. Now they
.. have got them, and, to their credit, are
showing proper appreciation of the goods.
“Circle”; The pet idea of the Yankee film-
plotter is to have a sweet young thing with her
hair down deserted by the rich man’s son and
left with a child. After a bleak spell of “room-
ing,” or factory toil, she gets into the movies
and (in about 10 minutes) becomes a proud
star in a fur coat. She writes her own
“story,” acts it and then there is a private
screening in a society drawing-room. The
contrite husband, in evening dress, jumps up
and down in his chair and makes American
faces. While the function is on, a discon-
tented “clurk” is burning down his chewing-
gum factory. In the last scene the trium-
phant movie-wife hurries in covered with
furs, ospreys and diamonds, and stamps and
puts her proud chin in the air. Husband
and his aged father grovel at her feet. She
forgives them like a queen. Cheap rubbish
made for the gum-chewing American flapper,
and we pay good Australian money to see it!
“Jacko” (from the place where they
grow) : Beaumont Smith’s film, “The Hay-
seeds,” is having as successful a run in the
country as any other all-Australian produc-
tion, though the exaggeration is silently re-
sented. The general setting is right, but the
family fights and clumsy capers of the prin-
cipals are all wrong. As a typical Hayseed
Duggan wouldn’t earn tuppence, while old
Dad would figure better as a retired Ameri-
can multi-millionaire.
“Unda Studi” : Allen Doone has not been
conscripted in America yet. He opens at the
39th Theatre, New York, in six weeks’ time.
Edna Keeley will still be his “sweetest girl
in all Ireland.” Doone expects liis indispen-
sable Maurice Lynch to join him. as well as
Frank Kenny, his seven years’ treasurer.
But passports—passports!
“Mello D.” : One thing I learned in a brief
expedition into picture playing was that you
mustn’t feel or express feeling. The emo-
tional actress who begins to emote remains
to be a comedienne. If you try to feel your
part the muscles of your face or arm will
stand out like cords, your veins will swell
and your sinews and everything else about
your body which is necessary to the expres-
sion of emotional feeling will bulge abomin-
ably.
“Private Boxer”: Finero turns his hand
to any stage job these days. He reeentlv
completed a wordless play, otherwise a mod-
ern pantomime, in one act. It is “Monica’s
Blue Boy.” and the plot, though not exactlv
concerned with the war. has an echo of the
biggest drama of all in it. The music has
been supplied by the accomplished Frederic
Cowen.

THE SECRET OF SUCCESS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I knew the usher well. He seemed
A nice, mild-mannered man;
But one night in the chill d.c.
He spoke in bitter terms to me
Anent the aeting clan.
“My, oath,” he said, “you take my tip,
They fairly give a cove the pip!
“Look at that marionette; he thinks
He’s made this blanky play ;
But, Lord! he cannot act for nuts.
He swanks about and smirks and struts
And kids he earns his pay;
But if the blighter only knew,
This show’s success to me is due 1
“You critics, too, are bluffed. You said
The op’ning act was slow,
But Stormer, in the leading part,
Once more revealed his matchless art,
And made the play a go.
It wasn’t ’im that was the cause,
But me, who started the applause!”
Victoria.
HARRISON O.

ANSWERS TO CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character ) will be
taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on the MSS. A o
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted; and no MS. will be returned
unless stamps accompany.
Business Man : We certainly would like a
sworn statement that you have been unable to get
your first payment of 2s. 6d. per bushel... .H.N.:
“Camp” is, in various N. S. Wales and South
Queensland abo. dialects, gonyi, koiyong, kirami and
nurra. “House” is much the same, with the addi-
tions of tura and kokere. But for something extra
for “house,” there’s the Vic. burburnonook or
porpornduk.... 1147 : You don’t make out a case.
You don’t even allege that he is an alien enemy or
attempt to show that his sympathies are anti-Allies.
Can’t attack a Danish-born naturalised Australian
because you don’t like him.-,. .F.W.J. (Eng.): No
interest to Australia.... Miss L. C.: Can’t read it.

A mention in dispatches:—
Please don’t overlook the claims of Miss Hilda
Schaper, of Lubeek (Vie.), when compiling a list of
the Anzac Sisterhood. She has three brothers. Two
are at the 1 ront, and the other would be there too
if he had the arm he lost some years ago. Mother
is a widow and can’t get any help; so the Aus-
tralian girl slogs along behind the plough and rides
on the seat of’ the drill. I don’t know exactly how
many acres she has drilled, but when she has finished
there won’t be any shortage in the acreage. She
feeds, harnesses and beds the horses, looks after
the sheep, milks the cows, gathers the eggs, and,
when all the outside jobs are done, goes inside and
does a bit of housework, sometimes topping off the
day’s performance by writing cheerful letters to the
boj's in France.
O.K. : Talking of Royal Families and
the Quaint rules that keep them holy, one of
the most humorous customs applies to the
dukedoms that are created for the monarch’s
younger sons. The first and second holders
of 'the precious title are Royal Highnesses,
aru l the> rank in the House of Lords as
“Illustrious” dukes. But after the second
t,( netatjon^. the illustriousness evaporates
Iving s great grandson becomes only
his Grace.’ like a mere ordinary duke
whose family pride started in a contractor,.
All of which British-born mummery is as
nothing compared Avith the varieties of rov-
alt.v and serenity in Germany, where the
frightfulness comes from. The odd *hk«A
is that it should have survived in Britain’
the open ridicule which, has ..ao.t yet hern
attempted in the remaining foreign
dominions of crowned Majesty.,.. Why, when
rvnbad s pantomime mother gets wrecked
on the island of Rumtifoo. >hc usually
addresses the reigning sovereign as “Dis-
gustrious Prince, ’ or “Inane Highness,” or
Most Assaulted Disgrace” or whatnot. And
the gallery sees the joke. But perhaps it
thinks that even ; ;a' burlesque.- title is not
more ridiculous than of- the—knight --
hoods in the latest list of Birthday Honors.

Government Notices.
Department of the Navy,
Melbourne, 17th July, 1917.
Appointment of Superintending Civil
Engineer—Henderson Naval Base.
A FULLY-QUALIFIED CIVIL ENGI-
-."i- NEER is required for the position of
Superintending Civil Engineer of the exten-
sive works in progress and projected in con-
nection with the Henderson Naval Base.
Cockburn Sound, Western Australia.
The projected works comprise heavy
breakwaters, deep-water quay walls, exten-
sive excavations and reclamations, extensive
dredging and Floating Docks.
Applications are invited from Civil Engi-
neers who have had experience in heavy
Engineering Works and the management and
control of men. Only those who have had
this experience and have held responsible
positions will be considered. Preference will
be given, all other things being equal, to
members of the Institute of Civil Engineers.
The salary will be £750 per annum and a
residence, the salary rising by annual incre-
ments on approved service to £9OO per
annum.
Applications, stating age and giving full
particulars of training and experience and
qualifications, and enclosing copies of testi-
monials, of which not less than two must be
recent, should be addressed to the Naval
Secretary, Navy Office, Melbourne, and
delivered not later than 14tli August, 1917-
Applications to be endorsed, “Applica-
tions for Superintending Civil Engineer.”
JOSEPH COOK,
Minister of State for the Navy.

IN BANANA LAND
“A.L.” : When Ryan’s Government decided
to open several butcher’s-shops in Brisbane
meat was about as high as when the cow
jumped over the moon. But prices came
down with a run, and meat is now as cheap
in Brisbane as in any capital city in Aus-
tralia, and a lot cheaper than it is in some
of them. Tory politicians and Tory papers
deprecate this “unwarranted interference
with Private Enterprise”—and then send the
missus round to the interference to buy the
family’s fodder.
The Gas Act of 1916 has already borne
fruit. Under it no gas co. may charge more
than, it dll on November 1, 1915, unless
granted permission by the Executive Coun-
cil. The South Brisbane Co. sent a man to
fix up a. shilling-in-the-slot machine, and he
put in new cogwheels which had the effect of
supplying less, gas for the bob. By a bit of
bad luck the chief gas-examiner just then
came along, and as a result the co. was sued
and fined £5 and costs.
A deputation which the other day talked
to Minister for Mines Jones about the South
Ulam marble quarries had weird ideas regard-
ing Australia’s consumption of tombstones.
This country, it was alleged, paid about
£2,000,000 a year to Italy alone for marble.
It hasn’t paid £2,000,000 for marble in all
its life. In fact, since this country became
a Commonwealth it hasn’t paid Italy
£2,000.000 if it put all the bills together—
the bills for olive oil and macaroni, for hats
and matches, for tartaric acid and motor-
cars. The Italian marble trade with Aus-
tralia is worth about £30,000 or £40,000 a
year. Still, figures don’t matter. Australia
ought to use vastly more marble than it
does; and some day it will, and with the
object of finding out what chance Ulam. in
the Rockhampton district, has of supplying
the demand, the Minister has promised to
send up .an expert to make a report.
Queensland Mines Minister Jones has been
inquiring into The Bulletin’s allegation
that Australian manufacturers are seriously
threatened by the shortage of pig-iron; and
it is highly probable that, if the money diffi-
culty can be got over, he will build a fur-
nace. He fin4s that something to produce pig
wouldn’t run into many thousands; and it
would be an excellent foundation for a steel
industry later on. One thing he is in no
doubt about-—the quality of the available
ore.
“Barcoo” : The universally-detested carrion
crow is at last being placed on his rightful
pedestal on Bananaland sheep-runs. On
many stations thb order of the sack is now
handed out to any person killing the bird.
Dead sheep are the depository of millions
of blowfly eggs, and in a very short time the
paunches are a living mass of prospective
sheep-destroyers. But frequently before they
have time to get a move on the raucous
gentleman arrives with some mates for
breakfast, and there is a clean plate when
they take their departure.
“Con Drew” : Randolph Bedford’s remarks
(B. 19/7/’l7) on fishing off Caloundra Head
swing the clock back 30 years or more to the
days when we angled off that same promon-
tory. There was an inexhaustible Supply of
fish there then, but mostly inside the head,
in the bay fronting Landsborough’s land-
grant. Old William Landsborough, the ex-
plorer, has long since gone. His eldest son
Sidney shouldered a gun in the S’African
squabble, and passed out at Bloemfontein.
Dead, at 78, Charles A. Thomas, of Pitts-
worth, one of the earliest settlers on the
Darling Downs. Incidentally, he ran the
first mail coach from Leyburn to Goondi-
windi, from Warwick to Western Creek, and
from Leyburn to Ashford (N.S.W.). That
was well over half a century ago, when the
only track was a string of marked trees,
obligingly blazed by Binghi.
A recent official statement indicates that
the cattle-tick came to Australia from Java
in 1872 and that the Queensland damage
from the pest is estimated at £7,000.000.
This takes no account of tick-fever
mortality. Damage to hides alone in
the Northern State is supposed to run to
about a hundred thousand per annum. Can
nothing be done? The United States claim
to have cleared about 250,000 square miles
of the pest in the last 10 years.
“P.C.deC.”: Recently Cloncurry smelters
were threatened with paralysis through lack
of coke. When Mines Minister Jones tried
to straighten matters out, he found N. S.
Wales colliery-owners had combined to ex-
tort extravagant prices from his State, be-
lieving, some of them, that Bananaland coal
was unsuitable for coke-making. At the
same time, tnese mine-owners were exporting
THE REWARD OF IMPATIENCE.
DE BORE: “I have been horribly insulted. One of the members offered me fifty pounds
to resign my membership of the club! What would you advise me to do?”
DE BEERE : “ Wait a bit. You’ll get a better offer.”
coke to America under special permit from
Prime Minister Hughes, who apparently had
been persuaded that Australian fuel could
be better used in the United States than in
keeping alive an important Commonwealth
interest. Then tests showed Ipswich coke
to be at least equal to the Ma State product;
and arrangements were forthwith made for
the supoly of 50,000 tons per annum for
Cloncurry. But the Shipping Combine re-
fused to carry the stuff, and simultaneously
sprang an extra 2s. 6d. per ton freight on
the coal it had been carrying north for
railway purposes. Jones promptly chartered
the s.s. Allinga, and she is now making two
trips per month. The Railway Department
is being savrnd in coal freight alone at the
rate of £12,750 per annum, employment has
been preserved for some thousands of people
in an inaccessible corner of the continent,
the output of more than a thousand tons
of copper monthly is being maintained for
war purposes, the people of North Queens-
land are being better supplied than formerly
in transport facilities, and particularly in
regard to cargo coming south, and the char-
tering of the ship is likely to return a small
d’rect profit to the State.
Dusk.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Beside the trees the darkening waters sweep,
But linger lovingly awhile to sing;
The eddies purl an evening litany
To the tall irises, whose blossoms bring
Among the reeds a color melody,
And to the dimming gold of cornfields creep.
Beneath the dreamy sky the iris seems
A mourner, clad in hue of royal woe—
A symbol calm of our deep agony
That yet is purple proud. For those who go
Blaz n the right by their brave destiny.
And nearer bring the harvest of our dreams.
QUEENSLAND.
KATHLEEN ROSE FRANCIS.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, July 21, 1017.
My Dear Thargomindah.—
Our substitutes for Royalty did a few odd
jobs last week, including time and a halt at
two annual meetings —the Red Cross
Society's and the Lady Bowen Hospital. On
Thursday night his Ex. hoisted Ins swag tor
Rockhampton, Blackall, Alice Downs and
Barcaldine. He expects to do good biz at
the Show at the last-named pitch.
J. D. Connolly, the new Agent-General for
Westralia, is an old Allora boy. He received
a stirring welcome when he looked in a few
days ago on his way to the great target foi
the Hun aircraft. Mother's house is shelter-
ing him at present.
Yesterday was Flag Day. The collection
f or the Red Cross —amounted to over ±o4U
when I last saw the tally.
The juvenile fancy dress ball in aid oi
the War Nurses’ Fund, held last night m
the Centennial Hall, will give a decent lift
to another good cause. Mrs. J. Stodart was
the organiser. •
Father Byrne, who recently was raised to
the dignity' of Monsignor, has been given
charge of the hi? parish of Ipswich: His
friends farewelled him in the Centennial
Hall on Wednesday night, and amongst other
gifts handed him a motor-car—not to speed
him away, but to encourage him to make
Hying trips back.
Mrs. A. A. Doyle, one of our prettiest
matrons, was hostess at a tea at the Cooee
Caf6 on Friday. She proudly received con-
gratulations that her young son, Lieut. Doyle,
with the R.F.A., has been decorated with
the Military Cross. Another of our boys,
Lieut. Ken McSwaine, has gained a like dis-
tinction.
Miss Florence Fuller was “at home” to
art lovers at Montpelier last Monday, and
her display of dainty miniature painting on
ivory was a source of envy and admiration.
I told you about a cat fight at a gathering
of Conscription v. Anti-Conscription females
last week. Well, the other night in the
Assembly the Home Secretary was asked if
he would give instructions that police be
detailed to attend these stadiums with orders
to arrest any real lady guilty of bullocky or
insulting language. The Minister gravely
replied that the matter would have his con-
sideration. So if you get an urgent ware
from me, my dear, you’ll know that I need
bail.
John N. McCallum has got together
new comedy eo. and on August 4 will inn/
duce “The Courtiers” at Cremorne Garden*
The “Turn to the Right” Co. farewelled with
“The New Henrietta.” The opinions on the
latter piece ranged from neutral to lu-ia
hut you’ll see for yourself before long. ‘ ’
The three Cherniavsky boys have been the
attractions of the week. To-night the season
ends at the Exhibition Ilall. The opening
night of the Follies is happening at the
Tivoli, and Wirths’ Circus is brightening
Musgrave Park.
Last Monday afternoon Leon Berliner
tea-ed the Cherniavsky trio at Lennon's
Hotel. The guests enjoyed, as well as any-
thing else, the singing of “The Garden of
Years” by Miss Lena Hammond, words and
music by Berliner. The Russian musicians
said such nice things to the composer that he
flushed a lobster-pink.
Young Eric White, a sunny-hearted lad
of Toowong, killed in France on April 2
was a grandson of the Becketts, of old St
James’s. Melbourne. Grandpa Beckett was
on the staff of the Age ; but he took almost
as much interest as his wife did in St
James’s Girls’ School, of which she was
headmistress. He died in 1872; she, four
years later.
Brig.-General C. J. (“Cam”) Robertson
C. of Toowoomba, has been awarded the
D. ; two sons of Major A. Clowes, 0 f
Warwick —Capts. Cyril and Norman—have

AUSTRALIA JANE.
“Maranoa”: Roma is sometimes satirical
and other times blasphemous when it men-
tions the subdivision of the Government-
purchased cattle-station, Mount Hutton. The
holding consists of some 400 sq. miles of, for
the most part, second-rate cattle country.
On this Minister for Lands Hunter has con-
ceived the idea of settling some 300 families
(presumably returned soldiers), on blocks of
from (540 to 1280 acres, some of the former
being as much as 25 miles from the proposed
railway. Local landholders consider not
less than 1280 acres of the better class of
country necessary as a living area, even if
handy to the proposed line, and from 3000 to
20,000 acres further back, according to the
quality of the country. A returned soldier
in Roma informed me the other day that he
had been allotted 1200 acres of grazing coun-
try (leasehold, of course) some miles out
from town. He expects to borrow “a few
quid” from the Agricultural Bank or the Re-
patriation Fund or something to fence it.
water it. build a house on it and stock itl
About 400 sheep is the carrying capacity of
this particular area in fair seasons, and a
man couldn’t get a meal a day off that num-
ber. It is another hint of the millions we
are going to lose, “a few quid” here and
another “few quid” there, while putting Bill-
jim to tackle a job where a right amount of
success is impossible.

“Wart”: For some years Launceston was
without even one pawnbroker; and now that
it has two it can’t run a synagogue. There
is a building, certainly, but it is 30 years
since a service was held there, and the trus-
tees are applying to the Court for permission
to sell the property. Ten years ago I tried
to hire it for a boxing carnival, but every
body was shocked at the idea, although I was
willing to open the service each evening by
reading a chapter from the Book of Jeremiah.
“F.N.T.”: The “malicious or criminally
careless” person who scares kids, by an
alleged spider in the cupboard, say, or a
promise of a monster that will creep out of
the dark (B. 5/7/T7), comes in all garbs.
The worst sleep-killing 1 had as a child was
caused by a South Sea Island missionary
who, at a children’s service, told us lots of
painfully interesting, purple-grey things
about Island customs and religious rites,
and gave us an exhibition of idols. While
he was showing us the biggest of these, the
venerable old man (yes, it was the late
Baton) mentioned how sometimes Island
brother was buried alive by his fellow-
countrymen. Gr-r-rrr! It was the first
time I had ever heard of anyone being
treated in this inconsiderate way; and that
night and for many nights afterwards I
absolutely refused to go to sleep in my own
little room for fear of being dragged and
treated to the same indelicate attention.
“Widgee”: Talking about why men enlist.
Bill S , a pal of mine, was a house-
painter. His wife says he went to the war
“to get away from the lead for a while.”
From lan Hamilton’s report on Defence
in Australia:—
The centralisation in the Defence Department at
Melbourne exceeds anything 1 have experienced dur-
ing more than 40 years' service in India, in the
United Kingdom, and in every part of the world
where troops, administered by the British War Office,
are stationed. Petty questions, which in a well-
ordered business would be dealt with locally, are
referred from districts to headquarters.
That is how Hamilton found it; and instead
of improving, the wretched thing has got
worse.
It was “Buck to the Army Again, Ser-
geant,’’ for Charles D. Rolfe at Melbourne
the other day. Born in South Yarra 37
years ago, he butted into the S’African war
under Colonel Tom Price, and with another
Australian, E. E. Hurst, subsequently took
up 25.000 acres in Rhodesia. There they
stayed till Wilhelm fired his mine, where-
upon Hurst went to England, joined the
Horse Guards, and in France gained his
commission and the D.S.O. Rolfe, though
married, offered his services to the S’ African
Expeditionary Force, but was rejected. So
he brought his well-knit frame and his know-
ledge of five languages to Australia, where
the army to which he belongs by birthright
welcomed him.
Back from two years’ service in Mesopo-
tamia, where hearts and bodies have been
broken, Chaplain-Captain O. W. Birch is
having a month’s spell in Melbourne.
Though it was “an awful nightmare” in
1915, organisation and skill have, he says, at
last triumphed. And nobody has triumphed
more than the Australian airmen and wire-
less experts. The latter would halt, unload
their equipment (collapsible mast and all)
from packhorses, and in 15 minutes, their
plant installed, start a “pitch” with a station
100 miles away. Once, getting under shell-
fire, they packed up and were on trek in
three minutes! Birch, who is a Bendigo boy
educated at Caulfield Grammar, was four
years a padre with the Indian army
before the Kaiser began defiling the world’s
altars. He was in the Boer War, wears the
Queen’s Medal with five bars, and, as one of
the 150 Australian soldiers at Edward Til’s
coronation, had the commemorative medal
pinned on his chest by the late royal good
fellow.
For a couple of generations Dr. William
Fortune has been President of All Hal-
lows Foreign Missionary College, near
Dublin, and sending to Australia three-
fourtbs of the priests who have come in
here as “seculars”—that is, not belonging
to any religious order. Ask any young or
middle-aged R.C. priest if he knew Dr.
Fortune of All Hallows. “To be sure. I
did,” he will smile. “Didn’t I ‘read’ under
him at Drmncondra?” Dr. Fortune had a
personal connection with Australia. lie was
appointed Bishop of Sandhurst (Bendigo);
but he' never saw the golden city. The
All Hallows people didn’t want to lose
William, so they petitioned the Pope to
leave him alone—and he was left alone
and died peaceably at Drumcondra. Only
one of the original directors of All Hallows
remains on earth —Dr. Del any, R.C. Arch-
bishop of Hobart.
Philip S. Seager, who has put in 54 years
in the Speck’s public service, and has been
Registrar of the Supreme Court for half a
life-time, retired the other day. He was a
leading light in the body that used to run
the tourist business in Hobart before the
Government took the job on.
Adelaide sees before it an engineering
problem. Its River Torrens has no mouth.
When it gets near the sea, six miles away,
it is blocked by high sandhills, spreads into
reed-beds, and works away gradually to the
Port River northward and the Patawa-
longa southward. The town of Henley
Beach has established itself on those sand-
hills, and is fast spreading over them. With
wet winters like this and the last, the new
houses are getting flooded ; and the crowded
trams are carried over ground by a viaduct
which was nearly covered last week. To
make a definite mouth for the river must
always have been difficult, and is now prac-
tically impossible, so all that can be done is
to drain the reed-beds scientifically into the
two above-named outlets.
“Dimitri Pushoff”: The doings of the
Spanish Censor (B. 5/7/T7) recall the
efforts of his Russian brother just before the
revolution. One day in the Duma the racket
was like that at a meat auction in Berlin.
On the following day the Vecheknie Vremya
reported the disturbance in nearly four
columns headed “Echoes of the Duma.” Only
one deafening statement had dodged the cen-
sor, and it stood in lonely magnificence in
the fourth column: “One of the speakers
declaimed some verses of Pushkin about
running streams.”

THE SECRET OF A
GOOD COMPLEXION.
By MME. ST. CLAIRE.
Any woman nowadays may quickly reju-
venate her complexion'at home by a safe and
painless process of gentle absorption. The
days of expensive and dangerous “operations”
are past, and each woman can be her own
beauty doctor. It has been discovered that
ordinary mercolised wax, applied like cold
cream, at night, will cause- the thin veil of
lifeless, sallow or blotchy cuticle to gradually
and gently flake off in invisible, flour-like
particles, revealing the fresh, vigorous and
beautiful young skin underneath. Mercolised
wax absorbs only the unhealthy and un-
sightly outer fi’m, and does not affect the
healthy tissue at all.
The process defies detection, and unveils
a lasting and beautiful complexion, which is,
of course, a perfectly natural one. For this
purpose thousands of women are now using
mercolised. wax, which may be obtained at
any chemist’s. Naturally the process also
removes all such surface blemishes as
freckles, blotches, pimples, tan, liver spots,
etc. It makes the face look years younger
and prettier, and renders the use of face
creams unnecessary.
* * * *
Another valuable piece of information,
which many women will appreciate, concerns
the success of the new treatment for super-
fluous hair. A remedy for permanently erad-
icating this ugly form of'facial disfigurement
now T seems to have been found. It is simpli-
city itself. About one ounce of ordinary pure
powdered pheminol, which can be obtained
at any chemist’s, is all that is needed to put
one in the way of completely ridding oneself
of superfluous hair. When applied to the
obnoxious growth this causes it to immedi-
ately disappear, and the continuance of the
recommended treatment is said to finally
destroy the hair roots, so that there will be
no renewal of the growth. [5

The Inky Way.
“Chin-chin” : So “Den” is marriecj. Well,
the Sentimental Bloke prophesied it when he
observed “The commin end of most of us is
‘tart’!” A dark, clever, handsome girl is his
Doreen. Meanwhile the book output is not
neglected. “The Glugs of Gosh” have passed
the 24,000 mark, and of the Christmas book.
“Doreen,” over 20,000 copies have been
ordered. The illustrations are by Hal Gye,
who has done them well. “Den” pointed out
to him as a model a sweet little girl in a
Swanston-street shop, and Hal memorised
her—for it was not possible, since she and
they were “not intrajuiced,” to employ her
on the usual basis. So, unknown to her, Hal
stole the beauty of her look, and some day
she may find herself famous.
* * * *
Leon Gellert, of S. Aus., whose short
realistic pieces, written on Gallipoli and in
hospital, are the first effort to win the
Bundey prize at Adelaide University, is
having an enlarged edition of his “Songs of
a Campaign” brought out by Angus and
Robertson.
Queensland police have just started a
Journal, said to be the only trades-union
paper for policemen on earth. Its motto is
“Unity is Strength,” and it illustrates the
remark by showing a bundle of batons.
# » # •#
The very resourceful W. K. Harris, who
once upon a time went touring the back-
blocks of this country in The Bulletin’s
interest, and wrote a book about a trip he
had from Newcastle to Adelaide, has won a
bar to his Military Cross. As a change from
overlanding, he is in the navy these days.
Adelaide Herald has had to stand an
unusual libel-action. It printed a spirited
account of fin election meeting, with details
of the excited behavior of a lady whose name
was given. As a matter of fact, she was
named from the platform by the elected
member, who was returning thanks. She
claimed £lOO damages for having disorderly
conduct attributed to her, but after a long
hearing Judge Buchanan found that the
Herald’s report, though it “might appear a
little colored,” was “fair and accurate.”
Judgment for the paper with costs.
Leonard Matters, of an Adelaide family
which produced a well-known suffragette,
has had a send-off and presentation at
Buenos Ayres, where he was editor of the
Herald. 111-health sends him and his wife
—who is literary too —back to Australia.
Blackburn, M.L.A., a long time ago gave
the Argus a chance to be humorously
didactical at his expense. It was on a
question of pure English. Blackburn had
declared his belief that “even a German
conquest of Australia would leave it hardly
—if at all—-worsened.” Often Grandmamma
has giggled at this word “worsened,” and at
last its author could bear it no longer. “You
deride my ‘peculiar English,’ ” he wrote
passionately to Grandmamma. “The word
‘worsen’ has, it seems, no place in the
vocabulary of the journalist. It is none
the worse for that. Approved by Milton,
Johnson, de Quincey, Southey, Carlyle and
Stevenson, this vigorous and simple English
word need not fear the journalist’s frown.”
As far as The Bulletin is concerned
Blackburn may say worsen so long as he
doesn’t do it in advocating principles which
may compel this country to also say Hoch !
* • * •
The genius at the other end of the morn-
ing papers’ cable service has taken to send-
ing messages anent new war poems. The
main “points” are set forth as though it
were a Royal Commission’s report.
F’rinstance, the Argus the other day:—
The Morning Post publishes a poem in which Mr.
Rudyard Kipling bitingly denounces the Mesopotamia
horrors. He says that the dead heroes cannot return,
and asks whether the slothful politicians and admini-
strators will be allowed to slide softly back into
power by the favor and connivance of their kind.
If it had only occurred to the cable people
to send a message when “Poems and Bal-
lads” was published we might have got some-
thing like this: —
Mr. A. Swinburne has published a poem warmly
eulogising Dolores (?). He asks that she should
touch his roses and make them redder. He also
states that while the priests gave seven sorrows to
their virgin, the sins of Dolores total 490.
The Argus would probably have added a
footnote saying that, as a search of “Who’s
Who” and the “Statesman’s Year Book” had
failed to reveal any mention of Dolores, it
was not unlikely that the name had been
mutilated in transmission.
WHY DO YE SING?
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Why do ye sing of raven locks
And lips of rosy red
When every clay the fields are strewn
With piles of noble dead,
And ev’ry night is dropping tears
Upon their hero bed?
There is a dawn for dalliance set,
There is an hour for joy;
But now we call for noble souls
That seek a high employ.
This is a day for bitten lips
That will not kiss and toy.
It irks me in these deathful days
To hear the laughing lyre,
When Honor cries for bloody bays
And breasts of noble ire
And souls that fling on dauntless wing
Across the gulfs of fire.
It may be Phyllis sings as sweet
As in the days of old;
It may be that her tresses meet
In many a twine of gold ;
I only know that bugles blow
And gallant hearts are cold.
Why do ye sing of raven locks
And lips of rosy red,
When ev’ry day the fields are strewn
With piles of noble dead,
And ev’ry night is dropping tears
Upon their hero bed?
QUEENSLAND.
F. BORN.

Mrs. J. M. Gregg’s Twins, who were
cured of Bronchitis by
Dr. Sheldon’s Hew Discovery
“My twins, a boy and a girl, were very bad with severe colds and bronchitis for
months,” writes Mrs. J. M. Gregg, 12 Bradshaw Street, Caversham, Soiuth Dunedin,
N.Z. “Their coughing was terrible to hear, and caused them to go blacTs in the face,
and, after one of these bouts they would be quite exhausted for some time. Their
little chests and throats were very sore. They were under medical treatment, but
with no beneficial results, and we tried every known remedy without any real benefits.
I was worried very much over them, but one day I took up a paper and saw Dr.
Sheldon’s New Discovery advertised, and am very pleased, for it has proved a great
blessing. I procured a bottle, and after using very little I saw a great change in
them, and by the time the second bottle was finished they were in good health and
completely cured, thanks to that wonderful medicine, Dr. Sheldon’s New Discovery.
I would strongly recommend all mothers and those having the care of children to
always keep a bottle in the house, as I intend to do myself, and I wish Dr. Sheldon's
New Discovery every success.”
What Do You Give Baby for His Cold ?
Foolish mothers dose their babies with home-made concoctions, and perhaps upset the little
stomach and make the baby cross and uncomfortable by this mistaken economy. Wise
mothers do not experiment with untried nostrums, but depend on a remedy which has been
compiled from the scientific prescription of a famous physician, and is designed to cure the
cold without upsetting the digestive organs. Dr. Sheldon’s New Discovery is this Remedy, and
mothers may give it to their babies, secure in the knowledge that coughs and colds will no
longer rack the little bodies. For croup, it has the most wonderful results. Whooping Cough
is cut short by the use of Dr. Sheldon’s New Discovery. For all the family ills resulting from
throat, chest or lung complaint the mother’s resource is Dr. Sheldon’s New Discovery.
Dr. Sheldon’s New’ Discovery is the quickest, safest and best-known
Remedy for Coughs, Colds, Pleurisy, Bronchitis and all Throat and Chest
Troubles, and can alw’ays be relied upon. Obtainable at all Chemists and
Storekeepers at 1/6 and 3/- a bottle.
