South of Gaza.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
FARED we afield in the gathering dusk
from the lines in the rail-head camp,
And we dimly heard through the dust
we stirred a battalion out on tramp.
The big guns cooled in the Turkish lines
’mid the green hills hidden away,
While the steadfast course of the Anzac
Horse was east till the dawn of day !
The limbers swung through the smoke that
hung on the worn, shell-crippled track,
And a smothering pall of darkness fell that
gripped the earth in a sombre spell,
Till the sleepy west bade night farewell as
the coals of day burned black.
Over the unfenced barleyfields we rode
through the hoof-woke night.
The mist rose high till the star-gemmed sky
was veiled in a film of white.
The mist fell low till it blurred the grass
and scumbled about our course,
And the squadrons streamed till the riders
seemed mute ghosts of a phantom force.
We rode till the grey of a dawning day
awakened the drowsy earth ;
Till the snarling, hot-lipped guns behind
made haste in the waxing light to find
The range of enemy humankind with thun-
dering gusts of mirth.
Up from a deep-worn watercourse we climbed
to a rolling lea,
While the mounts remained in the dip, close-
reined each one to its “number three.’’
We swept through the waist-high barley-
grass that waved in the breeze of morn,
While the larks on high carolled down the
sky and the quail whirred up from the
corn.
The rustle of feet in the bearded wheat
spread out w T here the rifles trailed.
A squadron of taubes was on the wing: ere
the Turkish lead began to sing
The big guns smothered the voice of spring
and the screaming shells assailed.
Down we crouched in the shivering grass
while the quaking hills ahead
Were battered ablaze till a blood-red haze
crept up like a pall of dread.
Our guns waxed loud and fierce behind to
hammer us out a track,
To double the chance of a swift advance for
the ghastly trench attack.
Over the dim far ridge’s rim the sun swung
aloft through smoke
That dimmed the vision of anxious eyes, till
... of the grass were seen to rise
Brown men in a trice of dumb surprise when
the Turkish batt’ries spoke.
Never before those ancient hills were rent in
a storm so loud,
So splattered with lead by cordite sped nor
wrapped in a battle-shroud.
To left and right as far as the eye could see
on the hazy flanks,
The shrapnel broke in a swirl of smoke on
the forward surging ranks.
We forged ahead where the poppies red
resembled the wasting blood
Of men who staggered and pitched and lay
to rise no more and view the array
Of mates who passed through the' dust away
in a panting human flood.
Down on the lead-whipped ground again we
fell for the want of breath ;
While the far-flung ranks whistled in their
flanks we witnessed a feast of death.
A thing crawled over the hill and climbed
through a tangled mass of wire—
A “tank” with a stream of smoke abeam and
its chattering front afire.
It lumbered along through a startled throng
and sickled them down like wheat,
And the Turkish shells rained back to break
the lines that came in its deadly wake,
Till the torn earth seemed to cringe and
quake beneath their tottering feet.
Out on the flank a tide of life lept up from
the earth once more.
'Twas a grim sight then, and Royston’s men
swept wide like a wave on shore.
The white steel flashed when the blades came
out, and the troops streamed over the
rise,
Till the rearmost man w T as lost to sight of
the watching bloodshot eyes.
Out of the vale in a shrapnel gale the led
mounts moved in the rear.
The whole line surged and straggled and
flowed downhill to the old Beersheeba
road
Where each fell under his final load who
failed to emerge all clear.
We clung to the hard-won hills that day till
the drooping sun waxed pale.
We’d pressed the attack tilll the Turks hung
back sore rent from the blown-out gale.
Only the snipers’ farewell shots were echo-
ing sharply then,
And the west was bright with relief in sight
for the watching, worn-out men.
We marched away at the close of day to
mount in the dying light:
We rode aflank through the tense, grey
gloom with plenty of sad, spare saddle-
room—-
The swift hoofs sounded a roll of doom to
the Turkish arms that night!
PALESTINE.
GERARDY.
The Automatic Barmaid.
[FOR TEH BULLETIN.]
RILEY, of the Harp of Erin, shoved the
last of his lingering customers out
as the Post Office clock was striking
six, and closed and bolted his moral bar door.
•‘To the divil with their Early Closing!”
muttered the old publican. “There won’t
be a living for a man in this trade if they
keep on the way they’re doin’!”
“Are they all out, Michael?”
Riley turned and nodded sadly at his grey
old wife. “They are! A year ago they’d
just be cornin’ in: but these are war times
an’ taxation’s heavy, and a man has to
turn trade away from his door. There’s
nothin’ in it any more, Kate.”
Mrs. Riley sighed and wiped her hands
on her apron. “I’m sure I don’t know what
they’ll be wantin’ next! Min used not to be
afraid of drink.”
“Ah, it’s not th’ min that’s afraid. It’s
th’ dirty wowsers that never takes a drink
that’s afraid.”
“Well, whichever way it is, they’ve shut
us up and we’ve got to make th’ best of it.
I’ve had a hard day, Michael—that back
bar’s a man’s job.”
“Well, please God, you won’t have to do
it again. Is that felly goin’ t’ work soon,
d’ye know?”
“He’s at it now, puttin’ down th’ rails for
it to run on. Ye’d better go down now and
see his min aren’t takin’ anything. There’s a
lot of things they might help themselves to
and you not be any the wiser. Go along
now and see they’re not helpin’ themselves
to your cigars.”
Mrs. Riley turned and toiled wearily up-
stairs while fat Riley bustled out to the
closed back bar. Behind the counter a dusty
young man, with his coat off and a folded
footrule protruding from his hip-pocket, was
directing the laying of what looked like* a
miniature railway track. Two younger assist-
ants were doing the work.
“How are ye gettin’ on?” queried the boss,
looking over the counter.
“All right, Mr. Riley. We’ll have her
mounted on the rails presently, and, as soon
as we have her wired we’ll be able to give
you a trial. Looks all right, doesn’t she?
The two men stepped down to the end of
the bar, where a sort of super-waxwork lady,
very stylishly dressed and full in the bust,
stared and waited to be set to work.
“She’s a fine-lookin’ lump of a girl,” mur-
mured Riley; “but I don't see why I should
buy her rale jool’ry—she’s not human and
full of vanity like flesh an’ blood.”
“Now, that’s where you make a mistake,
sir. You want her to look like a real
woman, an’ jewellery is part of her make-
up. You never saw a barmaid without jew-
ellery, did you?”
“Never.”
“Just so. Well, if you want her to look
like the real thing, put a tenner’s worth of
stuff on her. She won’t run away with it,
you know.”
“True for you, and I suppose she’ll have
to have it. That’ll make £7O for her com-
plete, an’ I only hope she doesn’t strike.”
“She won’t strike. You come down in an
hour’s time and I’ll have her all ready for a
trial.” / ,
“I’ll go and get my tea, then,” said the
publican, and left them to their work.
“Have they got that machine fixed yet?”
asked Mrs. Riley fretfully when he got up-
stairs. Being an old-timer, modern restric-
tions and innovations of the trade worried
her.
“In an hour’s time. An’ ye must get out
of the way of callin’ her ‘the machine.’ Sure.
I told ye before we must keep up th’ fiction
that Gwennie is a rale live girl. Min don’t
like to be waited on by machinery.”
Mrs. Riley laughed scornfully. “If th’
min can’t tell that thing isn’t a rale woman
they’ve altered since my day.”
“Never mind about that. What I say is
we’ve got to do our best and not be tellin’
every Tom, Dick an’ Harry that comes near
th’ place.”
“Well. I’ll tell y’ this, Riley: I’ll not call
her by any name -when there’s nobody by to
hear me. After all th’ years we’ve been in
this house an’ th’ fine-lookin' girls we’ve had,
to come down to a dirty sham like that, with
her inside full of springs an’ cog-wheels, an’
for all that wearin’ a low-necked blouse as
any girl would be proud to own—seems like
idolatry, so it does.”
“I don’t like it meself, Kate. But what
with wages goin’ up an’ profits cornin’ down.
I’ve got to take the cheapest way out, an’
that’s it. Maybe we’ll get used to it as we
did to th’ cash register.”
The Rileys had their evening meal in sad
silence, and were deep in dreams of gayer
days when there was a tap at the door.
“We’re ready for you now, Mr. Riley,” said
the inventor.
“Right you are! Come along, Kate, an’
let’s see this girl work.”
The party proceeded downstairs to the
back bar and found the new barmaid stand-
ing ready behind the counter. The inventor
expounded proudly: “Now you see her wait-
ing for a customer. As he steps on the door-
plate here, she glides forward a little as if
eager to take his order. As he pronounces it
the sound-waves strike a sensitive receiver
concealed in her ear—the left one here, near-
est the door. This is passed on. by our
patent device and she moves her right arm
towards the bottle required and her left to-
wards the clean glasses. Of course, as I
have already explained, the bottles and
glasses have to be arranged always in the
same way. We guarantee”
“Let's see her work !” interrupted Riley.
“Very good, sir. Now please watch me
closely.” The installer passed out through
the swing doors and immediately returned,
being very careful to press heavily on the
broad door-plate. The barmaid moved for-
ward smoothly like a lift and the installer
leant on the counter. “Sni-per’s whi-sky and
wa-ter !” he enunciated very distinctly. The
barmaid glided back, made the proper passes
with her arms and delivered the goods.
“Love-ly da-ay!” she sighed as she put the
whisky down, and Mrs. Riley gave a slight
jump of surprise. The money was taken up
with a sweeping action, registered, and the
change delivered while the installer was
drinking his whisky and pretending not to
notice the excitement of the Rileys. As he
set his empty glass down and moved away
towards the door, Gwennie glided forward
to retrieve the glass and clean it. “Good-
bye !” she sighed in farewell. Then she
picked up the glass, rinsed and wiped it and
glided back to her starting-point.
“Well, I’m damned !” said Riley. “A live
woman couldn’t have done it better.”
“Thank you, sir,” said the glowing in-
ventor. “Now would you like to have a
trial yourself. Or perhaps Mrs. Riley?”
No, Mrs. Riley wouldn’t. Mrs. Riley, as
a good religious woman, didn’t like it—’twas
too uncanny altogether. But her husband
gladly went through the ritual of entering
and asking for all sorts and combinations of
drinks, all of which were delivered correctly.
“I wish I’d had her twenty years ago!” he
cried in gTeat delight after an exhaustive
test.

“Well, I’m sorry you’ve got to have her
now!” snapped his wife and flounced out
of the bar.
Riley winked at the inventor. “Jealous!
D’ye see what ye’ve done now? Ye’ve broke
up me home, so ye have!”
Gwennie started work next morning with
a watchful Riley hovering in attendance in
case of accidents. But the automaton worked
beautifully, and Riley almost regretted that
lie had darkened the bar in accordance with
expert advice. At last he felt enough'confi-
dence in his new employee to go up town to
the bank. He got back just in time. A
half-drunk stranger was leaning on the coun-
ter waving an empty whisky-bottle at the
coldly-indifferent Gwennie and shouting:
“’Ere! wot’s this ye’ve give me?”
And every time he spoke Gwennie would
say, iu an infuriating way, “Love-ly da-ay!”
“What’s this—what’s this?” cried Riley,
bustling in, all nervousness and bluff.
“ ’Ere! Do you keep this pub?”
“Yes, me man, I do.”
“Well, you oughter sack that tart! She
give me this empty bottle, an’ ”
“All right, sir ! All right! We’ll give you
a full one. She’s a new girl, an’ doesn’t
know th’ run of th’ place yet.” (By this
time he was behind the bar tripping over
Gwennie’s tram-line and muttering warnings
to himself about seeing that all bottles were
left filled in future before going out.)
“Is she deaf, too. Mister?”
“No, she isn’t. Your w'hisky, sir.”
“Why didn’t she give me a proper answer
then? I asked ’er a civil question, an’ she
says: ‘Love-ly da-ay!’ What is she —a
blanky duchess? Look at ’er now —turnin’
up ’er nose at a bloke.”
“Ah-h, get along like a good felly an’ don’t
worry th’ girl!”
“I’m not worryin’ ’er! I’m on’y askin’ ’er
to be friendly !”
The rejected customer struck the counter
with his clenched fist. “Oh, all right—l can
get along without yer!” Then, in a burst of
scorn: “I can find a paddickful of better
tarts any day!”
Riley wiped the beads of perspiration
from his brow, and, the bar being clear,
tested Gwennie again. She worked per-
fectly ; but Riley shook his head doubtfully.
“I’m afraid there’s too many complications
in the business for you, me girl. He should
have fitted yeh with more conversation.” And
at that precise moment the swing-doors
opened and in strode the journalist who had
shocked ail the barmaids in Australia —even
the Grand Past Barmaids who have for-
gotten how to blush.
Beautiful mechanical Gwennie swam to
meet the customer, who at once swept off
his hat and called her sweetheart.
Gwennie merely stood waiting, wearing
her baffling Mona Lisa smile. The journalist
frowned slightly, leant on the counter and
stroked her rounded arm. “Darling!” he
said. “Do not misjudge me, dearest! I
have not come here to drink ! I have come
here to see and talk with you! Lean down
to the whisky, Melisande, and I will tell you
what the tallow merchant said to the typiste
on the last Thursday.” And then, stijl hold-
ing her arm firmly lest she should try to
escape, the great journalist poured into her
telephonic ear the very worst story he knew.
At least he poured part of it. He was
just working up to the climax when Gwennie
went mad with a whiz and a whir and shot
up to the end of the bar with the dazed
storyteller hanging on to her patent rubber-
and-steel arm. Reaching the terminus with
a crash, Gwennie immediately started back,
raced the’ full length of the bar, and was
half-way home before Riley could yell to the
journalist to let go. Gwennie’s other arm
was raking down the bottles from the shelves
as she went, and the steady roar of the little
wheels on which she travelled was punctu-
ated with glassy crashes and explosions. The
more trips she made the faster she went, and
presently Riley was too afraid to attempt to
catch her as she racketted to and fro, with
the limp journalist still clinging to her arm
like a monkey in a hurricane. When pre-
sently his grip slackened through exhaustion
he flew through the air like a storm-driven
leaf and landed under a settee with a crash.
Gwennie, relieved of his weight, flew back-
wards and forwards so fast that she only
seemed to be flickering; and presently she hit
the far end of the counter with a sickening
crash, fractured her metallic spine and fell
out of sight under the counter.
Riley has got a live barmaid in charge
now. But she isn’t tormented by the jour-
nalist. No barmaid ever is nowadays. The
journalist’s nerve is so shaken that he treats
all barmaids with deep respect, and has
never summoned up the courage necessary to
telpthe tallow-merchant story. Even Riley
can’t get it out of him.
KODAK.
Annunciation.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
BRENT pressed the red loam down
tightly with his boot, so that the
explosive should meet with proper
resistance, and then backed away from the
seat of action. It was the last big stump in
the orchard, and signified the end of his
work. There was other work ahead of him,
new and dangerous work, and now that he
had fully made up his mind to go he could
think of nothing else.
Below him stretched the clearing he had
hacked out of the scrub, a few acres planted
in young orange-trees, and to the left a big
paspalum paddock, where his small dairy-
herd found fat pasture. It had all been done
in four years, and he felt proud of his handi-
work. In the soft sunlight of early June the
light green of the young trees looked good to
him. and here and there a precocious seedling
showed golden fruit. When he came back—
and he meant to come back—they would all
be in full bearing.
A dull explosion shook the ground, and the
stump seemed to leap in the air and quiver,
the red loam breaking round its long, tena-
cious roots. Even the tap-root had been dis-
lodged.
“Good!” muttered Brent, examining his
work. “I thought three sticks of the stuff
would shift it. I’ll be able to finish it with
the jack.” „ ,
He sat down on a patch of dry grass and
felt for his pipe, though he did not want to
smoke. Up the track that led across a
corner of the paspalum paddock and through
the orange-trees his wife was coming with the
mid-day meal, salt-beef and sweet potatoes in
one billy and tea in the other. It was her
custom to bring it to him in the open when
he was very busy, and of late she had missed
no opportunity of getting out into the warm,
bright sunlight. .
Brent watched the slim figure in the floppy
sun-hat with the quiet intentness of a man
who is seeing something familiar from a new
angle. Although he had quite made up his
mind htf had not told her yet. He knew that
separation would mean more to her than to
him, for he would be coming in contact with
new experiences, while she would be merely
sitting still and waiting. Yet from some-
thing in her attitude he guessed that she had
divined what had been going on iii his mind
for the past few months.
“Better tell her now,” he thought. “It’s
about time.”
He did not anticipate that she would play
the baby. She had always taken everything
stoically in the two years since they were
married, for in spite of her apparent softness
there was a core of grit in her. And there
were no complications. They had no children,
and she could go to live with Molly, his
sister. As for the place, Connor was ready
to take it over on the half-share system.
“I got a scare,” she said, coming up and
putting the billies on the grass. “I forgot
you were using dynamite.”
“It’s the last big stump,” he said.
They sat down and began their meal.
Brent looked at her sideways. More than
ever he suspected that she guessed his news,
and he wished he had told her before. There
was something different about her that day,
a faint color in her cheeks, a light in her
eyes. She seemed almost shy and emotional.
There was silence for a few minutes as he
chewed his food and looked down over the
orange-trees to where a triangle of tin roof
shone in the sun.
“Well.” he said abruptly, “I’ve made up
my mind to go.”
She looked up.
“To go? Where, Harry?”
“Where all the other chaps have gone,” he
said, a little sharply.
It was a big decision after all; but he did
not want to be flamboyant about it. His
instinct was to take it as quietly as possible,
in order that she might do so too. She
seemed suddenly to grow very still, but there
was no particular surprise in her eyes.
“I only really made up my mind this morn-
ing, when I was settling that last stump,” he
said. “But there’s nothing that can’t be fixed
up easily. It isn’t as if we had any kids.”
“No,” she responded, flushing a little.
He looked at her secretly.
“I suppose it’s a bit of a knock-out for
you, Delia?” he said.
She was silent for awhile.
“It oughtn’t to be. I’ve half expected you
to make up your mind any time these lasl
few months.”
“You have?”
“Of course.”
He was glad she took it so sensibly, but
nevertheless the slight strain of vanity in
him was hurt. His mind was full of the
break it would make in their lives, for he
had never been away from her two days since
they were married. Yet she did not seem to
be greatly affected by the prospect. She was
merely still and preoccupied. Dor the first
time he felt that his mind was a mystery to
him. He could not guess what thoughts lay
behind her dark eyes.
“I’ve planned it all out,” he said, keeping
as closely as possible to practical arrange-
ments. “Connor would give his eyes for the
chance of taking over the place on the half-
share system, especially now that we’ve got
a dozen milkers going. And the fruit-trees
will be growing all the time. Lord knows
how long it will be before it’s all over, but
we’ll have something good to come back to,
anyway! And you’ll be more comfortable
at Molly’s than anywhere else. You two
always got along well together,”
They talked their plans over in detail, but
the woman’s mind seemed to have lost sharp-
ness and grip. Though she showed no sign
of emotion her eyes had an inward, absorbed
look. She glanced covertly at her husband,
and was not deceived by the evenness of his
voice. He was just thirty-two, bronzed and
hard-muscled, and he was going away to a
new, varied life that held danger and adven-
ture. The affectation of indifference was a
pretence on his part as much as on hers.
When he had finished his smoke she col-
lected the debris of the meal and stood up, a
crooked smile on her face.
“Well,” she said, “when have you fixed to
go, Harry?”
“In about a fortnight,” he replied. “I’ll
have everything square by then.”
“Make it three weeks,” she said. “We’ll
want one clear week to ourselves. We’ve
never had a holiday together, you know.”
“That’s true,” he assented.
It was all very matter-of-fact. Both
seemed afraid of the emotional possibilities
ot the situation, for they were not skilled in
words.
The woman went off down the hill, with
her mind numbed and every muscle taut. It
had all been very different from what she
had imagined as she had gone out through
the orange-trees, something singing in her
heart and her feet keeping time. This was
the day she had chosen to break the news to
ow '\ news - And he had anticipated
her by about ten seconds.
I i Wa l a f cr , et she ha( J ke Pt hidden for
weeks, though she had not felt sure of its
reality until that day. And she had looked
forward to the time when she would be able
to tell him. She felt that he would be stirred
re-awakened, and that for awhile the days of
their courting would come back. It was one
of the traditional privileges of womanhood
tion. et J °' V ° Ut ° f that moment of annuncia-
bHui, '“wait Si
not going to hold him back.” S 1
